THE RED PAGE.
Insomnia.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
For days I have not slept. For days and days
Of pain long-drawn and utter weariness
I have not had one single second’s grace,
Nor any moment’s lifting of the load
Of measureless anguish that weighs down on me.
And now to-night—indeed, I scarcely dare
To think it—now to-night it seems I may
Find sleep at last, and cease from consciousness.
I have not slept for centuries, I think;
I have forgotten what it is to sleep.
I only know that blessedness is there,
And peace ...
My brain swims in a lurid sea
Of sulky semi-consciousness. And now
I sink—l fall at meteoric speed
Through limitless space. For ages so I fall—
Ages and ages—all a listless blurr.
Substance has left me, and, attenuate,
I hurtle downwards through the ashen void
Swift as the glimmer of a new-lit star
To dark abysms beyond the reach of thought . . .
And next, I know that I can fall no more,
And consciousness returns in dreadful dreams. . . .
This is the gate of Hell, the innermost;
The gate whence issue all the fiends of hate
To goad the world to madness. Here enslimed
I lie amid shed foulnesses of souls
Cast out as base beyond Hell’s tolerance,
Unfit for clean damnation. All vile things—
The ooze of age on age of foetid crime
And treachery—besmear me on all sides.
To right, to left, behind, before, above,
Swart grinning devils mock me as I lie.
Slow-crawling things, all shapeless and all vague>
Peer from within, and, sidling forth, appal
My very blood with horror of their touch ;
An agony of fear takes hold of me.
Could I but die !—the sweetness of that thought,
The hopelessness, the dread ! There is no death !
No sound of footfall beats upon mine ear,
No blessed murmuring of articulate speech.
There is no light, save for a loathsome glow
Like phosphorus on dead flesh. The pulseless
air
Is unimaginably still, and seeped
In a most bitter silence. So remote
Is honest life beneath the open sky
That I detach myself inexplicably
From that pale clay called Me, and look upon it,
Contemptuous and half pitiful. It lies
All huddled in distortion of affright,
All scarred and seared where squalid needless sins
Have marked their passage in the wasted years.
My glance—so keen these new-found eyes of
mine !
My glance strikes through and through that
sordid clay
And weighs its worth remorselessly, until
The very devils seem to pity it.
I see great powers worn out by slow abuse
To utter impotence ; and every small
Faint latent possibility of good
Decoyed and dead, and etiolate and done—
Atrophy following on desuetude.
I see the heart—-a shrivelled, useless thing
Callous and cold, that once was eloquent,
The very fount and minister of joy,
Imperial with high hopes. I see the brain—
A mass of sorry lumber inchoate—
The brain that once was clean, well-ordered as
The coming of the morning. And I cry,
Facing the horror of this huddled thing,
This sentient carrion that is Me. “ Alas !
Could I but for a day have back Myself ! ”
While all Hell laughs at that insanity,
And fearful forms spring forth from out the
gloom
And crush me back to personality.
And so through an eternity of pain
And nausea that holds me back from death
I lie amid the ooze; while mis-shaped ghouls—
Hell’s scavengers—spit on me as they pass,
And souls, inborne to taste the cleansing of
The searching fires, turn from me with sick looks
Of utter loathing. And the meaner sins—
The grey and ghastly sins that will not burn —
Flung from the gate, fall on me, ’till it seems
That I (so weak and helpless and afraid !)
Bear all the sins of all the sinful world.
Oh, for a breath of air ! a single draught
Of fair, pellucid water ! Oh, to be
For just one moment on some wind-swept height
Above the sea, beneath the blessed sun !
I strive for utterance, but my lips are mute ;
To rise, but not a single nerve responds
To bid my muscles to their offices. . . .
And all the while the devils grin, and glare
With baleful eyes upon my misery.
And at that pass I wake. . . .
Awake ! dear God !
Awake ! The quiet stars peep in at me;
The wind of Night sweeps by and whispers low
The message of her solace. Presently
A distant bell proclaims the measured hour,
Puncturing the air with sweetness, and the peal
Shreds softly into filmy wisps of sound
That sigh themselves to silence.
But the dark,
Warm room oppresses me. I rise and light
The candles swiftly, and in their glow hug
A new strange sense of comfort exquisite.
I raise the blind sand fling the windows wide,
And draw the chill keen air into my lungs.
Who cares for sleep?—the East is purpling with
The promise of the morning and the day !
TASMANIA.
FRANK MORTON.
Imperialism and Democracy.
UNDER the mis-guidanceof Mr. Barton, the
Australian Commonwealth is at present
not only out-Englanding England in
English Imperialism, but is also setting
up a pinch-beck Imperialism of its own. The
acquisition of New Guinea, a step open to the
most serious question, has been quickly followed
by an interference in the New Hebrides, about the
mischief of which there can be no question at all.
There is a fictitious air of grand statesmanship
about such extra-territorial enterprise, the shoddy
idea of greatness which in private life leads many
- a man to subject his wife and family to miserable
penury at home so that he may “ cut p dash ” out-
side. So they are very tempting to a certain type
of mind. And, unhappily, they are of that class
of mischief which it isimmeasurablymoreeasyto do
than to undo. It is a simple matter “to take over”
New Guinea ; to get rid of it honestly and honor-
ably would be a task of quite different magnitude.
It is a simple thing—even a drunken man might
do it to decide that it is Australia’s duty to
actively interfere in the New Hebrides to coun-
teract French influence and to help to “redden”
the Islands. To withdraw from the enterprise
when our fingers begin to get uncomfortably hot
—that is quite another matter.
There is pressing need for a choice—a prompt
choice, seeing how difficult to be revoked is every
step forward to Imperialism—on the part of the
Australian people, between Imperialism and
Democracy ; Power and Liberty. Will the deci-
sion echo that of the goddess who typified wisdom
and declare
Yet not Power
—For Power of itself comes uncalled for—
But to act rightly, upholding the law we live by,
And because right is right to follow right
Were Wisdom— in scorn of consequence,
or will it declare for dominion rather than rightly-
acting democracy ? A decision must be come
to, and must be no vague ill-defined compro-
mise, but a resolute choice —Imperialism or
Democracy.
The two have never in the world’s history co-
existed ; in the nature of things are mutually
antagonistic. The pursuit of Power involves the
abandonment of the pursuit of Liberty, thougli
Power, in as much measure as it is needed fora
free people, “comes uncalled for”—pow.er to pre-
serve their freedom and to resist oppression.
The discussion of the value of a policy of Im-
perialism is generally obscured by the fallacy that
“ Empire ”is synonomous with “greatness and
a devotion to one’s own country arid one’s own
liberty without any desire to rob a neighbour of
his, with contemptible national aspirations. The
man who in England is prepared to shed the
blood of every nigger on earth to extend the
area of British possessions is a patriot; the man
who would put some check of conscience on inter-
national rapine, and who dares to suggest that
England has after all a very fair heritage at
present and might devote attention to improving
rather than extending her possessions; is a “ Little
Englander.” In Australia he is ail: absolute
traitor, and earns the contempt of every true
alderman. Yet, there could be no premise more
ridiculously and utterly wrong than that greatness
depends upon territory and population. In the
boundaries of ancient Greece, a country of about
the size of Tasmania, were settled at one time, the
Athenians, Spartans, Corinthians and half-a-dozen
other peoples, all of the one Hellenic stock, but all
absolutely independent of one another. No one
of these peoples at their prime probably counted
more than 1,000,000 souls. The land they inhabi-
ted was rocky and poorly blessed with rain. All
of the elements of greatness which the modern
Jingo regards as essential seemed to be denied
to them. Yet none can question the glory
of Athens, the greatness Of Sparta, the
magnificence of Corinth. Athens, in particular—
what great Empire, Persian, Egyptian, Baby-
lonian, Mohammedan, Spanish, English, has ever
rivalled the glory of that tiny little people,
dwelling on a rocky atom of the earth, in any one
of the elements of greatness—even in military
and naval prowess? In more modern days, Flor-
ence, the Netherlands, England in the days of
Elizabeth, prove that in no respect does greatness
depend on acres or national eminence on wide
dominion.
Even if a nation’s work is considered to be the
pursuit of military greatness, to the neglect of all
else, the object is sought but indifferently through
Imperialism. The pursuit and attainment of a
wide Empire brings a momentary flare of glory,
then the flame flickers, is puffed o,ut before the
sturdy breath of some little nation holding with
tenacious patriotism its poor acres, and there is
left but dust, the dust which carries down no
message to posterity, the dust which has
enveloped the once most mighty Spanish Empire,
and all the other great military Empires.
But if the argument is narrowed down to the
issue as between Imperialism- and a people’s free-
dom, happiness and true greatness—then the deci-
sion against Imperialism as a policy is open t/j abso-
lutely no question. There may be some hesitation
in accepting as a fact—what is nevertheless true—
that the small people who have attended to the
culture of their own liberties rather than to the
destruction of the liberties of others, have won
more “glory” than the great Empires : there
can be no shadow of a doubt that they have won
more happiness lor themselves, have contributed
more to the sum of that experience which makes
for the higher evolution of the human race. It
follows, as a matter of cause'and effect, that the
governing people of an extending empire cannot
worship at the shrine of Liberty. Empires are
extended over' the ruins of other people’s
rights, and oppression has always this
quality that it is as evil for the oppres-
sor as for the oppressed. The slave revenges
his injuries by degrading his master, and a people
held in bondage condemns its enslavers to a loss
of their liberties. A democracy, Napoleon said,
is a bad fighting government. He was right as
regards its fighting for the extension of Empire,
wrong if he intended to say that a democracy was
weak to-defend itself. Athens, where the purest
of democracy prevailed, overthrew the Persians at
Salamis and Marathon. Switzerland, a tiny
little Republic, has for centuries held its liberty
inviolate against the fierce assaults of most power-
ful nations. France herself, when Liberty had
degenerated into almost anarchy after the Revo-
lution, was yet able to hold all Europe at bay with
her armies. And how the Boers have fought, this
century, let English generals tell. A free people
can fight to protect its own liberties, but a free
people can never fight to destroy another people’s
liberties and remain free. It murders its own
freedom when it sends to the scaffold that of
another nation.
What, then, is to be the ideal of Australia?
The cultivation here of a free white race, jealous
of its own freedom, respecting that of others;
ready to defend its territory against an invader,
just as resolute not to invade another people’s
land ; seeking ever to perfect its system of govern-
ment and the condition of its people, anxious
never to impose upon unwilling others the burden
of its rule ? Or the fostering of au Imperial clan,
as lackey to another Empire, eager always for
foreign aggression and foreign rapine, sacrificing
Liberty so that it may be a better fighting force,
more obedient to discipline and more ruthless in
enterprise ? We are near to the parting of
the ways, one of which runs towards Imperialism,
the other towards Democracy. A dishonorable and
evanescent “glory” may be met with on the
former path (though certainly not at its end): a
real greatness can be the goal of the latter ; for
A g’-e/i.t city is that which has the greatest men and
women,
If it he a few ragged huts it is still the greatest city in
the whole world.
The place where a great city stands is not the place of
stretch’d wharves,' docks, manufactures, deposits of
produce merely,
Nor the place of ceaseless salutes of new-comers' or the
anchor-lifters of the departing,
Nor the place of the tallest or costliest buildings or shops
selling goods from the rest of the earth,
Nor the place of the best libraries and schools, nor the
place where money is plentiest,
Nor the place of the most numerous population.
Where the city stands with the brawniest breed of orators
and bards,
Where the city stands that is beloV’d by these, and loves
them in return and understands them,
Where no monuments exist to heroes but in the common
words and deeds,
Where thrift is in its place, and prudence is in its place,
Where the men and women think lightly of the laws,
Where the slaves ceases, and the master of slaves ceases,
Where the populace rise at once against the never-ending
audacity of elected persons,
Where fierce men and women pour forth, as the sea to
the whistle of death pours its sweeping and unript
waves,
Where outside authority enters always after the prece-
dence of inside authority, -
Where the citizen is always the head and ideal, and
President, Mayor, Governor, and what not, are agents
for pay,
Where'children are taught to be laws themselves, and to
depend on themselves,
Where equanimity is illustrated in affairs,
Where speculations on the soul are encouraged,
Where women walk in "■public processions in the streets
the same as the men,
Where they enter the public assembly and take places
the same as the men,
Where the city of the faithfullest friends stands,
Where the city of the cleanliness of the sexes stands,
Where the city of the healthiest fathers stands,
Where the city of the best-bodied mothers stands,
There the great city stands.
F.R.
England’s Intellect and England’s
War.
IN the cold, dry light of history the present
S’African war will be classed as the war of
the English plutocracy, of the German-.Tew
gang, the Yankee gang, and the home-
grown-millionaire gang, who control most London
newspapers, and the Chamberlain Cabinet. The
heart of England and the brain of England has
been against the war from the beginning even
unto to the end. Study the list of great English
“pro-Boers” (all who are not pure English
excluded—no green In any of these eyes)
John Buskin, philosopher, prose-poet, artist ;
the most charming sentimentalist of any age.
Herbert Spencer, philosopher, scientist ; the
most absolute and cold-blooded of political
economists.
George Meredith, the one supremely great
English novelist of the day, who this year has come
from his solitude to protest against the barbarity
of Ketchetierismjind Chamberlainism. He writes:
We are but steeling the remainder of their fighting men
to more desperate resistance—a spectacle forebodingly
piteous. It signifies also the further waste of our own
precious blood.
In dealing with the Boer, having the hope to conciliate
him, we must take his version of humaneness, or we shall
find that we have been guilty of bad policy, the most
exacting of a nation’s creditors.
Frederic Harrison, “the great Positivist,”
scientist, philosopher, man of letters.
John Morley, historian and statesman.
Goldwin Smith and James Bryce, the two lead-
ing representatives of English University life.
Not necessary to stretch out the list to include
Field-Marshal Chamberlain, Lord Coleridge, the
Bishops of Hereford and Worcester, and so on.
It is the foremost philosophers, writers, thinkers
of-England that are to be put in the witness-box
this time. Against those named, whom can Mr.
Chamberlain cite? If England to her own self—
herself as represented by her best thinkers—had
only been, true !

Arthur Symons, the personal, the intimate,
arrives in a new two-volume dress —Poems by
Arthur Symons. The five books from Days and
Nights to Images are here collected, with many
omissions and alterations. And a new book
is added —The Loom of Dreams —from which :
THE ONE DESIRE
If I think of your soul, I see
Your body’s be uity ; and then
I pray to your body again,
And your soul answers me.
So to possess you whole,
Two-fold ever the.same,
Come to me light or flame,
Come to me body or soul !
J*
Neatly put by a French-critic : “The French
novelist makes us desire to be in love ; the English
makes us desire to be married.”
Sub-Librarian Brazier puts out a booklet on the
“ Genius of Marcus Clarke.” He is the successor
of Marcus at the Public Library, and gives this as
his qualification partly, also being President of
the Australian Literature Society. Not having
known Clarke, Brazier undertakes an instructive
construction of his personality from his writings.
It may well lead a reader to despair of all his-
torical portraits, except by acquaintances. Clarke
rises ethereal, like Venus from the foam of the
ocean. There are about 40 men who knew Marcus
pretty well, and such of them as have noticed the
incense from this Brazier enjoy a good laugh. It
is, indeed, the kind of thing that is apt to produce
too much reaction. More than justice has been
done to Clarke already.

There are some confident girls in Maoriland, as
the issue of the Atom Quarterly shows. It is a
32-page mag. well-printed, just no\y very girly,
and proper, and prim, but holding out hopes of
one day being well-written, too. The art of
the Atom is ghastly. There is some writing
talent on the staff, but it expends itself mainly
in gush. The Atom, in fact, lacks pepper.

Some correspondents—
W. M. Whitney (in answer to “ G.H.,” 29/3/’O2) : I must
confess my ignorance ; I never heard of (<he poet Burgess.
Hence, any charge of plagiarism is clearly absurd. I

Some Cures Effected h Warner's Safe Cure.
The curative properties of Warner’s Safe Cure are -so well known that it is only necessary for
anyone to make inquiry for them to hear from the lips of those whom they know many instances of
the healing virtue of this remarkable medicine. ,
Warner’s Safe Cure acts scientifically. It exercises a specific action upon the liver and kidneys,
restoring them to health and activity. When the kidneys and liver are properly performing their
appointed task of extracting the urinary and biliary poisons, all diseases arising from the presence of
these poisons in the system are overcome, and the cure effected is radical because it is natural.
The common diseases caused by the presence of urinary and biliary poisons in the system are :
Rheumatism Indigestion
Gout Dyspepsia
Neuralgia Biliousness
Lumbago Sick Headache
Backache General Debility
Sciatica Gravel and Stone
Blood Disorders Bladder Diseases
Anaemia Bright’s Disease
For the reason stated, Warner’s Safe Cure has complete mastery over all these diseases. The
following typical letters are worth reading.
Kidney Disease Cured.
Post Office Hotel, Maryborough, Q.,
Nov. 3, 1900.
Hear Sirs,—l have much pleasure indeed in forwarding
the following testimonial as to the marvellous curative
properties of Warner’s Safe Cure, and you are at liberty
to use it in any way you think fit. ,
I may state briefly that I was a sufferer from chronic
Kidney Disease, which made my life perfectly unbear-
able- It is needless to say that I tried the best available
doctors both in England and Australia, with but one
result—absolute failure. I also tried different proprietary
medicines, but with no beneficial results. During this
time you can well understand the pain and misery I
endured—to say nothing of the expense. I was staying
with a professional friend, and related my trouble to him.
He suggested Warner’s Safe Cure. I ridiculed the idea,
telling him I was heartily sick of both doctors and medi-
cine The symptoms gradually became worse, the pain
more intense, and, in a fit of desperation, I despatched
my-assistant for a bottle of your invaluable remedy,
although expecting no better results from it than from
other medicines I had used. Thank God for that bottle.
It was but the forerunner of my complete cure. I used
half a dozen bottles, and my cure was perfect as
well as marvellous. I have recommended the medicine to
a number of friends, and they are not slow to endorse
what I have written. Hoping, sirs, that your remedy
will be extensively used in similar cases to my own, and
wishing you every success and prosperity, I remain,
Yours sincerely,
L. 0. Snowdon,
Surgeon Dentist.

Low, Languid, and Dispirited.
The Vagaries of a Torpid Liver.
A Useful Lesson for Sufferer.
The Case of tyr. J. DEGNAN*
(FOR THE “ REDFERN TIMES.”)
Among the most important missions of
inquiry placed on record was that recently
made by our reporter to the home of Mr.
John Degnan, Garden-street, North Botany.
This is due as much to the magnitude of his
discoveries as to the interest of the incident
he elicited on inquiring.
“ Will you favour me with a complete
outline of the vicissitudes you successfully
combated ? ”
“ Right willingly,” answered Mr. Deg-
nan, as he offered the pressman a seat. Five
years went by from the time my illness
started till it was finally conquered, and if
I did not have a bad time it was a caution.
Bear in mind, it was all through the torpid
state of my liver, and, believe me, I got
into such a very wretched condition that I
did not care whether I lived or not. Before
I was really aware what disaster was be-
falling nel used to feel as though I had no
life in me, and was always low-spirited and
sleepy. There was only one thing that I
was lit for, and that was to lie down, but
when I did so I could not sleep, my mind
being actively engaged in going through a
multitude of unhappy thoughts which
seemed to be born of the melancholy condi-
tion I was in. What caused me considerable
uneasiness of mind was that my appetite
became poorer every day, and still more
anxiety was engendered by the knowledge
that what food I did eat was of no benefit
to me, my ability to digest any kind of
eatables having vanished. In this way it
transpired that my strength wasted away
till it was often compulsory for me to knock
off work, although my employment at that
time was not of a heavy description.”
“ How did you feel after your meals? ”
“Just as if I had swallowed so much
lead. Something like a heavy ball seemed
to foim in my stomach, the pains arising
from which were simply horrible, and from
the stomach the heavy sensations travelled
up to my chest, thereby giving me extra-
ordinary difficulty in breathing. My head
ached to a degree which proved almost dis-
tracting, for I could not settle down to
read or do anything, and there was even a
swollen feeling in my eyes to annoy me.
Owing to a pricking sensation under my
shoulder-blades I used to think I had
caught a cold, but experience has taught
me that that was a deranged liver symptom,
while another one, although more torment-
ing in its persistency, was a nasty aching
pain across the loins, which I felt very dis-
tinctly whenfT moving about. The bitter
taste in my mouth was enough to turn any
man off his food, and the dirty coating on
my tongue used to make me feel sick. At
times, when exhausted nature claimed a
few hours’ rest, I fell asleep, but there was
no comfort in doing so, as the ugly diearns
I had simply beggar description. The flesh
gradually fell away from my frame till my
bones were quite visible, and my prospects
were anything but rosy, especially as my
head had long since been troublesome.
Without any ostensible reason it used to
start beating violently against my ribs, the
sound it made being quite loud enough to
lo heard several yards away. It seemed
more than likely that my heart was diseased,
particularly so as those fits of palpitation
were usually accompanied by feelings of
faintness. The woi ry of mind and pain of
body at last succeeded in wearing out my
patience, for it took but little to make me
irritable and excited, after which I sank
into the old gloomy groove of misery till
something else occurred to upset me. Ner-
vousness was not the least of my ailments,
as my nerves twitched involuntarily when-
ever anything happened to disturb me, and
in spite of the medicines I had taken, they
; till trembled at the least provocation.”
“ Where did you eet your medicine from,
Mr. Degnan ?”
“ I refer to the medicines which my
doctors prescribed for me, -but which I
might as well have left untouched for all the
good they were. I was always paying
money away to doctors, and never got any
value back for it; but now if I should
have the ill-luck to fall into bad health
again I would go straight for Clements
Tonic, as I know it to be the best medicine
ever brought within the reach of suffering
humanity.”
“ That being your opinion, why did you
not take some earlier than you did ? ”
“ Recause I had been suffering five years
before I was told about Clements Tonic,
and another reason vas because I had de-
cided against taking any more medicine, as
I thought it was useless. But a relative
came upon the scene and told me how
Clements Tonic had cured his complaints,
which were like mine in nearly eveiy detail;
so I promised to try it, and w hen I did so
it was the best day’s work I ever did, for
Clements Tonic piloted me to health. I
used to dread the very idea of taking any
food, but when I had been on Clements
Tonic a few days it was quite different, ; s I
was always the first to sit down to meals
and the latt one to finish eating, and befo. e
a week was over there was a great reduc-
tion in the severity of my pains in the
stomach. My food did not feiment and
turn to wind as in former years, but it
digested quite readily, and of course I got
the benefit of its nourishment.. The weary
feelings which had darkened my days were
wafted away by the invigorating qualities
of Clements Tonic, which likewise buckled
together my shattered nerves and na le
them firm and true. The greatest s rprise
I ever got in my life was when I discovered
what a splendid medicine Clements Tonic
was for promoting healthful sleep, and I
say ‘ healthful ’ advisedly, for there was no
languidness or depression on awaking, such
as is experienced after taking sleeping
draughts or other such means for producing
slumber. The anxious misgivings which I
had borne in silence when my heart was
given to palpitating were not long in being
dispelled, for Clements Tonic made that
organ beat with the regularity of a clock,
and I can make the same statement in
regard to my inteinal functions generally,
for I never suffered from constipation after
taking a few bottles of Clements Tonic,
which relieved my chest pains and head-
aches very easily. It was pleasant to get
up and notice that my tongue was clean and
that the bitter taste had left my mouth,
but what was still more 1o be appreciated
was the fact that every ache and pain had
deserted my body, which was regaining its
weight from day to day.”
“ Are you sure about the completeness of
your cure? ”
“I am quite positive that Clements Tonic
made a new man of me by giving me better
health and more strength than I ever had
before, Not long ago I was helping to cut a
road through the mountains, about ten milts
from Nowra, and I found that I could do
the heaviest manual labour going without
being unduly fatigued, and, as Clements
Tonic gave me the health to do it, you caii
publish my story in any way you want.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
1, John Degnan, of Garden-street. North Botany,
in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth
of Australia, do soltmnly and sincerely declaie
that I have carefully read the annexed document,
consisting of five folios, and consecutively numbered
from one to five, and that it contains and is a
true and faitl fid account of my illness and cure
by Clements Tonic ; and a'so contains my full per-
mission to publish in any way my statements —
which I give voluntarily, without receiving any
payment; and I make this solemn declaration cu-
scientiously believing the same to be true, and by
virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act, 19C0.
Subscribed and declared at Darlinghursk this twenty-
eighth day of February, one thousand nine hundred
and two, before me,
THOMAS MOORE. J.P.

Cough=Curing
“The Cough was eased almost
immediately-is now completely-
cured ”
Coughs are as common in this sort of
weather as roses in June; all kinds of
coughs from all kinds of reasons, yet all tfie
coughs and all the reasons give way to one
“ 109, Nursery Road, Lozells,
“Birmingham, England,
“ April 9th, 1 901.
“Dear Sirs,— My child suffered from
measles, which left behind a most distressing
couft- I tried several remedies for him,
including an emulsion made by another firm,
but it had not the least effect and did not
relieve the cough one atom. I was then
recommended to try Scott’s Emulsion and
to this end procured a bottle, and from that
time my child commenced to steadily im-
prove. The cough was eased almost imme-
diately, appetite greatly improved, and
vitality and strength increased. I beg to
say my child is now completely cured,
and to Scott’s Emulsion only can I in
X
n-
HARRY SHARRATT.
(From a photograph.)
justice give the credit of this satisfactory
result. I consider Scott’s Emulsion im-
measurably superior to the other Emulsion
I tried, and its pleasant taste and palata-
bility is a great point in its favour, the child
taking it readily from the first, but it was
only with the greatest difficulty he would
take the other preparation. To show you
how noticeable the improvement must be in
my boy’s condition, I may state our friends
frequently remark upon the change in him
and say how healthy he now looks. —Yours
truly, (Signed) E. Shabratt.” .
“To Scott’s Emulsion only can I in
justice give the credit for this satisfactory
result.” The result Mr. Sharratt alludes to
was the cure of “ a most distressing cough.”
For cough-curing is easy if you know just
what to do. All the coughs of these bitter
winter days will yield to Scott’s Emulsion,
and we have no hesitation in promising
you prompt, almost immediate relief,
with ultimate cure. Is it not the part of
wisdom and comfort to cure the cough you
have?
We are bound to point out to you, not
more in our interest than in your own, that
while curing coughs is easy enough if you
take Scott’s Emulsion, it by no means
follows that you will cure your cough if you
accept anything said to be “ just as good.”
There never can be an imitation just as
good as the original. As for “ substitutes,”
no substitute for the good done by cod-liver
oil has yet been found.
Then, too, after cod-liver oil has refreshed
and reinforced the blood, two other ingre-
dients—blended in Scott’s Emulsion —come

Various Verses.
t Coolgardie.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.)
For gold he went —God made men so,
All restlessness and striving,
While women sit at home and waib,
And find the whole earth desolate
That erst was gay and thriving.
Oh, my poor boy ! I watched him go
Till in the dimmest distance
He turned and waved his hand to me ;
That winding road my eyes still see
With weariful persistence.
His letters came, and were they gay,
How sore my heart, and jealous !
And were they sad —my tears would flow;
He needed me, who might not go
With comfort swift and zealous.
Amidst the feverous heat and drouth
He worked, and I at leisure ;
Whene’er I felt the south wind blow
My longing thoughts to him would go
Stifling, in search of treasure.
I can no longer weary him.
With eager love’s beseeching.
He lieth still, he will not come
Adown that winding roadway home,
Nor listen to love’s teaching.
VIC.
M. A LSTON.
Forgive, Forget.
The first kiss after lonely years.
The first kiss and the last —
Like a grey wall the future rose,
Black storm-clouds veiled the past;
He turned to me his searchless eyes,
His lips with ebbing breath —
Was it worth the pain of those severed years—
To kiss at the gates of Death ?
Forgive, forget—Love lingers yet—
Condone the human fault;
Forgiveness is but silence drear
In the darkness of the vault.
Each one of us is frail enoucrh,
Heed what the old love saith,
Lest you kneel to kiss in your agony
By the very gates of Death.
JOHN CAREW.
The Thunderstorm.
Her garden sobbed beneath the rushing cloud,
Wild wings fell fluttering in tumultuous bowers,
And through the river gloom of blinding showers,
Swayed the sweet petals of her lilies proud.
And the tall poplar by her window bowed,
Before the passion of the unknown powers
That wrapped her being through those, tranced
hours —
What wonder that her gentle heart was cowed !
Yet, when a wondrous peace suffused again,
With tender light her glistening Paradise,
And song and perfume thrilled to limpid skies.
From bird and flower whose joy was almost pain,
Not theirs the tremulous glory in her eyes
That dreamed of lightning flash and blinding rain.
D. O’R.
When Australia Wakes.
When Australia smiles,
When the wizard sunset’s wiles
Work a sweet transfiguration
Over forest, plain, and station.
And the mountain’s dim defiles,
Is there any fairer nation,
When Australia smiles?
When Australia sleeps,
When the moonlight softly steeps
All the city’s ugliness
In her glamour-sweet caresses,
How the glory creeps
O’er the gum-spread wildernesses,
When Australia sleeps !
And what shall be the glory
When Australia .wakes ?
Outdone the Old World’s story
When the new morn breaks !
With eyes worn red with weeping,
We watch the white dawn peeping;
Ah ! who would then be sleeping
When Australia wakes ?
P. F. R.
Surely the world moves when a religious
paper (Australian Churchman) publishes this—
As cleansing the city and suburbs and general sanitary
measures are the order of the day, I wonder if it has ever
been brought to the notice of the many preachers in our
churches, chapels, and meeting-houses of the intolerable
suffocating atmosphere that their congregations have to
endure.
Christianity —perhaps because it was started in
opposition to Judaism, one of the cleanest
religions of all time, and partly, perhaps,
because the wealthy pagans of Rome washed
regularly—became deeply tainted with dirt at the
ou t Set with traditions of saints whose sanctity
mainly depended on not washing, and whose
charity led them to carefully rescue the vermin
which fell from them. Now there is a happy
tendency to get back to Moses and clean, venti-
lated temples.

THE ANCHOR FENCE.
THE ANCHOR FENCE has been pronounced by the leading fence experts as the strongest
fence in the world. It is made with plain or barbed wire, and cannot injure of live stock
nor can the live stock injure it or displace any of the wires, owing to the use of the Anchor Clamp.
Bv means of the Anchor Clamp Pinchers, the Anchor Clamp securely fastens the horizontal and
cross wires, thus consolidating the strength, so that the who<e -fence resists an attack on any
part of it. As the strength of a chain is guaged by the strength of its weakest link, so a fenceis
only as strong as its weakest part—the Anchor Fence is equally strong throughout, and being
constructed wholly of large wires is much stronger than a fence in which small wires are used.
A MARVEL OF STRENGTH AND SIMPLICITY.
“ In replv to vour enquiry, we have just completed the erection of two miles of
Fencing with the Anchor Fence Materials, and have no hesitation in saying that for efficiency,
economy, and promise of durability, it is by far the best fence we have had expenence of, and this
opinion is practically emphasised by our recent order for a further 10 miles. Yours faithfully,
JOHN Q. & P. WOOD, Brundah, via Oowra, March 20th, 1902. _

CAUTIONARY SIGNALS.
The annual disturbance known as “Hospital Saturday” will strike Australia this week. After this warning if men allow themselves to be “caught out”
in it The Bulletin will not be responsible.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,

postage on Inter provincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
€ l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

O wing to the imposition ofl\d. postage, the subscription
to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.

postage—ild. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.

SATURDAY. MAY 3. 1902.
The Alleged Reform Movement.
The political reform movement, which com-
menced in South Australia, broke out again
with extra violence in Victoria, and spread
thence to Tasmania, and which is being
ardently boomed by the Tory press in N.S.
Wales, has a good many of the ear-marks of
a glaring Tory fraud and conspiracy. It is
in Victoria that it has taken most definite
shape, and there it has developed, to a great
extent, into a miserable howl on the part of
a very lightly-taxed community, and es-
pecially the very lightly-taxed property-
holding section of the community, against
paying the necessary price of civilisation; into
an elaborate series of misrepresentations to
bolster up the legend that taxation is unduly
heavy and needs to be reduced ; an organised
attack on the only decent and progressive
branch of the Legislature* disguised as an
attempt to promote public economy by
saving its salary; and a vigorous effort
to bolster up the stagnant and in-
jurious Legislative Council because it is
cheap, so far as drawing no direct remu-
neration is concerned —a characteristic
which it shares with bushrangers, lunatics,
dead bodies, lepers, vagrants, and other
undesirable characters. The programme
outlined by the reform party, at its great
Melbourne conference, has undoubtedly some
good points, but the party doesn’t go far
enough on its good points, and the bad ones
are the most conspicuous. It proposes that
there should be greater economy in State
Government, which is beneficial but exceed-
ingly vague, for it doesn’t show where a
single economy of any size is to be effected.
It demands that money shall only be bor-
rowed for reproductive works, which idea is
an improvement on present practices, but it
doesn’t go nearly far enough ; for there should
be no borrowing at all, either for productive
or unproductive works. And if the proposals
of the conference are adopted, there remains
the fact that an immense number of unpro-
ductive works are absolutely essential in
every civilised community, and when the
conference’s proposal is adopted these must
either be abandoned, which means a relapse
into semi-barbarism, or executed out of
revenue, and the reform party shows
not the least inclination to supply the
necessary revenue. Also, the confer-
ence demands that the miserably-paid
Ministers, who receive about the salary
of a very small bank manager without his
fixity of tenure, shall have their pay reduced ;
and that the ill-paid M.’sL.A., who are
expected to supply the wisdom of Solon for
the wage of a bank-clerk, and pay heavy
election expenses out of this, and sacrifice
probably alife’sprospects for a precarious poli-
tical billet which is often of only three years’
or less duration, shall have their remunera-
tion further cut down. Also, it is proposed
to cut down the dimensions of the Assem-
bly, which proposal is reasonable enough
within certain limits. And it is proposed to
preserve the Legislative Council, whose
hatred of every progressive idea is the
principal reason why the Assembly has very
little useful work to show for its money
—which is the most insane throwing away of
a great opportunity that any “reform”
party was ever guilty of.
Undoubtedly, it is possible to run the
affairs of Victoria, and of the other States,
more economically than at present —the
experience of 1896 and adjacent years proves
that. But it isn’t desirable to return to the
grievous sweating of these hard times merely
to save a gang of selfish Fat men and un-
principled landlords a little taxation. It
would be worth doing it if the loan curse
could thereby be abolished, but that is quite a
different thing. Victoria is the most cheaply-
governed State in the Commonwealth, bar
Tasmania, and both of them are governed a
good deal more cheaply than the United
Kingdom, even in time of peace, though the
average fool person who looks only at exter-
nals never sees this. The regulation attitude
is that of the Tory person who moved the
resolution about “alarming extravagance”
at the Melbourne reform conference, and
who wildly asked his audience to—
Consider that this handful of people were spending
about £7,500,000 a year.
The Victorian expenditure, it is true, as
shown in the public accounts, pans out at
£6 13s. 4d. per head per annum, and when
this is compared with the United Kingdom’s
total expenditure, as shown in a very
differently-arranged system of public ac-
counts, of less than £3 per head per annum,
it looks dreadful enough.
* * • »
But, after all, the difference is in
Victoria’s favor. Its State outlay includes
the cost of running the railways, and
the Britisher pays that also, only with
the difference that he pays it to private
companies, and it doesn’t appear in his
national balance-sheet. But the fact that it
doesn’t appear in that public fashion makes
no alteration in the fact that he does pay it.
Also the cost of maintaining police, charities,
and public education falls on the State
revenue in Victoria—in the United Kingdom
these items are mostly charged to local taxa-
tion, and again they don't appear in the
national balance-sheet, but the citizen pays
them all the same. The relative situations
as regards the amounts actually paid by the
people are about as follows :
Tasmania (year 1900).
Paid in taxes, postal and other revenue .. £680,206
Paid to State railway manager in fares and
freights .. .. .. .. .. 192,561
Paid in local taxation, about.. .. ~ 147,012
£1,019,779
Equal to £5 19s. lid. per head, or if land
revenue (£71,203) is included, the total is
£1,090,982, or £6 Bs. 4d. per head.
Victoria (year to 30th June, 1900).
Paid in taxes, postal and other revenue .. £4,064,614
Paid to State railway managers in fares and
freights .. .. .. .. .. 3,010,795
Paid in local taxation, about.. .. .. 1,185,307
I
£8,260,716
Equal to £6 17s. Bd. per head, or if land
revenue (£375,267) is included, the total is
£8,635,983, or £7 3s. lOd. per head.
United Kingdom (year ended 31st March, 1900—before
the war-taxes were imposed).
Paid in national taxes, postal and other re-
venue .. .. .. .. .. £119,839,905
Paid in local rates, collected by national
Government .. .. .. .. 10,000,381
Paid local taxes direct to local authorities,
about .. .. .. .. .. 60,853,848
Paid to private railway managers in fares and
freights.. .. .. .. .. 98,854,552
£289,548,686
Equal to £7 4s. 9d. per head. And the
Victorian and Tasmanian have, in proportion
to population, five times the railway facilities
that the Britisher enjoys. In Tasmania
there is one mile of railway for every 333
people ; in Victoria, one for 361; in the
United Kingdom, one for 1759 people.
* * * *
In each case water rates have been ex-
cluded. If the amount which London pays
to its private water companies could be
readily ascertained, and water rates were
included all round, it might have made a
slight difference, but the variation is not
likely to be of great importance. The main
fact is that, taking the figures of the last
year, before the war taxes upset all calcula-
tions, the Tasmanian and Victorian were
paying a good deal less than the inhabitant of
the United Kingdom, and grumbling a great
deal more, and getting five times the railway
accommodation. Yet the U.K.*is supposed
to be a not very badly-managed community
on the whole. Certainly Victoria, as far the
poorer country by nature, has no right to
spend as much as the United Kingdom does
—still the ordinary individual who includes
Victoria’s railway receipts as taxation, and
doesn’t include the United Kingdom’s rail-
way receipts as taxation, and who, there-
fore, figures out Victoria as being governed
at nearly £7 per head, as compared with
England’s £3, or less, is just a plain fool,
and a hopeless one at that. Victoria’s paid
Assembly has done better than England’s un-
paid House of Commons, insofar that it runs
the country at a cheaper rate. It has done
worse, insofar that it should have the courage
to tax Victoria up to the British level, which
would mean adding about another £500,000
a year to the public burdens; to save another
£500,000 a year by hard economy, or, if that
is not possible, it should tax above the British
level; and it should construct the State’s rail-
waysand otherpublic works with the proceeds,
thereby making an end of the loan curse for
ever. In other words, the worst foolishness
of the paid Assembly of Victoria is that it
doesn’t tax the sort of people who constitute
the reform movement more heavily for a good
purpose, and its best excuse is that the selfish
and unpatriotic Legislative Council, which
the reform party is so anxious to pre-
serve, wouldn’t allow it to do so if it
tried. If the principal weight of the chari-
table, police and education vote were laid on
the local bodies as in Britain, and the local
taxation on property went up in consequence,
to something like ss. in the £ rental as in
Britain, undoubtedly it would be possible to
obliterate the borrowing curse, and put Vic-
toria’s affairs on a better footing. And if the
Assembly received a mandate from the
people to abolish borrowing, and to make
the public finances clean and respectable,
and the House of 48 Rich Landlords, whose
blatant refusal to tax its own class up to
anything like the British level blocks all
reform, were abolished, its record is good
enough to justify the hope that it might do
the work.
* * * •
Of course, there is no reason why, in
addition to the abolition of the House of
Landlords, the Victorian Assembly should
not be reduced to, say, 60 members. But if
it is reduced, the salaries of Ministers and
individual members should be handsomely
increased. Britain may have a large
leisured class, whose members can give
their services gratis, and it may be
satisfied with the kind of services it
receives from them, but Australia is differ-
ently situated. The kind of politician that it
can get gratis is exemplified in the dull idiocy
of its unpaid Upper Houses. If the country
wants to economise, it should start with
unpaid bank-managers, gratis bosses of in-
surance offices, and honorary Governors,
Chief Justices, and railway commissioners.
If it can’t get men to efficiently fill small
billets like these for nothing, it shouldn’t
expect to get men to do efficiently the much
larger work of managing the whole country
for nothing. The cheapest and most igno-
rant sneer is that Avhich is directed at the
politician because he doesn’t run the country
to absolute perfection for about the wage a
man gets for running a locomotive, and the
proposal to make him more efficient by pay-
ing him less is an equally brilliant inspira-
tion. On this subject here is some of the
reform party’s language :
Mr. E. 0. Warde, M.L.A., said during the last year
Ministers’ salaries amounted to £94oo—(Too much) —
members’ salaries, £25,800—(T00 much ; and laughter)-
the Council and officers, £6275—(G00d boys ; and groans)
—and Assembly officers, £9l44—(groans.) “ Hansard,”
instead of costing £15,000, as stated by the press, cost
only £6670. (Still too much ; and disorder.) The State
reading room had cost £1233 ; refreshment rooms—(ah ;
derisive cheers, and smacking of lips)—£1000, and alto-
gether the total expenditure on Parliament was £66,527.
(Say it all,again ; and derisive cheers.)
It appears from this that it costs a good many
thousands less to run the whole Parliament
of Victoria, even with all its needless frills
and officers, than it costs to run the purely-
local Bank of Victoria. Truly, it looks as if
the State should look for the gratis bank-
manager first and the gratis politician after-
wards.
• • * •
Along with some good features, the alleged
reform movement has, therefore, these in-
curably bad ones : It embodies a persistent
jeer and scoff and curse at the only House
of Stale Legislature which represents
the people and over which the people have
any real control. It jibes at the £3OO a year
M.L.A. because, in some few respects, he
has proved as incompetent as the £2OOO and
£3OOO a year bank managers who made the
appalling smash of ’93. It complains that he
doesn’t manage the country so well as he
might, when almost everything he attempts
is nullified or mutilated by an irresponsible
Upper House, which the “ reform ” party
seeks to preserve. Its policy is, by implica-
tion and indirectly, a glorification of the
Upper House, wherein are gathered so
many of the blindly-incompetent bank and
financial wreckers who nearly made Victoria
insolvent a few years ago, and who fought
against the measures by which George
Turner averted the downfall. Its accusa-
tions about the inordinate cost of running
the country under the Assembly’s manage-
ment are, to a great extent, just plain lies.
Its implication that the Assembly stands in
the way of reform is mostly untrue. The
Victorian Assembly at least passed, by a
small majority, the second reading of the
Bill for the creation of a popular Convention
to draft a new cheap reformed Victorian
constitution, and the Bill was dropped
because the smallness of the majority
encouraged the M’s.L.C. to let it be known
very clearly that they would have none
of it—also because Peacock lacked the
courage of his convictions. If the
reform party devoted itself to getting
the House of Rich Landlords out of the
way, and then gave a mandate to a reduced
(and better paid) Assembly that it required
better financial methods, it would probably
get them, for there would no longer be a
capacious waste-paper basket in the Land-
lords’ Chamber to serve as a grave for good
ideas and projects of reform. Until that
body is shifted there is no possibility of
serious reform, and there is nothing in the
alleged reform party’s programme about
even amending it—let alone shifting it.
* * * *
The alleged “reform” party demands
mainly reduced taxation, which must almost
inevitably mean more borrowing, for though:
Victoria is not run on quite the intensely
economical principles of 1896, it is run—
apart from its ridiculous war expenses—
as cheaply as any civilised country can
be without being a sweater of a very
bad order. Certainly contingents and old-
age pensions have increased the expenditure
since June, 1900, but, even with its old-age
pensions, it is run at a less annual cost per
head than Britain is without old-age pensions.
A lot more taxation—the proceeds to be set
apart exclusively for public works purposes,,
the abolition of borrowing, the abolition of its.
Upper House, the reduction of the salaries
of the Governor and other purely ornamental*
individuals, a smaller and better-paid Minis-
try and Assembly, and the hanging of a few
bogus reformers whose whining about their
very light burdens is a disgrace to the man-
hood of the community, are Victoria’s real!
requirements just now.
Britain’s Poland.
The British Government falls back again* on tlie-
old, stupid, brutal coercion process in* Breland,
and the Crimes Act has been proclaimed! in nine
counties. There is very little crime in. Ireland at
any time—it is, in most years, the most law-abiding
section of the United Kingdom, and, just now the
Chief Secretary admits that there are fewer violent
offences against person or property than at any
previous time on record. Nevertheless, Ireland
is under all manner of extraordinary laws and
repressions; the right of public meeting is almost
wholly suspended ; the inestimable privilege of
free speech is “off”; and all manner of violent
means are being taken to make Ireland love Eng- •
land very much. The Irishman, according to
English ideas, commits three sins that are beyond i
all pardon, and a fourth that is nine times more -
horrible than the other three put together. He ob-
jects to the aggregation of huge estates in the hands ■
of greedy, rack-renting landlords, many of them i
English absentees, in which respect he is just like ■
the democrats of Australia, and wants to follow the ■
policy adopted in S. A. and Maoriland, and strongly ,
approved in other States. He wants Parliamentary
Government of his own in purely local matters,.
and he agitates for it just as all the Australias did I
till they secured it. And he wants, just as Maori-
land, Victoria, S.A. and Canada do, to build I
up his local industries by Protection. These are ■
very small and ordinary demands. Even this;
loyal and Jingo land of Australia would probably
have expressed its feelings by hooting the Duke of
York and throwing road-metal at the Governor if.
they were denied to it. And finally, as the mGstt
hideous crime of all, he is suspected of not having;
the profound affection he should have for the
nation which has made his country the ruin it is.
* • • *
Ireland’s old Parliament voted its own aboli-
tion, not by the votes of the people’s representa-
tives, for in the Irish House of Commons less than
100 out of 300 members were really elected. The
Government, or the peers who had the right of
nomination, simply appointed enough alleged
Irish members, many of them English military
officers, to carry the Union. Ireland was then
more thickly-peopled than England and had
about two-thirds of England’s population, but as
it was allowed less than half its fair share
of representation in the British House of
Commons, it had no voice worth mentioning
in the joint councils of the United Kingdom.
Many of its industries had been ruined by war or
directly abolished and prohibited by the English
Government in the interests of English manufac-
turers. So when the Ereetrade frenzy struck the
country, Ireland’s new manufacturing industries
were too young and feeble to stand the shock of
foreign competition, and died out to a great
extent, while the older and more deeply-rooted
ones of England kept up reasonably well. Eng-
land, with about twice its fair share of Parliamen-
tary representation, decided the new fiscal policy
mostly “on its own,” and wholly in what it:
regarded as its own interests. By confiscation
and other processes the soil of Ireland had mostly
passed into the hands of huge proprietors, a large -
proportion of them absentees. The rents of both
Ireland and England were spent in England, and.
England prospered—lreland didn’t. Ireland,,
with its manufactures blighted by the Ereetrade;
policy, which struck them when they were too;
young to stand the shock, and with hardly any
minerals, became, of necessity, mainly an agri-
cultural country, and even England has found
that its agriculture can’t stand the Ereetrade
policy. And the Anglo-Irish landlord proved a
merciless rack-renter. When the English agri-
cultural landlord became too extortionate his
tenantry left the farms, drifted to the towns and
cities, and became artisans or miners—therefore
there was a close limit to rack-renting in England.
The Irish farmer had no mines and hardly any
city industries to go to, and if he couldn’t raise the
money to leave the country he stayed on his farm
and starved miserably for the benefit of the land-
lord, and his helplessness was the landlord’s oppor-
tunity. His poverty made him ignorant—poverty
and ignorance go together. His farming was bad
because he was too poor to buy implements or
education. His country became the only one in
Europe which was falling into a state of utter
depopulation.
* * * *
And England, in a semi-repentant mood, will
do anything for Ireland except let it build up
its industries again in the way that England
built up its own industries ; or legislate seriously
against the big landholders—the peers and
squires to whom the English mind grovels
so profoundly ; or allow Ireland to manage
the local affairs which England has shown
itself so utterly incapable of managing that it has


only succeeded in wiping out half the population.
A little has certainly been done through the
Government purchasing large estates and cutting
them up for re-sale, but it is very little, and when
the Irishman finds that the Government _ can
afford to buy land at a fair price, and re-sell it at
13s. 4d. per acre per annum—this covering interest
and a portion of the purchase money—he only
realises the more what an iniquity is the average
rental of sometimes double that amount. And
his anger at the Government which can afford him
this relief without loss, and won’t do it on any
large scale because it doesn’t want to compulsorily
buy out the sacred duke and the holy squire, is
very natural.
* * » •
And when the Englishman found that the Irish-
man didn’t love the rack-renting landholder or the
Government which supported him, and that he
was agitating against them, he was shocked
at the other fellow’s iniquity. So the
right of public meeting is, to a large ex-
tent, suspended again to make the Irish-
man feel more affectionate, and extra repres-
sive laws are put in force to promote good feeling,
and even the little that was being done to break up
the large estateshasbeen adjourned. Ireland’s want
of affection for England is still a thing which the
Englishman can’t understand, and when certain
Irish members cheered in the House of Commons
on hearing that a military force belonging to the
people who had wiped out half their nation by
selfish mis-government had been defeated, the
moral sense of England was shocked at the black
ingratitude of it. It was just as shocked as was
schoolmaster Wackford Squeers at the want of
affection shown by his pupils, and it took exactly
the same method to promote better feeling. The
fact that Ireland has lost half its population since
the beginning of the Freetrade era doesn’t prove,
in the Englishman’s view, that England’s system
is a failure ; it is all due to the natural cussedness
of the people—just as the hideous death-roll in
Ketoiiener’s concentration camps only proves
the deep villainy of a race which dies on pur-
pose to bring a kind enemy into discredit.
And the fact that England is not loved by this
large section of the people of its own United
Kingdom is only a further proof that any want of
affection towards it on the part of foreign
nations arises from the natural malignance
of the foreign disposition. Having reasoned
things out this way, the Salisbury Govern-
ment proposes, quite honestly and seriously,
to try another spell of coercion. The
self-complacency which believes that the best part
of a nation has abandoned its country for purely
imaginary grievances, and that there can’t be any-
thing seriously wrong with the policy which has
almost obliterated a race, because it is an English
policy, and that a community can be made to love
its rack-renting landlord by being arrested until
its affection for him becomes manifest, is an in-
teresting study.
The Drudges of the Bush.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Many hearts go out in pity
To the children of the city,
To the young lives maimed and crippled in the
oity’s roar and rush ;
But it isn’t yet the fashion
To waste poems of compassion
On the little white-slave drudges of the much-
belauded bush.
Dragging on from day to day,
With no time for rest or play,
Sad-faced, sad-eyed little drudges are the children
of the bush.
Much we talk of education,
And of building up a nation
Of strong hands and noble hearts, and of the
glories yet to rise.
Schools in plenty are provided,
Where the young minds may be guided
In the pleasant paths of knowledge, in the
wisdom of the wise.
But the little children weep—
“ Give us rest,” they say, “ and sleep,
For we cannot see our primers for the sleep
within our eyes.”
Through the land a voice is wailing,
And in vain deaf ears assailing,
’Tis the sad cry of the children. Brothers, listen
to it now :
“ Have you given us, soul and body,
To the cow, and pig, and ‘ poddy,’
Sold us over into bondage to your gods, the swine
and cow ? .
Have we done ye aught amiss,
That ye bring us forth for this,
Just to give our young lives over to your deity—
the cow?”
Ah, in vain the children’s pleading,
For no ear their cry is heeding;
Out at dawn the young slaves hasten to begin the
toilsome day;
With dull brains where sleep still lingers,
And with aching backs and fingers,
In hot yards they sit and suffer, sit and toil and
tug away.
And the separator moans,
As it grinds the flesh and bones,
Of the little, helpless children, for whom no one
thinks to pray, n
GLENGARRIFP.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
More Concentration “ Bag 1 .”
Last week the cable reported that the death-rate
in the South African concentration camps during
the month of March was 45 per 1000, and if the
cable wasn’t drunk that is equal to 540 people out-
of every 1000 dying per annum—a rate which
would practically extirpate the remaining women
and children of the Boer race inside two years.
If the figures are given correctly, the mortality
beats the worst record of any plague in history, it
beats the prisons of the Inquisition with the
torture-chamber and the auto-da-fe thrown in, and
it is such a stupendous horror that it almost
amounts to proof of an intention to kill off the-
helpless women and infants and thus simplify the
Boer problem by wiping out the race. Of course-
it is quite possible that the cabled figures are-
wrong, but right or wrong this fact remains : All
the large, respectable moral daily papers of this
country published the figures, and accepted them
as true, and not one among them all uttered a <
word of serious comment on its own horror.
Jingoism has apparently killed any semblance of
decency or humanity among its votaries.. Apropos
of the management of the concentration death-
traps, Adelaide Chronicle, which no one suspects
of being a Boer organ, publishes this (19th
April, 1902) from its own correspondent in South
Africa. The lady writes from Bethulie :
The inspector of Burgher camps, Mr. Bowen, has been
here for a fortnight trying to ascertain the cause of the
terrible mortality in Bethulie camp. On his arrival he
at once dismissed the superintendent, who seemed to
have been entirely unfitted for his position. In fact, I
was told that his qualifications for the post consisted in
being a great sportsman, and having been the means of
recovering some officer’s valuable dog, which had been
lost. He was supposed to live in the camp, but spent his
time at the hotel, while the 4000 people under his charge
died by hundreds (1200 in six or seven months). The
over-worked doctors either fell ill and sometimes died
themselves, or resigned their hopeless task. The
camp had every fault possible, Mr. Bowen told
me—overcrowding, tents too near together, and
never moved for months ; bad sanitary arrangements ;
insufficient water supply, and a poor and scanty dietary.
Under his direction the tents are being spread out, so as
to cover a much larger area on new ground, the dietary
is improved, and already the death-rate has lowered.
Epidemics of measles and whooping-cough are respon-
sible for many deaths, but at present debility and
atrophy among the mothers and a sort of marasmus
among the children are what the doctors have to contend
with. Some of the people are really mere skeletons.
“ Only a course of port wine, jelly, and change of air
would be ordered for them,” as the superintendent
quietly remarked to me, “ were they in a position to get
either.” He says he has just obtained from the Govern-
ment permission to allow tinned milk for young children,
which has hitherto not been given except in cases of sick-
ness.
Bethulie, be it noted, is not away far back in the*
disturbed districts. It is on the border of Cape
Colony and on the railway line, right under the
eyes of British authority, and easily accessible for
the supply of provisions and necessaries. Practi-
cally it is in the glare of the limelight, so to speak.
And even there, it appears, nearly one-third of
the prisoners—the women and helpless infants —
were killed off in seven months by starvation and
neglect, and many of the survivors reduced to
mere skeletons, before even so much as an
inquiry was started. The fostering care of the
British Government consisted in appointing, as
officer in charge, a man whose qualification was
that he had recovered a lost dog. Nothing is said
in the report about the ignorance or the unclean-
liness of the Boers being the cause of the frightful
mortality—apparently that lie has fallen through.
The Chronicle’s correspondent adds that typhoid
(essentially a dirt-disease, and, therefore, a fair
evidence of the habits of the people) was quite
unknown in the Orange State before the war.
When Miss Emily Hobiiouse told the awful story
of the concentration camps many months ago, the
only result was a flat denial of her statements, her
prompt exclusion from the country to prevent any
further revelations, and the ejection from the
camps of the Red Cross nurses who were doing
their best to alleviate the hideous mass of human
suffering. Some small attempt at reform only
comes at this late hour, when, with the gradual
pacification and re-settlement of the country, it is
impossible any longer to exclude observers and
keep the great iniquity dark. The concentration
camps of South Africa are the foulest blot
on Britain’s history, and after the brutish
and cold-blooded slaying of women and
children in these places of infamy there
is no room for any further British comment
on the doings of Weyler, the Spanish butcher of
Cuba. As for the occasional reports that have
reached here by cable that the confinees in the
camps expressed their profound gratitude for the
good treatment they have received, and their grief
and indignation at the baseless insinuations to the
contrary—seeing that it was the people who were
responsible for the horrors who sent the alleged
verbal testimonials along, these are just about as
valuable as Weyler’s unsupported assurance that
the Cubans really approved of his methods. As
a kind of finishing touch to the story, by the way,
it is just cabled that the farms of a number of
Boer leaders—not Cape Colony “ rebels,” but
officers of the Orange Free State—have been sold
to pay for the board and lodging of their families
confined in the camps. How much per week is it
proposed to charge each of the living skeletons of
Bethulie for the starvation diet and the services
of the boss, whose qualification was that he
recovered a British officer’s lost dog ? And how
do the skeletons of Bethulie, where there was
sufficient publicity to give a fair chance of some
special care, compare with those in the concen-
tration camps further oack and away from obser-
vation ?

Me the Bushveldt Buccaneers—a Question.
The “ official account ” of the court-martial pro-
ceedings in S’Africa culminating in the execution
of Morant and Handcock is now available. It
is very detailed in its corroboration of the facts we
already possess, but observes a most significant
silence on one point, about which it is the duty of
the Federal Government to demand full par-
ticulars. There were in all jive officers of the
Bushveldt Buccaneers put on trial at the first
court-martial. Three of them were Australians, the
fourth was probably Lieut. Picton, an English
officer. Who was the fifth ? The Bulletin can
answer the question, and give his name, but
prefers that that should come through official
sources. Suffice it to say that he was a
“ political agent ” and that he was represented
as being in the confidence of Ketchener and the
headquarters staff. Now what became of this
English officer? We know what happened to the
Australians concerned—two shot, one imprisoned
for life. As to English officer Picton’s fate we
are also informed; he was not shot or imprisoned,
but “ cashiered.” As to the other English officer,
the captain who was also a political agent and
supposed to have been in the confidence of
Ketchener, about whom there is so marked a
silence? Was he found guilty or not guilty? In
the latter case, was any punishment inflicted upon
him, or was the fact of his close association with
headquarters taken as a sufficient excuse ? The
Bulletin trusts that Mr. Barton will be so far
disobliging to Mr. Chamberlain as to ask for full
information on this point.

The Dying Babies.
There is always room to make a few remarks, in
a strictly moderate tone of voice, on the persistent
refusal of the Australian infant to grow up and be
a man-or a woman, as the case maybe. This
habit of his is more noticeable in Melbourne and
Sydney, chiefly because there are more of him in
these cities, but it is pretty frequent everywhere.
Nearly every day the metropolitan papers report
inquests on at least a couple—often more—of
.small infants who mostly die because they feel
that way. The evidence nearly always shows
improper feeding, want of attention, inherited
disease and so forth, but no jury ever verdicts
murder or manslaughter against the responsible
parties. Wherefore this paper is moved to return
a verdict on its own account, which verdict is one
■of wilful murder against each and every State
■Government. The case against Victoria will
hold good against all the States. The Cabbage
Garden Government was lately told, in almost so
many words, by a coroner named Dickson, that
babies were dying like flies, and unless the
Government moved towards a scientific, general
treatment of unclaimed youngsters their
deaths were at its own door. On April
2nd, at Ballarat, coroner Dickson spoke once
more. He was inquiring into the decease
of an infant boarded out with a nurse, through
whose hands 16 babies had passed in four years,
of which 16 no less than LO had died. The
nurse said she was “going to give up keeping
babies as they were too much trouble.” Mr.
Dickson said he was pleased to hear it, though he
failed to see where the 4 ‘ keeping ” came in. He
also remarked, on learning that the Department’s
dietary scale had not been adhered to, that
departmental regulations were practically a farce,
as nurses dieted infants according to their own
sweet wills, and the only thing that would stop
the slaughter of the children was a properly
managed Foundling Hospital. The lady superin-
tendent of the Victorian Infant Asylum a
Foundling Hospital without the anonymity-
speaks even more strongly. “It is a blot on
humanity,” says she, “ that innocent babies should
suffer for want of ordinary care and attention, and
the sight of wizened, suffering, little creatures
brought daily to our door would cause the
heart to ache with pity for their very help-
lessness. Yet, for lack of accommodation, I am
compelled daily to refuse admission to homeless
infants.” And so they die —like flies. It is a
very shiftless and uneconomical proceeding. The
country is always declaring that it wants popula-
tion, and here is population going to waste. It
costs very little more to rear our own foundling
than to import a full-grown citizen —perhaps when
allowance is made for the saving of the inquest (a
jury of 12 decayed citizens of loose habits at 4s.
each, and a funeral and other items mount up) it
costs less. There was a time when this country
could disregard trifles like these ; it can’t now.
During the last ten years the six States of the
Commonwealth only gained some 8000 people by
excess of arrivals over departures—this despite the
attractions of the Westralian gold boom ; and as
things were less promising in the latter part of the
decade than the early part, next ten years may be
worse. This isn’t a time for a promiscuous
dropping of infants.

The “Herald’s” Policy of Stousli.
Sydney Morning Herald, which has an old-
established reputation for ignorant illiberality, is
doing its best to push the Senate of Sydney Uni-
versity into “ dealing with ” Professor Wood, for
his open expression of anti-war opinions, after the
fashion that a band of larrikins deals with a
dissentient, by “stoush.” The Herald, for all
its dull respectability, has always had a trace
about it of the Cabbage-tree Mob, which was the
first of Sydney’s “pushes”; and that influence
shows strongly just now. An article from Mr.
Wood’s pen in Manchester Guardian is the cause
of the Herald’s present irritation, an article
which, judging by its cabled summary, seems to
have stated some plain truths as to Australian
opinion on the Boer war. In protesting against
those truths the Herald manages to tell, and sug-
gest some half, and more than half, falsehoods:—
What Mr. Wood may think or say or do is of little con-
cern to the public, and if he chooses to join the loud
minority on this question that is a matter for his own
consideration. But we have a distinct interest in what
the Professor of History allows himself to say and write,
especially after the censure of him by his own Senate in
a similar case. He is practically a civil servant, and he is
employed in a State which wisely prohibits its servants
from faking an active part in politics.
Professor Wood is not a civil servant in any sense
of the word. And it is not conceivable that Mr.
Wood signed an article or letter to tne Manchester
Guardian as “ Professor Wood ” (though such a
vulgarity mignt seem natural enough in the
Herald office). The article, it may be quite
safely presumed, was signed “G. A. Wood,” as
was a recent one to Sydney D.T. regarding
that other terrible pro-Boer, Field-Marshal
Chamberlain. What Sydney University Senate
intends to do in response to the Herald
article we can’t say; but its recent reproof of Mr.
Wood suggests that it is in need of a kindly word
of warning not to make an absolute ass of itself.
Oxford University has just appointed as Regius
Professor of History a much more extreme “pro-
Boer ” than Mr. Wood (the distinguished Mr.
Bryce, to wit), and throughout England there
are bishops, generals, professors, naval officers and
big civil service officials who hold and declare
publicly the same views as does Mr. Wood. There
is no suggestion in England about sacking them—
not even the military officials. And surely Sydney
can afford to be as liberal as England. After all,
the city is a very small pumpkin patch in an inter-
national sense, and the actions of its University
and the attitude of its papers don’t attract very
much attention in St. Petersburg, or Berlin, or in
De Wet’s last camp. If Sydney University
Senate rushes to save the empire from tottering,
by acting on the advice of the Herald and the
News, and breaking its agreement with Professor
Wood, it will only (in addition probably to
suffering a financial penalty for the breach) figure
as a fool in the face of England. The civil service
argument (even if Mr. Wood were a civil servant,
which he is not) is silly bigotry, in view of the
fact that the civil servant who approves of the
war is allowed to call out for blood at the top of
his voice. And the demand that a Professor of
History must not write or talk about current
history, unless his remarks suit the views of a
number of persons who know little or nothing
of history, is a humorous item.
