More American Encroachment,
Mr. Reid said that he and his party believed in manu-
factures, but not in coddling them at the general expense.
They believed in their manufactures working fairly and
honestly at what they could produce. If Protection was
a good thing for labor, why should it not be extended to
the bush laborer, the hand on the farm and to the
boundary rider? If this policy were good it must be ex-
tended away from the cities to the country. (Hear, hear.)
And if it were a bad policy it should be abolished. Only
two days ago the protectionist organ noted with satis-
faction the fact that in 10 years America exported
£540,000,000 worth more goods than she imported. One
could expect that this excess of goods must have been
paid for in coin, or else where had the goods gone ?
Mr. Crouch (Vic.) : Gone to pay the interest on the
debts.
One of the great griefs of the Foreign-trader is the
fact that the United States, under its Protec-
tionist policy, has become, not only the richest
country in the world, and the greatest manufac-
turing country in the world, but also the greatest
exporting country in the world. Last year its ex-
ports exceeded its imports by over £116,000,000, and
they have shown a large excess for many years,
except in the occasional disastrous periods when
the U.S. relapsed into a semi-Freetrade policy,
and was very sorry for it afterwards. With the
surplus the Republic first paid off its foreign
debts, public and private. Then it reached out
for the position of the world’s money-
lender, which Britain used to hold, and
within the last few years it has lent
many millions to Scandinavia, Russia, and
even to the British Government, a con-
siderable proportion of the recent British war-
loans having been subscribed in the United
States. It has bought up most of the British
match-industry, a large part of the British tobacco-
manufacturing industry, and enormous masses
of securities of various kinds. One of the
London city railways is reputed to be almost
wholly owned in America. It recently purchased
a large English shipping co. And now the cable
reports that the principal British fleets trad-
ing in the Atlantic—representing, it is said,
“five-eighths of the cream of British ship-
ping”—have been formed into a big trust
or pool or combine, and that the whole
affair will, in future, be under American manage-
ment, and there is a cry of dismay in England
to the effect that the mercantile supremacy at sea,
which the United Kingdom has held for 42 years,
threatens to wither in a night. Away back in the
middle of last century the United States, which
had been a growing marine power for years, over-
took Britain and became the greatest ship-owning
and sea-carrying Power in this world. It was
growing with such velocity in those days that it
bade fair to build up a mercantile marine that
would outnumber that of all Europe, but—the
great Civil War came, its shipping; was sunk,
burned, Blown up and practically wiped off the
face_ of the sea, and by pure accident Britain’s
marine supremacy was restored. But though the
first notable event of the Freetrade era was the
loss of Britain’s commercial supremacy on the
sea, and it was mere accident that prevented the
loss being a permanent one, hardly anyone re-
members that episode. The general impression
is that that supremacy hasn’t been challenged
for ages, and that it is an unthinkable thing
that it should be challenged, while the idea
of it being lost is treason and insanity.
Yet it is being lost. In 1900, the British
shipping going in and out of the ports of the
United Kingdom was 2,428,931 tons less than in
1897 ; the foreign shipping in British ports was
10,115,478 tons more. Between these years there
was a slow decline by the weary process of attri-
tion. The hurried Yankee, with his billions and
his velocity, now conceives the idea of buying up
the British mercantile marine in one lot, and,
though he hasn’t succeeded, he seems, judging
from the alarm that prevails in England, to have
got control of a considerable proportion of it,
even if the old flag is still left flying as a matter of
form. And silly little Freetraders like George
Reid still inquire what becomes of the surplus
exports of the great Protectionist Republic, and
suggest that they doubtless go to pay interest on
its outside debts. With that surplus the Republic
is buying up the earth before their eyes, and the
Freetrade nation, and the Freetrade joss, and the
Freetrade theory that the secret of national great-
ness lies in lots of cheap imports, are gravitating
to an obscure place on the back seats of history.
*
Un-Australian.
General Hutton’s promulgated war scheme does
not come up to the high Jingo expectations
aroused by his BARTON-approved speech, when he
proposed to form one army for home and one army
for foreign service. Nevertheless it contains a
good deal of that monstrous proposal, wrapped up
in euphemistic words. If he can’t get all that he
wants directly, General Hutton is willing to get
some of it indirectly. What else was to be expected
of him ? He is a purely British person, and his
only Australian sentiments and feelings centre
round bis fat Australian salary. Hutton can no
more be expected to frame an Australian military
policy than could a French general. His scheme
is thoroughly un-Australian, and devised for pro-
tectingßritish interests only. True, he has invented
a pretty name for his disloyal work —disloyal in the
sense that it is inimical to the country which
he has undertaken to serve in return for that
country’s good cash—but that doesn’t change its
character. General Hutton says
Two factors may be considered as governing the future
organisation and administration of the military forces of
the Commonwealth—namely (a), the defence of Aus-
tralian soil; (b), the defence of Australian interests
wherever they may be threatened.
The defence of Australian interests wherever they
may be threatened ! It is a nice-sounding phrase
and likely to fool the unthinking Australian,
but for centuries to come Australian interests can
only be threatened on Australian soil. We have
no interests outside Australia. It is sheer non-
sense to talk about the protection of Australian
foreign trade. As far as the necessaries of life are
concerned, Australia is the producer and Britain
the trader. What is called “Australian trade” by
the men with a disproportion of waist-band to
hat-band is mostly British trade, and it is the
Briton who should pay for its protection. Our
own coastal shipping is another matter : that we
should protect ourselves. It is admitted by Gen.
Hutton that the peculiarly isolated position of
the continent makes it practically impossible for
an enemy to attack it in force. Giving his own
words:—
Oversea aggression could only be attempted (1) by »
raid of two or more cruisers, with a small striking force
for the purpose of landing, (2) by a large and well-equipp d
force conveyed in numerous transports and escorted by
an enemy’s fleet. The latter attempt may, under exist-
ing conditions, be considered difficult in the extreme. . . ,
Any force destined for aggression would have to be of
sufficient strength to conquer and hold either an im-'
portant strategical position or a considerable portion of
territory under certain conditions of jeopardising, if not
losing completely, its communications by sea. . . . Efforts
at oversea aggression upon Australian soil will, in all
probability, therefore, be reduced to raids by enemy’s
cruisers on its defended ports.
Out of General Hutton’s own mouth, it is thus
shown that all Australia wants to protect her
interests—interests which do not extend beyond
her own coastal seas —is a well-equipped citizen
array, good coast fortresses, and a handful of war-
ships tied absolutely to her shores. But the
General’s conclusions don’t agree with his facts.
The facts are Australian, the conclusions are Eng-
lish. Let us take the former and reject the latter.
—
Writes a man from the New Hebrides:—
To compare the Burns-Barton Emigration Scheme to
Harley’s South Sea Bubble is to compare a less thing to
a greater, a pair of pigmies to a giant. But they are
both bubbles, and when the Burns-Barton Bubble does
burst it is certain that, though Burns and Barton, who are
apparently carrying on the business of Burns and Pilin',
may declare that they did not know it was loaded, many
innocent persons will be damaged by the fragments. It
should be perfectly cjear to the lawyer who is Federal
Premier, even through the legislative fog that so
frequently obscures the mental vision of politicians, that
he has no right to use Australian money to en-
courage British settlement outside of the Commonwealth.
Nor has he any right to accept from a firm of land specu-
lators the offer of 100,000 acres outside the Common-
wealth. What in the name of common sense has the
Government of Australia to do with “ British ” settlement;,
or “ British” High Commissioners? What an undignified
sight is the Federal Government of Australia in partnership
with an outside land-grab schemeand subsidising a private
company ! It would be amusing, were it not so serious,
to hear the Federal Premier booming the New Hebrides
and breathingforththreateningsandslaughtershould any.
one hint at such mere cold facts as that theyare rocked by
earthquakes and periodically swept clean as with abrooin
by hurricanes, that fever broods over them ceaselessly,

SEMPER IDEM
BUCHANAN WHISKY
and that white children born in the New Hebrides are
subject to a terrible disease in which their bones rot,
that cannibals delight to bark and bite under the palm
trees, and that man and nature are alike—vile. The en-
terprising firm that has admitted the Federal Govern-
ment as a sleepy partner carries as its house-flag a thistle
rampant and it must gladden the heart of Scotchmen to
know that it blossoms like the rose in the Hebrides
fertilised by Australian money. On this progressive
plant does the Federal Government complacently
browse so far. It would be interesting, to know
why the Federal Government holds a brief in this
fearsome case. The Federal Government as a land-
agent has no originality. Its statements are just as
incredible as any other land agent’s, just as inflated, and
just as unreliable. Why has this undesirable land not
found tenants hitherto? Simply because most of the land
now available in the New Hebrides is absolutely worthless
and will produce nothing. And in the Cannibal Islands,
owing to the habit of intercostal interment, it is difficult
even to raise the dead—let alone to raise a crop.

4
The “Runaway Girl” is due at Sydney
Royal on Saturday, and (unless some unexpected
deviation was made from the programme after
these few well-chosen remarks were written) the
very brief career of Janet Waldorf’s co. has
already come to an end. A three nights’ season
of the frequent ‘ ‘ Romeo and J uliet ” opened last
Saturday, and the violent and ill-regulated families
of the Capulets and the Montagues battered each
other in Verona after their usual illogical fashion.
A shoddy little royal family, with the sur-
name Ladder or Staircase (at least that is the
English of it) ruled in Verona about the alleged
date of Shakespeare’s story, and it is difficult to
imagine what Can Grande I. and Can Grande 11.
and Can Signorini, and the other members of it,
were about to allow such proceedings as the
dramatist details. The gross incompetence of
this family of Cans was apparently the
cause of a nice girl’s death when Juliet
met her untimely fate. Miss Janet Waldorf’s
Juliet confirmed the impression she created as
Rosalind. She is not likely to combust the
dry, old city of Verona to an extent which
will greatly affect the insurance. She gives a
bright, intelligent rendering of the part, and it is
a bright, intelligent face that looks down on
Romeo from the balcony of Capulet’s unencum-
bered freehold property. This writer isn’t so
sure as he was last week that Miss Waldorf’s voice
is hard, and begins to be somewhat pleased with
the variety of that speaking organ. Also she has
a suitable waist and other good qualities, though
to speak of her as a star of any very great magni-
tude would be absurd. Norval M‘Gregor’s
Romeo proved only a moderate production. H. H.
Vincent’s Mercutio was really excellent; so were
Athol wood’s Friar Lawrence, and Mrs. Walter
Hill’s nurse. The Waldorf co. has fled through
Sydney so quickly that it is impossible to
adequately size it up. Until the members of a
new combination have time to settle down, and
borrow money off each other, and become really
acquainted, they are not seen to the fullest
advantage. Also when a recently burnt-out
manager opens on new premises and puts up three
dramas in ten days that handicaps trie co. some-
what. Making allowance for all the circumstances
the Janet Waldorf co. has done very good work.
** * *
“ The Merry World ” musical comedietta
at Sydney Palace should be given a better place
on the programme. Coming at the end of a very
long performance, it meets a jaded audience and
Buffers accordingly. A bright, merry little trifle,
with an effective chorus and such good performers
in light opera as Ada Willoughby, Rose Suther-
land, and Rose Hamilton taking the principal
parts, it is worthy of being the “ star ” turn of a
variety entertainment. This Saturday, another
“ great attraction ” is announced, Wong Ching
Gong and family, jugglers, who will drive a coach
and four through the Aliens Immigration Restric-
tion Act, write out 50 words in a European
language, and do many other wonderful things.
That is not how the programme puts it, but The
Bulletin’s own lie. Regarded as a lie invented
hurriedly on a wet day, it is not so bad.
'** * *
Sydney’s long-deserted Criterion Theatre
is to be re-opened on Saturday by the Alfred
Woods and Maud Williamson dramatic co. which
proposes to play the “Gates of Bondage.” It is
explained that this is Hall Caine’s “Deemster”
dramatised in five acts by Miss Williamson her-
self. The “ Deemster” is not quite new in
Sydney, but there is always a hope and possibility
that Miss Williamson, who is a tall girl with a
bright smile and a good deal of ability, has so far
departed from the original that the old story will
be nearly unrecognisable. The title commits her
to nothing, but as a matter of fact the title of
almost any melodrama will do for almost any other
melodrama just as well. A great deal of trouble
would be saved if all melodramas bore the same
name, and nobody would be any the worse. The
name need not mean anything so long as it is
large and gloomy. “The Bones of Gloom” is
hereby entered for the competition as a useful
expression that would serve almost as many
purposes as a hair-pin.
#* * *
“ The Span of Life ” draws to its last
nights at Sydney Lyceum, after which “ In Sight
of St. Paul’s” will supply some new assassina-
tions and another brand of shock. Meanwhile the
marvellous Six continue to take their acrobatic
genius to the wilds of Africa to get the heroine
and her friends out of trouble, and the ship near
the Coffin Rocks moves with surprising steadiness
through a violent storm, and doesn’t heave worth
a cent in very trying circumstances.
*% * *
“What Women Wed,” at Sydney Empire,
is a good comedy, which is not much spoiled by
the Cosgrove company acting it. The Cos-
groveians, in a fit of industry, studied their parts
to a quite unusual extent before the curtain rose
on the opening night, and knew a great deal more
than usual about what they intended to say or do
—not that they were needlessly positive even this
time. Various samples of what women wed
appear on the stage in the course of the comedy.
A schoolgirl marries somebody called Vivian ;
her teacher throws herself away on a son of the
aristocracy for the glory of keeping him in
scented pocket handkerchiefs by hard graft.
When the eau-de-cologne and champagne and
other necessaries of life cannot be kept up by
school-teaching, she elects to gain more by
appearing in comic opera every night. The comic
opera business is an entire secret, and the husband
a semi-secret, so that she leads not a double but a
treble life. This gets on her nerves, so that she
ends up by thrashing the blue-blooded one round
the stage in Act 111. After this peace is con-
sidered to be restored. None of the actresses are
very suitable to their parts, though Miss May
Renno has lifelike touches and Miss Cissy
Whiteford makes a convincing small girl in a
bright, short frock, such as no boarding-school
would permit a pupil to sport merely to do sums
in. John Cosgrove keeps the drama on its legs
as the blue-blooded personage who gets up early
at eleven o’clock or so, and brother Tom is the
usual gambolling servant-boy that would only be
retained in a lunatic asylum controlled by one of
the incurable inmates.
*# * *
By the time these remarks appear Fitz-
geralds’ Circus will have folded its tent and
vanished into space with its gentle Boxing Kan-
garoo, its heavy elephant-riding Lion, and all the
various persons who traverse space in the sure and
certain hope of getting their feet or their teeth
against something solid at the other end. It was
a varied show, on the whole, and the animal
element was strong, so that the part of Sydney
that likes entertainments of this sort, and
especially the small boys who creep in under the
tent-walls in an unostentatious manner, are sorry
to see it go.
** * *
Sydney Tivoli has, during the week, wel-
comed back old favorites in Derenda and Breen,
the comic club jugglers ; Peggy Pryde has given
her opinion of men with the same strident vigor
nightly ; and Callahan and Mack, in their idyll of
Irish peasant life, have helped to keep the lease
and goodwill of the Tivoli together.
** * *
A Brough testimonial project started in
Melbourne by Mayor Gillott, Surgeon Fitzgerald,
little Zeal and others was nipped in the bud by a
letter from the farewelling manager, as soon as
he heard of the circular that had been sent
around. The letter said: “Mrs. Brough and I
have been informed that some of our friends
desire to present us with a tangible token of their
good-will, and we very sincerely appreciate their
kind thought. At the same time we hope that you
and others will understand a feeling that prompts
us in anticipating you, and denying ourselves the
pleasure of accepting this, and that you and they
will not consider us ungracious in so doing. We
have had so many proofs of friendship, and believe
our friends are so closely associated with us, that
we venture to ask them not to further increase
the debt of gratitude we already owe to them.
Their loyalty to us as artists for over 17 years
speaks for itself as to their recognition of our work
&c. &c. ” The manager who refuses a positively
farewell testimonial from his old adherents is
something more than grateful if gratitude means
only a lively sense of favors to come. There are
no more Melbourne favors to come for Robert
Brough and his wife. They are going away with
a lively sense of the popularity they have enjoyed
among all sorts of people in the past, including
the large number who lately have got into a habit
of thinking themselves “tired of the Broughs.”
Manager Robert remarks philosophically that
the public have never failed to support his
management and warmly appreciate the work of
the co., when he managed to put up the right
play, and when he didn’t the fault was his.
Everywhere in Melbourne one hears it
said with always the same emphasis— ‘ ‘ I’m going
to pay my guinea to hear Melba.” The guinea
limit has been fixed by public opinion seemingly,
but the idea of an auction sale of seats for the
first concert is resented. As a matter of fact
Musgrove, before he left for London the other
week, stated that he had no intention of auctioning
any Melba seats. The box-plan will be open to
the public—first come, first served—and no
large purchases of tickets by speculators will be
permitted. Musgrove hopes to gain popularity
by his management of the Melba tour. He can’t
make much profit.
** * *
The last week of the Broughs at Melbourne
Princess’s commenced on Saturday with a revival
of “ A Village Priest,” which fiije dramatic play
brought out all the heavy-artillery resources of the
present co. The performance, if not forcible at
every point, was stronger in some respects than
the last revival. Brough is a good, improving
Abbe, though one would still prefer to see him in
his old part of the grizzled convict. Mrs. Brough,
whose acting grows very patchy of late, strikes a
powerful patch in her scene with Armand, where
also Ernest Vere (as the Armand aforesaid)
makes an expected impression. Charles Garry
is the second best convict Melbourne has
seen, and the other characters are kindly treated
by Miss Vaughan, Miss Gillies Brown, Miss
Temple and Miss Brenda Gibson. The two latter,
like Mrs. Brough, repeat their original griefs of
nearly ten years ago. But all this refers to what
happened on Saturday and Monday. Two per-
formances of “The Liars” were to follow the
play that Sydney Grundy borrowed from the
French ; to-night (Thursday) “Sowing the Wind”
will reappear for the last time, and to-morrow
“Mrs. Tanqueray ” will bring the long and honor-
able series of Brough seasons to an end, it is said.
&* # *
Frank Thornton and his newly-imported
co. take the Princess’s stage on Saturday (3rd),
with the definite intention of stopping there three
weeks. Thornton has, one time or other, been a
popular identity at three other Melb. theatres,
but this will be his first time at the Princess’s,
which is probably not the reason why he will pro-
duce “ Facing the Music, ” preceded by “A Test
Match.” The double bill looks promising, because
Frank Thornton’s previous shows have been roar-
ingly successful as a rule. Also, there is a hopeful
appearance about the fact that old friend Alec.
Mayne will figure as “ representative ” in front of
the house. Memory associates Mayne with
Brough and Boucicault’s highest old times at the
Bijou.
** * *
Her Majesty’s is taking two nights’ rest
after the lucrative season of “Dorothy,” and the
departure of the comic-opera co. for other pas-
tures, but on Saturday business will be resumed
at that roomy establishment. Miss Janet Wal-
dorf and the co. who played “As You Like It”
in Sydney will transfer their Shakespearian effort
to the place where Lauri lately took a benefit.
Melbourne has not seen any sort of Rosalind for
some years, therefore the arrival of one said to be
a good sort is awaited with a considerable amount
of sober enthusiasm.
** * #
Saturday (3rd) is to bring “ The Favorite ”
to light at Melb. Royal, and preparations for
Anderson’s horsey drama are proceeding on the
usual scale of completeness. Meanwhile, “Greed
of Gold ” continues to strike one of its characters
dumb. The plot seems calculated to strike them
all dumb, but up to now the other people have
preserved their power of speech, and, with the
exception of the ballet, they talk freely.
** * *
At the Bijou a new bill has been ordered
for the same night, owing to the revolutionary
spirit of the times. The manager who didn’t
change his bill this Saturday would run a risk of
being classed as an Unprogressive, hence the
announcement that the “Wrong Mr. Wright ” will
go into recess after Friday. “ Smith ”is taking
over the business from “ Wright.” Smith is the
man who meditates the defeat or death of his
wife’s aunt by means of a horrible “ angel ” cake,
and a good many people remember with regret
that the first Australian Secretary of the Cook
Ladies’ Union will nevermore be guilty of that
cake.
** * *
Marshall-Hall’s concert at the Town Hall
last Saturday was not too severely classical for
the ordinary admirers of “ great ” music. In
Beethoven’s “Leonore,” No. 3 Overture, fifty-five
instrumentalists did their best to play up to the
apostle’s narrative description of the master’s
meaning. No orchestra could possibly persuade
any sane public that Hall’s narrative, extending
to about 80 lines of small type, is not gorgeous
nonsense for the most part, but this Orchestra
presented the alleged adventures of Leonore
and her bloke in an impressive musical
light anyhow.- The more naturally popu-
lar “ Pastoral Symphony ” wa3 perhaps not
quite so well played, nevertheless it pleased
the audience for nearly 40 minutes, which is say-
ing much for all parties concerned in the perform-
ance, from Beethoven down to the second bassoon.
Madame Weidermann, in sombre tones and a be-
coming black silk costume, sang a couple of songs
(in glommerglumen German) from Wagner’s ;
“Tristan und Isolde,” and then young August
Siede had the glory of conducting the first
performance on record of a new Symphonic Over-
ture, composed by himself. The overture has
one brief lapse into affable melodiousness, also
one distinctive feature, for its ending sounds very
like an introduction to something else. Also it is
without doubt a most musicianly work, insofar
that it provides active and harmonious employ-
ment for many instruments at one time. But it
suggests that the painstaking Siede might have
spent his time much better in trying to strike out
an Australian line of composition for himself. Why
more symphonic overtures at this late hour of the
day ? Why not try to produce a musical impres-
sion of a picnic in the Bush —a Sunday-school
picnic, with its incidental giddiness, for choice —
or a service in the Jenolan Oaves ?
** * *
At Rickards’ Opera House the estimable
Craggs are executing their greased lightning
acrobatics for the last time in Melbourne to-night
(Thursday). Also Pastor, the laugher, and the
young man described as “Australia’s baritone,”
are about to pass on and leave room for new
arrivals.

has been no confirmation of the melancholy rumor that
young Gerardy intends to get married before returning
to the haunts of the Australian girl, so the damsels who
chased him last year may continue to hope on.

The National Bank’s profits represent £4 7s. 7d.
per cent per annum on the shareholders’ funds as at the
commencement of the half year. This is a very substan-
tial improvement on the £4 Is. 9d. per cent, earned dur-
ing the half-year, which ended 30th September, 1901;
still, it is a much lower rate than many Australian banks
are realising just now, and the growth of the new reserve
is not at all rapid. It would certainly have been much
better if the increase of the ordinary dividend from 3 to
3j per cent, had been postponed for another two years.
Compared with the figures of a year ago, the business
shows up as follows :

The reserve of cash and liquid securities stands at 39 per
cent, upon all liabilities to the public, which is a very
durable amount, and the worst feature in this connection
is the decline in the reserve of State securities. Other-
wise, there is nothing very remarkable about the figures.

This entry has been standing for a long time on
the notice-paper of the House of Representatives
Mr. Chapman : To move —That, in the opinion of this
House, it is desirable that the Commonwealth should
take over a proportion of the public debts of the States
according to the respective numbers of their people, as
soon as the same can be done on terms of any substantial;
profit to the Commonwealth and the States concerned.
The matter being one of the greatest urgency, and|
having apparently dropped out of notice, The Bulletins
would like to advertise it a little by moving these amendt
ments:
That the Commonwealth shall take over the public-
debts in question, even if no immediate profit cap be;,
seen. There must be an ultimate profit by their consol.-,
dation and unification.
That the interest payable on these debts and" the?
amounts paid for the management of the said debts*
at the date of taking them over, and a. fur then
amount equal to £ per cent, on the debts, shall be-
made a fixed charge on the revenues of the Common*
wealth till the debts are repaid. The said;£ per cent,,
and all moneys saved by conversion of the debt into*
lower-priced stock, by reduction of the debt, and: by
economy in management, shall .be devoted; to the repay-
ment of loans as they fall due until the whole debt is-
extinguished.
That as there are debts falling d.:p every year for the-
next 19 years (and almost every year after that); the-
amounts varying from £835,000 to £11,763,800, and; these-
debts are being renewed by the States at great loss>
which loss might be saved, in part at least, if the Federal
Government offered its own, consolidated; stock with
sinking-fund attached, the case is urgent, and the matter-
should be attended to as soon, as the tariff is- out of the
way.
That the Constitution, shall be amended as soon as
possible to empower the Federal Government to take over
other State debts besides those which existed at the
beginning of the Commonwealth, and consolidate and
redeem them also.

The profits are equivalent to £lO 18s. Id. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ funds, as at the commencement
of the half-year—a quite unusual figure, even in this
period of revival. Both the big Anglo-Australian Banks
(Australasia and Union), after a long- period of very un-
obtrusive progress, about doubled their profits inside six
months, just when the war broke out, though whether
any business or circumstances or pickings arising in Eng-
land as a consequence of the war were responsible for
this pleasing result is another matter. The Bank of
Australasia now holds £13,878,371 of deposits (an increase
of £227,539 during the past 12 months); and its reserve
of cash and liquid securities is £5,111,820 (a decrease of
£622,543, but still equal to 30 per cent, on all liabilities to
the public). The reserves, for the first time in the ’Asia’s
history, have passed the million limit. Taking all the
evidences into consideration the honored names of Jeans,
manager, and Blogg, accountant, are appended to a highly
satisfactory balance-sheet, despite the fact that the profits
are dropping away a little from the sensational figure
attained in October, 1900.

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footsoray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
Inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
j’oints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts,nuts,forgings, andall sorts of iron-work.*

Vanning Tables.—The very newest and what
gives exceedingly fair promise of superseding all other
tables is the machine known as the Wesley-Baird Vanning
Table. The table is of South Australian invention, and
has given excellent results on Broken Hill ores and ores
containing pyrites, carbonate of copper and tin. Like all
tables, the best work is done on “ fines,” and more espe-
cially when the product is properly classified. The
Central Mining and Tramway Co., of 424 Little Collins-
street, Melbourne, and 40 Hunter-street, Sydney, are the
sole agents in the Eastern States. The table has a capa-
city up to 30 tons per day.*

The expense rate of the Equitable remains
about the same. The figures for the last six years have
been :
Expenses in Proportion to Total Income.
1896.. £l7 12 6 per cent. 1899.. £lB 10 3 per cent.
1897. 17 3 3 „ 1900.. 16 19 5 „
1898.. 16 2 0 „ 1901.. 17 1 8 „
These rates are not very high for a society which does
such a large proportion of new business—which is also the
most expensive kind of business. The interest earned
can’t be accurately ascertained—the fact that the co. took
over during the year the business and funds of a small
rival institution is a disturbing element—but on paper it
is £4 Is. lid. per cent on the average funds, or £4 15s. sd.
per cent, if the gain in valuation of assets is included.
This latter is a very handsome figure, and distinctly above
the Australian average. The increase in the value of
assets is almost a regular source of income with United
States life offices, whose funds are mostly invested in
Government, municipal, and railway securities and free-
hold properties, which, amid the general prosperity of
the country, have a very regular upward tendency. In
debt-laden Australia, where the weary process of writing
off losses and depreciation appears to have no end, this
source of income is practically unknown, barring the fact
that an obscure co. occasionally inflates the paper value
of its office property to avoid showing a deficit, or for
some other purpose. The Equitable Life has now, on a 4
per cent, valuation, a splendid surplus of £14,797,717, and
its assets all appear unimpeachable, except that foolish
paper item : “ Deferred premiums on policies when paid
semi-annually or quarterly deductible from amount in-
sured in case of claim, £526,003.” Far too many offices
are addicted to this silly paper asset. It doesn’t amount
to much among nearly £69,000,000 of assets, but it is a
purposeless affair, and as the Equitable could write it off
without serious damage to its surplus, it should be
written off.

Last Monday Sydney Telegraph admitted in
its commercial column that “ the States and the Com-
monwealth greatly need a £9,000,000 tariff, and it is likely
to be £500,000 short.” This is the natural result of the
cutting down effected by the party whose interests lie in
the suppression of Australian manufactures —the
M’Millans and their kind, who live by importing the goods
of the English, French, and German manufacturers. And
the Telegraph, which admits that the tariff is already
lower than Australia can afford, is hollering to have it
further cut down in the interests of this country’s
enemies.

Tooth and Co., the great Sydney brewing co.,
continues to furnish a solid evidence as to how the
industries which the Freetrade Government of N.S.W.
protected so heavily have prospered. This prosperity of
N.S.W.’s protected industries was, of course, set down to
the credit of Freetrade, but that is only to be expected :
, Profits, Added Total
less Depre- Dividends. to Re-
ciation. Reserves, serves.
March, 1897 - £40,055 7 p.c. =£31,500 £8555 £78,441
Sept., 1897 - 33,074 7 „ = 31,500 1574 80,015
March,lB9B - 40,278 7 „ = 31,500 ~ 8778 88,793
Sept., 1898 - 33,625 7 „ = 31,500 2125 90,918
March,lB99 - 40,741 7 „ = 31,500 9241 100,159

March, 1901- 39,898 7 „ = 31,500 8398 124,193
Sept., 1901 - 37,269 7 „ = 31,500 5769 129,962
March, 1902 - 41,536 8 „ = 36,000 5536 135,498
The reserves consist of reserve fund, £100,000; undivided
profit, £15,498, and reserve for equalisation of dividends,
£20,000. The latter is an objectionable item in this sense,
that when the profits of any concern fall off so badly that
it has to draw on its reserve to maintain its dividend it
has generally fallen into a state in which the reserves are
much more urgently required for the purpose of writing
off losses. In other words, the only time when a reserve
for equalisation of dividends is really wanted is a time
when it is, as a rule, wholly unjustifiable to draw upon it
for the purpose for which it is intended—therefore it is
much better to merge it in the general reserve. Tooth
and Co. raises its dividend a point this half-year. It has
£137,619 (more than its whole reserve) invested in N.S.W.
Government securities, and £32,837 in cash. The income
from interest alone (£9149) is sufficient to pay a 2 per
cent, dividend. Altogether Tooth’s is a fine and en-
couraging example of the growth of a carefully pro-
tected industry in an allegedly Freetrade country.
• * * *
It is expected that there will shortly be floated
the first Commonwealth loan—£2so,ooo to begin with,
and almost all of it for unproductive works which should
be paid out of revenue. Even the works which profess to
be productive are only patches, repairs, or replacements
of worn-out works which were not paid for. The loan is
to have a sinking fund attached which will repay it in
about 47 years—which, even if some Treasurer doesn’t
annex the sinking fund, will be 30 years after most of the
works are worn out or obsolete. The Constitution
gives the Federal Government power to retain for its
own use a quarter of the customs and excise, and
if it retained that quarter it needn’t float any
loan, but the habit of floating a loan is in-
curable. The tea duty, which the Federal Parliament
threw away with a light heart and an idiotic grin, wasn’t
heavy enough to hurt anybody, and if it were replaced,
with a definite instruction from Parliament that the pro-
ceeds were to be used for Federal public works, there
would be no need for any loan. The Federal Parliament
has unlimited powers of taxation outside of customs and
excise, and if it used some of them there would be no
need for any loan. But the borrowing leprosy has
become so bad in Australia that the Federal Government
apparently feels it to be its duty to have a loan, even if
it doesn’t require one. No family is complete without a
loan, in fact.
♦ * * •
The Ocean Accident and Guarantee Corpora-
tion, an English co. of great size which does a large busi-
ness in Australia, closed a rather unfortunate year on 31st
December, 1901. The passing of the British Workmen’s
Compensation Act (which came in force Ist July, 1898) is
partly responsible. This opened up an immense new
business, for there was a scramble of employers to re-insure
their liability. A number of small and unduly excited
companies arose and rushed for this new business at low
rates. Some of the old companies refused to cut rates
and let most of the new business go past their doors, and
these mostly prospered. The Ocean, among others,
reached out for the new business rather recklessly,
acquired a very large amount of it, and only now begins
to have doubts about its value. As a number of the small,
excited new companies have already retired, it is hoped
that the worst of the rate-cutting process is over, also
the managements are becoming better acquainted with
the nature of Employers’ Liability Insurance under the
new laws, so affairs may shortly improve. In recent years
the Ocean Co.’s experience has been as follows :

Owing to the form in which the accounts are
presented and the constant increase of capital it is
difficult to be certain of the rate per cent, of the dividend
in one or two cases, but the amount of it is shown clearly
enough. After a period of amazing expansion the
Ocean Accident and Guarantee Corporation is in this
position : Between 1895 and 1901 its reserves have in-
creased by £605,639, but of this £177,082 was subscribed
by the shareholders themselves, and only £428,557 is
accumulated profit. With the enormous increase of the
business, a much larger proportion of the reserves (no
less than £345,302) has to be set apart to cover unex-
pired risks. The really sensational profit was made in
1898, when the great rush of employers’-liability insurance
set in, and the income more than doubled in one year—
that is to say, the great rush of new premiums
arrived, paid in advance, and the claims didn’t fairly
begin to arrive till later. Since then, though the
business has kept increasing the profits have grown con-
stantly less till last year (despite the fact that the share-
holders have put another £250,000 or so into the concern
which is earning interest that helps to sw T ell the profits)
they were only half what they were in 1895, when the
income was one-fifth the size. Apparently, therefore,
experience shows that the new business, which was really
of an experimental character, has been taken at inade-
quate rates, and isn’t nearly so valuable as it looked. The
only way it can be made valuable, apparently, is by
raising the rates, if the competition allows of that, or by
rejecting the less profitable business.

further reduced) for rebuilding Che reserve fund.
* * » *
The satisfactory price at which flax stands in
England (£34 per ton) has given a stimulus to the industry
in M.L., and South Islanders hope to reap a fine harvest.
New mills are being reared in hot haste at Lake Brunner,
Kumara, Lake Mahinapua, and further south, while the
established mills in the North Island have been roused
to increased activity. Profits on the fibre are large just
now, and indications are that present figures will be
maintained for some time. Regulations have been framed
by M.L. Govt, making the grading of flax compulsory.
• * • •
Some of the smart millers fell in this year by
refusing to go into the local market as buyers of wheat, pre-
ferring to wait and get it at their price later on. But the
speculator had to be reckoned with, and he bought up
much grain for export. Now the millers must buy at an
advance of about Is. 3d. per bushel, or else import from
America and give the Federal revenue a helping hand.
Had the millers acted fairly they would have got their
grain at Sd. less per bushel than they have to pay now,
and the producer would have benefited by at least 6d. ;
as it is the middleman has scored.

A very big N.S.W. brewery-flotation is on the
boards, and is expected to mop-up some of the public’s
loose capital.

Chief Justice Griffith, six barristers, and four
solicitors are wrestling with the big M‘Arthur-Forrest
cyanide patent ease in Brisbane. Costs of infringement
are laid at £5OOO. Whatever the verdict, the Privy
Council will, for a certainty, have the final handling. The
result covers enormous interests.
* • * *
Silver the other day got down to 2s. ; it has only
once been lower. The Broken Hill mines used to get from
3s. 6d. to 4s. 2d. for it, and in one half-year a parcel of
“ fine ” sold for 4s. 9Jd. The world’s yield of the metal
has been standing almost still for nearly ten years. Long
ago 23J-times more (in weight) of silver than of gold
was produced ; the ratio is now only 12 to 1, which is the
lowest since the sixties. If ordinary rules applied to
silver its price ought therefore to streak up ; but an
artificial price was preserved so long (the U.S. dropped it
only 12 years or so back), and so many Govt, cellars became
stuffed with the metal, that ordinary rules don’t apply.
No doubt, if the world doesn’t want as much silver as it
used to, the market can go on falling in spite of a
diminishing output. Yet a diminishing output must tend
to keep the price steady ; while if the demand keeps
steady, a diminishing output must mean rising prices.
The writer doesn’t know what the demand is ; 12 different
authorities tell 12 different yarns about it. But, taking
into account only the much-reduced and still reducing
ratio of silver to gold won, the position looks better for
silver-men than it has done for four or five years, notwith-
standing the 2s. quotation. Anything in the shape of a
substantial or rapid advance is, for all that, hopeless.
* * • *
Junction North people did very little in the half-
year to March 31. They repaired and secured the shaft (the
work had become “an imperative necessity”) and started
driving at 1037 ft. They will dawdle along with this, to
tap the lode, while the lead-market is mending. Mean-
time, for a co. that is doing practically nothing the ex-
penses are much too high. Mine wages last half were
£9OB, and expenses of management £417. The co. has a
surplus of liquid assets, mostly cash, of £3695. The
directors, or some of them, have been anxious to get
Block 10 mill working on two shifts daily again ; but it
cannot be done without reducing the grade of ore
put through. As at Block 14 it is picked stuff
that has been going up. As a guide to the value
of the lode, the mill-assays are thus worthless.
There has been an improvement in Block 10 values
lately ; but it is doubtful whether it can be kept
up. One of this co.’s shafts is giving a great deal of
trouble. No B.H. mine has improved so much lately as
the South.

The German magnetic-separation people, who
put up a plant to treat Broken Hill zincs, are now build-
ing a plant to treat the tailings at the old Burra copper
mine. The new Burra co. has not yet started any deep-
sinking.
* * * •
Cobar Gold Mines (late Fort Bourke): Last
year 72,403 tons of ore, 43,211 tons of tailings, 22,934 tons
of slimes were treated for 16,4790z5. gold of the value of
£51,276, the gross profit being £11,590, nearly the whole
of which was written off for development and deprecia-
tion. Disappointing results attributed to three causes :
falling off in value of ore for a considerable period, assays
at one time being as low as 3Jdwts. ; presence of copper
in the ore at 216 ft. and delaj'in starting the slimes-plant.
Recent advices show an improvement in ore-value—aver-
age 6dwts. January returns showed a net profit
of £lBB5. Manager confident that dividends will
be paid during current year. Mining charges 4s. lOd.
per ton, milling 2s. Sjd., tailings, 3s. 6Jd. A parcel of ore
sent to smelter returned Bdwts. 19grs. per ton, and 4.8
per cent, copper. It is inexplicable why co. does not
sink below 200 ft. to test the lode. Chances are that the
mine will become a valuable one in copper and gold,
particularly in the southern section where there is a
massive ironstone outcrop beneath which there should be
a big body of solid sulphide ore.
* » * *
Writes Waddy : —Cobar mining is at a very low
ebb just now through drought. Mt. Drysdale, Occidental,
Young Australian, Great Western, Mt. Pleasant, Fort
Bourke (Oobar Gold Mines) are all practically shut down.
Chesney is to close as soon as Woolcock’s concentrating
trial is finished, and the big mine (Great Cobar) must
follow suit in a couple of weeks. Many have been
the concentrating experts at work on Chesney ore,
and of them all Woolcock seems to be making the best
show. Chesney ore is not easy to concentrate
successfully (i.e., to get 70 or 80 per cent, out) as the sul-
phide (in itself very good) exists largely in thin lamina-
tions, making it difficult to concentrate without a more
complex system of classification—for equal weights of
round grains and flat grains do not concentrate equajly,
as Chesney has too often demonstrated. On the trial,
half the plant is working 12 hours daily, and the length
of the run (about two weeks) is very short to prove real
working conditions.
* * * *
Fort Bourke makes free mention of the copper
in the lower levels in the last report to hand, but nothing
seems to be done to see what the value of the copper is
below. Said report must be a shock to Australian share-
holders. Mining expenses are not likely to be much less,
more especially as the ore is now got from fairly heavy
ground needing a lot of timber and care, and if the new
Mines Inspection Act is administered as passed, it will
not improve the next balance-sheet of Fort Bourke.
* * * *
The Occidental people have also been bumped
by the same Act and made to do a lot of practically
useless work — i.e., useless for the purpose intended— and
the exact interpretation of the open-cut clause in the Act
is being anxiously awaited by the owners, the Occi-
dental being a pure open-cut, with a shaft sunk alongside
“the cut” for haulage purposes. If the clause men-
tioned is strictly carried into force the Occidental must
shut up.
• * * »
The wisdom of O’Gorman’s big dam is now
understood by mining people here, but none of them are
taking the lesson to heart sufficiently to excavate a few
decent dams. Most of the small co.’s, as soon as they get
enough for a div., break their necks in their hurry to cut
up the unwonted coin, and let water-conservation go.
The big co. is gradually increasing its storage by the holes
made in procuring filling for the mine ; as for the rest
their trust in Providence is large—for Cobar.
• * * *
Mt. Boppy is a promising mine. Fine plant
erected and slime treatment plant on same lines as that
of Cobar Gold Mines is going up. Stopped for water like
the rest of them, but they are making provision by a
second 40,000-yard tank. The mine has its poor spots,
and the 12dwt. average of the first crushing campaign
didn’t come out of these places. There seem to be two
short (longitudinal) shoots of stone in the main lode
running over an ounce up to two, which are mighty
handy to sweeten up a poor crushing. It isn’t hard to see
where they are either, if you go underground. The stone
is very hard, agatised almost, and the rate of crushing is
only a little over a ton per stamp per day, and in a modern
mill this tells its own tale. A very curious bend is visible
in the lode, underground—practically a right-angle turn,
something not often seen. All the plant is good and
strong, and, given rain, this mine will be heard of, but
hardly as a constant 12dwt. proposition. Say Bdwts.
Outside of the established mines, the prospect-
ing shows are nothing. They seem principally to be
worked by Govt, aid, through which, ’tis said, Mt. Boppy
was found ; but history sayeth not if a kind and paternal
Govt, collected its advances when the property was sold.
* * • *
Payne’s copper process said to be treating Lewis
Ponds (N.S.W.) ore at ss. 9d. per ton. It is a wet pro-
cess, and published descriptions do not disclose any
difference from methods of precipitation which were in
use b.c. Rio Tinto ore is treated solely by leaching after
roasting ; and the main source of Wallaroo and Moonta
revenue is from the lixiviation of that which, until
ecently, was considered waste material. Apart from the
merits of the Payne process, there can be no question
that under many conditions lixiviation is preferable to
smelting of low-grade ores. If the reported results from
the Payne process are correct, there are some fat divs. to
be earned from the enormous accumulation of tailings
and slimes (from 2 to 3 per cent, value) at Girilam-
bone mine, to say nothing about the hundreds of thou-
sands of tons of suitable ore in the top sections of the
mine.
• * * #
Hannan’s Eureka has given the finishing blow
to that much-boomed district, Davyhurst, by abandoning
its option on a property there. There is no longer any
excuse for the existence of this ancient wild cat, and the
sooner investors recognise this, and also the selfish
trickiness of Adelaide directors, the better will it be for
their pockets. The chairman pointed out that, though
the original property was forfeitable for non-payment of
rent and non-fulfilment of labor conditions, no one had
worried about jumping the ground. Nine-tenths of the
Adelaide companies’ mines are quite safe from the jumper,
if forfeitable to-morrow, as no jumper would look at them.

Frank Gardner thinks that Perseverance yields
will be brought up to 17,0000 z. per month, and that £2
per year should be paid in divs. The “ low price of the
shares reflects discredit on the discrimination of the
mining investor.” He estimates the value at £3O per
share. Frank was ever modest.
* * * •
Attempts are being made to re-gal vanise that well-
known Kauffmann litter of decayed “ cats,” the Standard
Exploration Co. Proposed scheme provides for flotation of
a new co. of £500,000 in £2 shares. Of new issue, holders
in old co. to be entitled to one share paid up to 14s. for
every two held, or 382,820 in all. Of the assessment of
65., is. is payable on application, Is. on allotment, and
balance in calls of 2s. at intervals of not less than three
months, as required. If fully subscribed this will provide
£114,846, out of which creditors will get £24,450 in cash,
and £3,000 will be lodged in court to fight promoters and
directors for malfeasance. This would leave £87,396 net,
subject to heavy deduction for liquidation and flotation
expenses. Creditors also receive 73,678 fully paid shares,
bringing total issue to 456,498, with 43,502 in reserve.
With the possible exception of Paddington Consols (W.A.)
none of the 12 mines owned by the old co. are of any
value as gold mines, and several are already forfeited and
abandoned. The scheme is absurd on the face of it, and
is hardly likely to see fruition. Meanwhile Paddington
Consols has been pawned for £15,000 with which to
appease pressing Australian creditors. Seems a poor
deal for the mortgagees—a great Eastern wool-firm—
unless they can refloat co.

Golden Arrow (W.A.), bossed by ex-cornstalk
Charles Edward Hogg, going to reconstruct for fourth
time. Proposed new co. is to have the mere trifle of
1,000,000 shares of ss. each. Holders of old scrip are
entitled to three new fully-paid ss. shares for each £1
share held, and have option of applying for one new ss.
share carrying a 2s. liability, payable 6d. on application,
6d. on allotment and balance as required. This will
provide £25,000 when fully called up and is guaranteed by
Hogg and Co. As a market movement of Is. will mean
£50,000 on total scrip, Hogg is evidently possessed of
high-falutin’ ideas of finance. Mine now got the rich but
patchy Hill End chute, but what is that to a million-scrip
co. ? As a few divs. are in sight the shares might be worth
a flutter if there were not such a lot. But a million 1

Great Fingall must have had a big slice of luck
during March as the 40-head battery is said to have run
744 hours, and treated 5084 tons for 86390 z. including
tailings and concentrates. This means that the battery
ran throughout the 31 days of March without losing the
fraction of a second, and the record per stamp was 4.1 tons
daily. Truly a marvellous performance, and justly
heralded by the shares reaching the £9 mark. But what
will the godly John Bull say to this public flouting of the
Sabbath 7

A Fingall wild cat, omitted from last week’s
notes, is the Great Fingall Main Reef (W.A.), launched
into this vale of tears by the Mount Yagahong crowd—
also responsible for such felines as Star of the East, Nan-
nine Consols, Nannine Goldfields, Nowthanna, Champion
Reef, and Champion Extended. All these have suffered

return.
* * * *
South Kalgurli (W.A.) plant has now got fairly
into its stride, and during March 2800 oz. were got from
2780 tons. It was alleged by the management that with
such a yield profits of £5OOO monthly would be earned,
but this figure is hardly likely to be realised. In last
financial year the co. treated 17,000 tons for 16,5000 z. of
gold, worth £63,000, for a paltry profit of under £lOOO.
This makes working costs £3 13s. per ton. Enlarged
plant Is treating exactly double the tonnage for double
the quantity of gold, or, say 34,000 tons for £126,000. At
a profit of £5OOO monthly the year’s expenses would only
amount to £66,000, or £ll9s. per ton—a reduction of 46J
per cent. Unless developments and repairs are totally
sacrificed no such result need be looked for. This mine
is bone-dry, and water alone must cost the co. at least 3s.
per ton treated. Net profit will probably pan out not
much more than half the predicted amount. Even so,
shares at current prices should be a fair spec.
» * * •
Imperial Boulder (W.A.), after being fooled to
squander money on a Davyhurst wild cat, came to the
end of its tether, and is asking tenders for its machinery
and plant. And to think that, in the 1899 boom, the
scrip of this rotten concern was eagerly snapped up at
6s. apiece 1 The only consolation is that, one by one,
the Adelaide-bossed stringers are fast disappearing.
The sooner the lot are wiped off the slate, and the direc-
tors go back to jam-making and kindred pursuits
the better. In mining they are a dismal failure, and, in
finance, they’re children.
* * * *
A few days ago the great and only Lake View
Consols (W. A.) was for the first time in its history thrown
open to press reps. Needless to say that nothing remark-
able happened, and the press and the public are not one
whic worse or better off than before : for the matter of
that, neither is the mine nor the management. When a
mine has been in existence for seven years, and has a
shaft down 1150 ft., and levels opened up at every 100 ft.
down to 1000 ft., and for the past year has done 1000 ft. of
development monthly, it would take Plugger Bill on his
bike to get round the course in a shift. The only
thing apparent is that in many places, owing
to slackness of assaying, the drives have lost the lode, and
the manager “ dunno where ’e are.” The difference
between lode and country is here so slight that nothing
but constant assaying keeps one on the right track, and
that involves at least 25 assays daily. The northern lense
of ore appears to have been lost below 300 ft., and the
southern below 600. The two are connected above 100 ft.,
but not below. The northern lense has been opened up
for a length of 1000 ft. down to 300 ft., but the southern
lense dwindles from a length of 1000 ft. at the three upper
levels to 120 ft. only at 600 ft. level. Very few payable
stopes are at work. The rich Perseverance lode, on the
west of the north block, is practically untouched. The
opinion of the working miners is that, purposely or wit-
lessly, the drives have not followed the lode, and that the
mine has been messed up. This opinion is probably cor-
rect, and is confirmed by the case of the Associated
Northern Blocks before Capt. Rodda’s advent. Under
Irwin’s management, sinking and cross-cutting were done
and nothing payable found. Rodda started by assaying
every foot of one cross-cut till be got on to gold, and, by
following it up, located the continuation of the rich
Brown Hill lode, which has already produced some 5000
tons of Boz. ore, with a good deal more in sight. If Rodda
was bossing L.V. Consols, writer would go nap on it.
m m m *
Great Boulder Main Reefs (W.A.) March
return reads 19370 z. from 1995 tons. This is the first
return below ounce average. During 1901, co. treated
20,600 tons for 26,6000 z. and paid no div., so what hope is
there from present reduced average! Lower levels
appear to be of little or no value, but as mine, like most
of the Bewick Moreing shows, is a sealed book, and
informers promptly get the bullet, reliable information is
hard to get. Shares are nominally 10s. and quoted at
15s. —unaccountable for a non-div. payer.

contradicted in press any intention to amalgamate, but

Waldington- Pomeroy - Doolette - Brookman push, who
foisted the patent process prodigy on the Great Boulder.

SOCIETY
The Sydney morning papers were dumb as.
oysters over the Tren with -Age fracas. The idea
of the Argus publishing Tren with’s attacks on its
rival in extenso must have paralysed S.M.
Herald editor Curnow. The Sydney dailies rarely
admit each other’s existence, and the Sydney
public miss quite a lot of fun in consequence. Fancy
S’James Fairfax writing a letter to Sydney D.T.
flatly contradicting a statement printed therein
reflecting on his honesty ! Yet David Syme did
this to Melb. Argus ! The whole affair seems
anything but “dignified,” but in these days a
paper with “dignity ’’ gets left. The only person
who comes out well is probably Trenwith. The
spectacle of the Age and Argus quarrelling over
him (for it really amounts to that) is an advt.
rarely.achieved by a politician, and should be his
salvation. If the threatened Protectionist daily
gets going in Sydney, the Telegraph and
Herald will have to alter their methods, even if
only to refer to “our junior morning contem-
porary.”
* * * . *
S.M. Herald, replying to Prof. Wood,
says in a leader that pure Patriotism (not spelt
with a “y”) was the motive which mainly
sent Australians to S’Africa. And at the
exact moment when that Herald leader was
being written, .250 Australian bushmen were
assembled in a city hall, squealing like rats in a
trap because they saw no chance of being accepted
for S’African service. Patriots ? Alas ! poor
devils, they were hungry, most of them—work-
less, penniless, hopeless, and ready to fight for the
Czar, or for the Devil, at five bob a day.
THE SCRUPLES OF THE AMATEUR.
NEOPHYTE: "But, really, I could never make
up my mind to wear tights.”
MANAGER: “My dear, that’s nothing, so long as the rest of you is nicely made-up nobody
will notice it.”
Melb. Argus has curdled the blood of its
oldest and fattest adherents by telling
Mason that Yic. Assembly has no real use for his
wig, gown and mace, and would rather pay him
at the rate of £6 13s. 4d. per working day than
retain his services at double the amount. The
Parliamentary pomps of the British Constitution
are pardonable enough in poor old Britain, per-
haps, but they never should have been mimicked
in this young country, says the hitherto Tory
journal. The glory of Mason is a ridiculous ex-
travagance ; the Mace is only fit to be stolen in a
mysterious manner, like the bauble which disap-
peared from Matthew Davies’s room of yore;
the gown and wig are insults to commonsense.
Also, the dolled-up President of the Leg. Council
and the Black Rod and other Gruy Fawkesian
features of legislative government are matters that
cry aloud for radical reform or scornful abolition,
says the Argus. The Argus, of all papers! It
is time for The Bulletin to move a vote of
thanks to the little Dook o’ York, who converted
a grovelling organ of “loyalty” into a red repub-
lican rag by omitting to bestow a knighthood or a
photograph on the expectant boss.
* • * *
The plague, which apparently has a more
or less precarious hold in Sydney, now seems to
have settled down in Brisbane as a regular
•inhabitant and resident elector. Three cases in
one day are not uncommon in Philp’s unclean and
ill-drained capital, and according to the relative
gopulations that is much the same as 13 cases in
ydney.
»' * * *
Some cheerful evidence before the Royal
Commission on Sydney water supply by the
Principal Assistant Engineer to the Water Board,
that has such a marked disinclination to paying
its way :
In the event of the Prospect Reservoir becoming
exhausted, it would become necessary to tap the Nepean
at a little below Penrith, where there was a natural dam
in the river, with a capacity of 1237 million gallons. The
use of the water so obtainable should be restricted. to
what was required for domestic purposes only, for which
10 gallons per day would be an ample allowance. If the
whole population remained in the metropolis there would
thus be a total consumption of 5,000,000 gallons per day.
As, however, there wou d probably be an exodus of nearly
half the population if a water famine was in view, leaving
only about 300,000 persons to be served, there would be
about 20 gallons per head available for 200 days.
It is a pretty prospect that financial maladminis-
tration and other mismanagement has brought
within sight—the doling out of contaminated water
in famine quantities to the moiety of a half-aban-
doned city’s population.
• • • *
Adelaide, where holy women are said to
parade the streets o’ nights accosting men with
the idea of “ testing their virtue,” is busy with
another social-purity discussion. For nearly 20
years the statues of Hercules and Venus (in the
“ altogether ”) have adorned the city. Now some
prurient minds have discovered that they are in-
decent, and want them removed—or “suitably
draped.” One correspondent to the Adelaide
papers, “Purity,” gives a remarkable scrap of
anatomical knowledge—
Our first parents were “ made in God’s image.” Much
of the likeness has been lost through sin ; and, in the
face of human nature as it is, not forgetting- its enemies—
the world, the flesh, and the devil —such indecency is not
calculated to help us to the position from which mankind
has fallen.
Knowing as much as he evidently does, “Purity”
should tell the world exactly to what extent
Adam and Eve differed in form from Hercules
and Venus, or from modern man and woman.
E. T. Smith, the Adelaide ex-brewer, who
has gone ’Ome in a sort of semi-official way to
represent Premier Jenkins, as it were, at the
coronation, is probably fated to enjoy himself
more than any other gentleman on the job. Smith
revels in “loyalty,” he does. He got his little
bauble years ago when he was Mayor of Adelaide,
and his first glow of satisfaction was hot upon him
when he discovered that the “silver wedding ” of
the present King and Queen would fall due in
another month or two. Whereupon he rushed
round to the telegraph-office and wired to Mayor
Bingey Benjamin of Melbourne (who had also been
lately bebaubled) exhorting him to show his love
for the Throne by doing something. They both of
them did something, especially Smith. If The
Bulletin remembers aright they worried their
future monarch and monarchess with some article
of value and an illuminated address—something
that the Royal Pair were probably expected to
glance at before passing it into stock.

The Melbourne Tramway Co. (dividend
on actual capital paid up by shareholders, 24 per
cent., and £37,505 added to reserves) lost an old
and valued employee recently—one Edward
M'Donough. He had been with the co.
almost from its inception, but having suffered
some years ago from a paralytic stroke, lie lost
his nerve and became unfit for work. So in the
end he lost his billet, and Melbourne Tramway
Co. (dividend, 24 per cent.) having apparently no
pension system or provident fund for worn-out
employees, M'Donough provided for himself a
week later with a long rope and a sudden drop.
Melbourne Tramway Co. (dividend, 24 per cent.)
is understood to sympathise deeply with his
widow. It is commonly reported that the divi-
dend of Melbourne Tramway Co. may be as much
as 30 per cent, this year.
• » * *
Victoria, which can find money for bonuses
to well-paid Jenkinses, still gaols its sick and
aged poor. The other day a young woman was
sentenced to six months at S. Melbourne for the
usual “ having insufficient means ” —really because
she was ill and hard-up; while a day or two later
magistrate Panton awarded the same punishment
to a decrepit old derelict who had been found
resting her body on the steps of a Baptist chapel
the while the city’s parsons were gathering in lost
souls by the ton at Simultaneous Missions.
* « • *
At the tile-making and pottery works of
Goodlet and Smith, Merrylands (N.S.W.) women
and girls are now employed at tile-dressing, work
previously done by lads. The firm had, some
time ago, trouble from a strike of the boys, so
girls were introduced. But the toil is too
arduous for young women; a portion of the work
consists of wheeling barrows; the dust, which
continually floats through the buildings must
have an injurious effect on the lungs; and, in the
cold weather, the wind that strikes through the
building will be severely felt. Is. this the
beginning of Cradley Heath in Australia?
* * • *
One of the first cases tried by the special
court constituted to conduct prosecutions under
N.S.W. Health Act was against a wealthy
Sydneyite who planked down a considerable sum
towards sending a local Cohentingent to S’Africa.
For “ not rendering these dwellings fit for human
habitation ” this “patriotic” person was proceeded
against on two charges, and orders were made
prohibiting the occupancy of the premises
concerned.
* * * »
In the case of the woman Sinnot, stabbed
and burnt in her house, by some person unknown,
at Allanton, Maoriland, practically the whole
of the body—legs, arms, shoulders and head
—was reduced to ashes. All that remained,
besides fragments of bones, was a lump of charred
flesh that had once been a human heart, and from
which protruded an accusing steel fork —the
spike on which the murderer may yet be impaled.
* • * *
Canon Pain, of Darlinghurst, Sydney, goes
to Gippsland (Vic.) as C.E. Bishop at £BOO a
year, something more than double his present
salary. The appointments, very frequent, of late,
of local C.E. clergymen to sees in Australia, don’t
yield the profits to hangers-on which appoint-
ments made in England do. The “tips” in
Australia for a traveller to a See rarely exceed
£lOO, and a thrifty man of Scotch religion may
escape with £2O. In England a new Bishop is
bailed up at every step. The Secretary of State
and the Attorney-General are the first to tap
him ; then Archbishop, deans and others levy.
Messengers innumerable “touch” him. The
“Petty Bag” chases him, and a officer called
“Half-Hide” hunts him. If not a man of
property he has to borrow to get into the Kingdom
of Bishops. Small comparatively as the costs are
in Auscralia, Bishops-designate even here have
before now followed up their first prayer for light
with a letter to some “ dear friend ” for a
temporary financial lift.
• * * *
A colonel of the Commonwealth army was
called on at Melbourne, a few days ago, to show
cause why he shouldn’t contribute to the support
of his aged and destitute father who had just
obtained an old age pension of Bs. a week Some
cold, proud men, with an expensive uniform and
£9 a week salary, would rather have maintained
two aged fathers than argue the squalid matter
out in court, but the colonel struggled with the
matter at some length and wanted to go into the
question of the old man’s undeserving character.
The court, however, held that the venerable’s
alleged bad qualities were no reason why the com-
munity should support him, and made an order for
4s. a week.
• * * *
A Victorian old age pensioner, having had
his allowance raised from 4s. to 6s. a week, was
proceeding homewards, rejoicing in the prospect
of the occasional smoke represented by the extra
25., when a stentorian voice recalled him. The
next pensioner on the list, having been casually
asked as to the whereabouts of his wife, had told
the Court she was just then living with the
previous applicant, whereupon a policeman ran
like the wind to fetch the previous applicant back,
and the Commissioner glared at the immoral old
reprobate, and, remarking that “he had no right
to keep another man’s wife,” promptly knocked off
the small increase. Yet the old man had sound
precedent. There have been respectable Vic-
torian State pensioners—Judges and the like —
who have kept other men’s wives, and some of
them didn’t stop at one or two. But the State
expects its old age annuitant to buy a halo with
his first week’s screw—and wear it. He mustn’t
drink or smoke, or be at all giddy : in fact, he is
pretty clearly given to understand that the sooner
he wears a real halo the better.
* * * *
The Anglican and Presbyterian clergyman
of Mt. Morgan recently horrified the “ unco
guid ” by refusing to pray for rain, in the follow-
ing startling terms :
We regret most sincerely that Thursday has been set
apart by proclamation as a day of humiliation and prayer
for rain, and beg to intimate that we shall not hold any
special services on that day. ... If the drought is, as
we believe it to be, a visitation of God, on account of our
national sins, then let us, as a nation, first show some
evidence that we intend to give up our sins.
Surprisingly logical for a brace of parsons.
MOSMAN AND MILSON’S POINT TRAM.
TO THE EDITOR.
Sir, —The conduct of schoolboys on board the
steamers to and from Sydney connecting with these
trams is of moment. Busy men, worried by anxious
thought on important subjects, are daily driven half-
distracted by youngsters, who romp and shout all
over the decks, becoming rude and insolent on being
remonstrated with. Could not the Ferries Company
arrange that these boys, when riot under the control
of some adult, should be confined to one part of the
vessel, and be made by some slight supervision to
remain stationary and quiet during the trip ?
—Yours, etc., North Shore.
Save your tears, my gentle Jingo,
Blot the grief-drop ; —pray BE cool,
None of these are English Tommies
Gripped in gyves of Boer rule.
These be merely Mosman kiddies
On the ferry-boat to school.
FAIS CE QUE VOUDRA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The summer season duty’s left behind us,
Life’s dull drab hue a little while has fled—
With this the only rule to hold or bind us,
Fais ce que voudra— thus the days are sped.
We stray in couples, where old Ocean’s breezes
Whisper sweet secrets to his shores that
dream —
We revel largely in illicit squeezes—
Fais ce que voudra is our law supreme.
The studded skies o’erhang a land enchanted,
Whose fairies whisper, “ Whereso’er you find
A kiss will do most good, there promptly plant it;
None wander here but they are also blind.
“ Heed not the rusty saws that preach of duty
(This is the hour when Mrs. Grundy snores) ;
Here, at the shrine of loving, laughing beauty,
Fais ce que voudra, and what wealth is yours !
“ Take this for most of joy and least of sorrow
Of all that Earth’s philosophers ha\ e taught
(Your girl to-day may be his girl to-morrow)
Fais ce que voudra and the rest is naught.”
vio.
DELM.


THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,

The blind musician sentenced, at Sydney,
to penal servitude for life for trying to burn his
wife to death, had been under the eye of
the police for years as a ruffian and wife-beater.
A policeman who once arrested Bottrell had a
very bad time at the hands of the blind musician,
who kicked him in the stomach and battered him
badly with his fiddle. Bottrell always sang sacred
music, and his hymns used to bring him much
sympathy from the piously-inclined.
HIS HOLIDAY.
MAGISTRATE (to vagrant): “ Well, sir, what do
you do all the week? ”
VAG. : “Nothing.”
MAGISTRATE: “And on Sunday? "
VAG. : “ I take a day off.”
A nasty bit of reading from the Age :
A Ballarat miner working on “contract” —some mine-
owners now object to the term “ tributer,” as it is
indicative of a system of sweating—earned only 4s. during
the month which ended on Saturday last. During the
preceding four weeks his earnings did not total more than
25., and in Jan. and Feb. he made Bs. 6d. ; while in the
month which embraced Christmas and New Year he
received nothing for his toil in the bowels of the earth.
The unfortunate man has a wife and nine children to
provide for, and all are in a condition of semi-starvation.
Although following a laborious occupation, the tributer
himself has not tasted meat for nearly a month. The
last meal of meat which the family partook of consisted
of a sheep’s head and pluck. There are scores of tributers
in Ballarat in similar straits, but, like the man in ques-
tion (whose case came under the notice of the local
Ladies’ Clothing Society), they are hiding their poverty,
and would suffer on rather than solicit alms.
Some people do have luck ! A little while
ago, a wardsmaid at a N.S.W. country hospital
didn’t feel very well, and went to bed. Next day,
the wardsmaid’s room-mate “touched something
cold ” in a drawer, and she told the resident
surgeon. The resident surgeon asked the sick
wardsmaid where it was, and she said it was in a
box in the drawer; also, that it was alive, and
that she had cut its throat. It had been alive,
and its throat had been cut all right; and the
blood-stained scissors with which the horrible job
was done—she admitted all of it—were found
under her mattress. The ordinary jury, with the
ordinary untrained J.P.-coroner, sat on the
infant; and a verdict was found that the infant
“ died from wounds inflicted on its throat by its
mother”; and the young woman (no question of
“ temporary insanity ” or other - excuse being
raised) was committed for trial for murder. In a
few days, however, some clever young person in
the Crown Law office discovered that the un-
trained jury and the untrained J.P.-coroner
hadn’t said whether the mother had cut her baby’s
throat for fun or by accident or otherwise ; and
in consequence, the prosecution was dropped
through a crack there and then, and the lady, who
was turned out of gaol, has probably, by this time,
left for foreign parts.

Last meeting of the Sydney Andy
M'Guire A.N.A. discussed the difficulty the
association found in sweating the medical
men of the State, and then went on to
consider the establishment of a weekly news-
paper. One delegate declared a “first-class
journalist could be procured to edit the paper for
thirty shillings a week,” and that the whole ex-
penditure of the publication would be “about
three pounds a week.” From this A.N.A. idea
of how to cater for the alleged minds of its
members, the public can form a. pretty accurate
idea of the association’s liberality to those who
care for their carcases (they have got carcases).
* * * *
Re breach of promise. Supreme Court of
Louisiana (U.S.) has just decided that the death
of either party is a breach, entitling survivor to
recover substantial damages from the estate ! Not
so foolish as it looks, either, seeing that an engage-
ment presumably contemplates marriage, upon
which he would have made a will in her favor; and
that, in fact, he would probably have made such a
will anyway, if death had only been known to be
imminent.
A Broken Hill jury, locked up for the
night in an unfinished ease, sent out for a set of
ping-pong. It was a tough case; and next even-
ing, after the summing up, the jury found them-
selves divided. When things looked hopeless
ping-pong was proposed again by a man who was
for acquittal. That broke down the opposition.
The twelve fell over one another in the rush to the
court-room. It was their only hope of escape.

About time that somebody got to business
on Melbourne University. The evidence taken
so far by the commission of inquiry reveals a very
knock-kneed state of affairs. The Registrar swore
that the engineering school cost £2300 a year, and
for that sum turned out from six to ten bachelors
of engineering. In mathematics, £l6OO was spent
in educating 91 students. Further, he asserted
that the only school that paid its way was the
medical school. The Arts course, he said, had
fallen off, because there was no “bread and
butter” in it for anyone save teachers and clergy-
men. One of the clerks at the University swore
that he had been there 12 years, and that his
salary was £l2O a year. He was a married man
with one child.

One reads in the Melbourne newspapers
of an inquiry being held re the skilful book-keep-
ing of ex-Accountant Dickson, lately retired to
Melb. Gaol, or some similar health resort. But
nobody asks the one Dickson question worth
troubling about: How did Dickson get quit of the
heavy paper securities which were supposed to be
in his keeping ? Everybody knows that the un-
happy punter lost thousands to bookmakers.
What they would like to know is the history of
the stolen bonds.

One of the Melb. simultaneous-mission
howlers says that he “ hasn’t been able to put a
cigar or pipe in his mouth since he came to realise
