(partially) converted rhymer sings :
There must be a lot more trouble brewing
at Melbourne Women’s Hospital, for Byron
Moore lately resigned his position on the com-
mittee, “ owing to pressure of other duties,” and
at the same time Mrs. David Syme retired from
the fray. The present state of affairs at the
W.H. invites questions as to why the manage-
ment proved satisfactory enough for so many
years. If it is all owing to the sex of the bosses, as
the male doctors would have one believe, how
comes it that the rival Queen Yic. Hospital,
organised and conducted entirely by women, has
been always well spoken of up to now ? Common-
sense assumes that two or three or four clawsome
cats have made all the mischief for the W.H.
committee, and will keep the pot boiling until
the tamer tabbies conspire to turn them out.
Australia is growing notorious for hospital rum-
pusses, conducted under the pettifogging patronage
of an intensely parochial press.
From the evidence of defendant (aged
about 70), in a Melbourne wife-maintenance case,
showing the futility of Love’s old dream (news-
paper report):
When he contemplated marriage, and was introduced
to complainant, he resolved to first endeavour to obtain
an insight into her character, and went to lodge with her.
(Great laughter.) But he made a great mistake. (Re-
newed laughter.) She took every care that he should see
her only at her best. She was perfect before marriage.
But, oh 1 what a change ! (Great laughter.) When wit-
ness came home, she would be muddled with drink. He
repeatedly told her that he had only two teeth left—(more
laughter)—and he wanted her to be most particular about
the meat. But she would always overcook it —(great
laughter)—apparently to annoy him. He could not eat
it. When he complained, on one occasion, she seized a
poker. He did not remonstrate with her. What was the
use of remonstrating against a poker ?

MAMA (to Julia's suitor): “ You are more than

THE SUITOR: “My figure! Why, what's the

The casual Australian again ! Three
employees at Perth (W.A.) sanitary depot were
lately brought up before the observing eye of the
law for neglecting to report the finding of an
infant’s body among the city garbage. Their
defence seems to have been, in a general way, that
things like that were always liable to turn up at
the destructor, and they hadn’t the least idea that
they were expected to report such matters. For
instance:
One witness stated that, on the night in question he
saw Girst carrying the infant’s body on a pitchfork into
the engine room, but he did not think any more about it.
It transpired that somebody remembered the
leg of a woman being also found among the
garbage and burnt, but he was loose about
the date, and no one seemed quite certain how
often women’s legs, or even whole women,
transpired. The man who burned the infant
said he “was used to burning all sorts of
garbage, ” and the infant had been shovelled in as
part of the ordinary routine. The three persons
Actively concerned in this mechanical pursuit of
duty were fined small sums, but unfortunately
nothing happened to the witness who thought no
more about it when he saw a dead body being
carried past on a pitchfork. It is painful to see an
unobservant person sunk in mental apathy, when a
fine of £1 might have wakened him up.

For sheer brutality, the Melbourne hos-
pitals just now would be hard to beat. The
Women’s Hospital has shown how absolutely
callous it can be to the sufferings of women, and
Melbourne Hospital seems to be following suit
with much vigor. Recently a laborer hobbled
into the City Court on crutches, and in awful
Sain. He is 51 years of age, and on the day before
rood Friday he fell in the street and broke his
leg. He was taken to Melb. Hospital and treated
during less than three weeks. Then he was fitted
with crutches, and, having a few shillings in his
pocket, was hustled into the street. A day after-
wards he was found by a constable terribly ill, and
his leg so bad that he was unable to walk. As the
man was quite helpless, the magistrate sent him
to the #ao(-hospital.

A lawyer of versatile habits put up a
remarkable performance in a Maoriland court a
week or two ago. He was engaged to
appear for (say) Miss Smith who had
“gone wrong” and had a baby, and
who wanted affiliation money and con-
finement-expenses. In the same court
waiting for her case to come on was (say)
Mrs. Jones who wanted judicial separa-
tion from her husband by reason of
incompatibility of temper while in
liquor or something of the kind. Miss
Smith was out of the court when she
was called. Mrs. Jones mistook the
husky utterance of the court official
for her own name and fled into the
box. Then the lawyer, who had never
seen his client or else forget what she
was like, demanded particulars as to
the birth of her child, amount of
medical charges &c., and Mrs. Jones,
who had a child and who was rushed
off her feet by the tumultuous
lawyer, gave the facts and won-
dered what they had to do
with the case. The court,
in a tired perfunctory way,
gave judgment for
confinement ex’s

at the manner

Bishop Moorhouse (formerly of
Melbourne) has been getting himself
abused for suggesting that ping-pong indicates
“a kind of moral weakness.” London Sunday
School Chronicle condemns the game only on
account of the general gambling that it is giving
rise to!
♦
The huge strikes in Belgium, with ructions coming
close up to civil war, are a warning against putting
frills on an electoral system. Belgium is the
place where they give you a vote for being a man,
and another for being 35 and living with a family
(sanctioned by the Church) in a house that pays
five francs tax, and another for being 25 and
drawing 100 francs interest a year from the Bel-
gian Savings Bank, and another for having a
University certificate, and another for several
other things which this paper hasn’t time to
specify. You may not use more than three votes,
though; but if you don’t use these, gaol may be
prescribed. Also there is a scheme of pro-
Ejrtional representation. The men of the
abor, or Socialist, party say that the frills are
no doubt very pretty; but as practically all of
them are on one political side, the others might as
well be naked for all the good their little vote
without frills does them. The Clerical reply is
that Intellect and a Stake in the Country must
count before Brawn ; to which Brawn replies as it
most effectively can—by knocking off work for a
few days, and asking Intellect and the Stake how
they like the look of things. And all on account of
the frills —which is something Q. Premier Philp
might find worth remembering.
OPHELIA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As I looked on her sorrow-soft eyes,
And her brow,
Clouded now,
That once had been bright as the skies—
I said to my heart: “ Poor, sweet dove,
She has been disappointed in love ! ”
As I looked on her chin, childish-weak,
And her lips,
Thin red strips,
Passionless, mirthless and meek—
My heart said to me : “ Nay, good sir,
Love has been disappointed in her / ”
P F.R.
I send no smoke up-curling to the stars,
And my body is a Temple unprofaned,
For a spirit has descended and explained
That his weakly stomach heaves
More than mind of man conceives
When he smells the horrid odor of cigars.
The blandishment of wine is not for me,
Nor, equally, the blandishment of beer
(Soft tack only is allowed to enter here) :
I must never take a skinful
Of the whisky of the sinful—
Let me wash this solemn Temple out with tea.
Yet my mind is racked with agonising doubt,
And with tremulous foreboding when I dine,
Dropping fish and Irish stew into the Shrine ;
For beans upon the floor
Just inside the Temple door
Are matters that I have my fears about.
No Temple should have meat upon its walls,
Lungs and casual things like that a-hanging up,
And coffee washing round as in a cup.
And where all is pure and holy
There should not be common, lowly
Bacon falling from above into the halls.
O man, view thine own contents for a space.
They are untidy, baggy things and strings
(Of such are pontiffs, emperors and kings)
And undigested food
In melancholy mood
Stalks, ghostlike, up and down the sacred plaee.
This place is not a Temple—not by miles—
This transient, brief receptacle for eggs
That’s borne around by two pale weary legs ;
And Indigestion drear
Is the only spirit here,
Roaming, unburied, in these shadowed aisles.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, April 29, 1902.
Mt Dear Arint.—
One Whittier, a poet by trade,has written—“the
meal unshared is food unblessed.” If it be true,
so ought to be the converse, and hence the dinner
to Mr. Barton was a greatly blessed meal. The
Town Hall was decorated with the good old flag
and pot-plant properties. Five hundred guests
sat down. The chief table wore a wavy wreath of
ivy leaves, and cauliflowery chrysanthemums
were here and there. Towards 7.30 guests
began to arrive in knots, in squads, in companies,
until presently there was an army of black-and-
white men, every one of whom seemed to be
eagerly seeking a long-lost brother, and to find
him every half-yard. Such an affectionate body
of men—for an ante-dinner crowd ! They held
each other’s hands or elbows whilst whispering
Cabinet secrets, and laid hands on each other’s
shoulders. Some of the younger men strolled in
with both hands in pockets, and a mighty affecta-
tion of nonchalance. These were playing to the
galleries, packed with squaws. A few men came
in hurriedly, made their way through the crowd
as if through brambles and briars, went straight to
theirplaces, sat down and looked bored and hungry.
Enter Eiffel-Tower Dibbs, with the K.C.M.G.
bauble on his neck ; then John Forrest, a ribbon
across his shirt-front and a jewel over his heart ;
then the Guest, escorted by the Mayor of Sydney
in his official robes. A burst of band-music
and of clapping ; one original extended his
arms and waggled his fingers at the Guest,
after the fashion of the Daphne-worshippers
in the late lamented “Ben Hur.” Then the
Guest was seated with Forrest, Hughes, Wise and
Fysh for near neighbors. All the gentlemen at
this table save Mr. Wise looked like presidents of
cattle - clubs, and had “Advance Australia”
figures. To the strains of a dashing march Mr.
Barton began to eat oysters off the shell. The
menu, copies of which were distributed with
packets of sweets amongst aforesaid squaws in
the gallery, showed the dinner to be a splendid
one —on paper.t
I heard afterwards that rminy of the guests got
nothing to eat but bread and cheese ; others stayed
their appetite with Charlotte Russe, loaf-sugar or
whatever happened to be in the vicinity. Food
was plentiful, but so was the lack of attendance.
One unfed gentleman took his expensive “meal ”
lightly but is very bitter about the number of men
required to respond to the Parliamentary toast.
“Four persons to respond to the toast of Parlia-
ment,” he exclaimed, “ now if they had employed
40 persons to curse it ”
At 8.30 the eating ceased, but drinking went on
and every man had a cigar in his face. A girl told
me, that an authority had told her, that you can
always tell a bounder by the fact of his not
removing the band from his cigar before he
smokes. Well, nearly every man retained that
sash on his weed. “My friends, Sir John Forrest
and Sir Philip Fysh will reply to this toast,” said
the Guest, and sat down. No man who had much
more than smelt the cork of the “ Irroy” could say
Philip Fysh twice quickly without falling over
the alliteration, but the Guest said it as
plainly as I write it. Then big John F. spoke at
length, every word as distinct as his personality.
After that the guests started conversation on
their own account. When the diners were at the
dessert there was a ‘ ‘ sensation ” in the southern
gallery. Down into the reserved seats stepped
the Mayoress, Mrs. Hughes, in one of her second-
best frocks, blue and white; Mrs. Barton, in
black, with a big black velvet bow on her organ
of veneration ; Lady Forrest, in white and black,
with violets on her shoulder-straps; Mrs. See,
Lady Fysh, Mrs. D. Mackenzie, and a number of
other ladies who might be classed as “ sundries.”
Thick-voiced speakers killed the time, and it was
10.30 when Mr. Barton rose to reply to the toast
of “Our Guest,” but everyone, squaws and
braves, were weary and half-asleep; besides, there
weren’t many of the former left, and Mr. Barton’s
reference to the Franchise Bill just passed gave
no electric shock to the galleries. The nows was
three days old, but it was evidently the first that
many present had heard of it. It was long after
11 before the band banged out “ God Save.”
A doctor’s marriage to a doctor has given variety
to the Sydney weddings of the week. The bride-
groom, Dr. R. Hardman, is a son of Mr. R. Hard-
man, of Enmore, the biscuit manufacturer; the
bride is Dr. Ada Affleck, of Innisfallen, Manly,
and was professionally engaged at the Coast
Hospital. A phalanx of nurses was present at the
ceremony, and at the reception a host of doctors
wished the happy pair good luck. Women doctors
usually marry into their own profession when
they do marry, and none of the women doctors
are old enough to have it said of them that they
don’t marry. It is said Dr. Hardman and his
wife intend to give up medicine, both of them,
the bridegroom taking to biscuits and the bride to
domestic happiness.
London correspondent of a local daily is gratui-
tously horrid over Miss Lilian Copeland’s presen-
tation at Court. “ Miss Copeland was presented
by Mrs. Chamberlain, which, of course, means,
not that Mrs. Chamberlain personally introduced
her to the King, but that Mrs. Chamberlain
vouched for her respectability. As a matter of
fact, Miss Copeland never even saw Mrs. Cham-
berlain the whole evening.” Why this offensive
particularity, anyhow ? Is it Miss Copeland or
Mrs. Chamberlain who wants to avoid too close
association ?
You recollect the rosy, broadly-smiling, bux-
som Daisy Belmore, of the Barrett Co., who took
: the parts of Lady Dacia in “The Sign of the
Cross ” and the serving wench in “ King Alfred.”
She married in Melbourne last week a Mr. Wax-
man, brother to Albert Whelan, the mimio who
has made a name in London.
Signor Steffani has set up as a voice-trainer and
developer in London, and has extensive advts. in
the papers. Madame Minna Fischer, one of Amy
Castles’ teachers, who will be remembered in
opera by Australian theatre-goers of 10 years
ago, has a son just back from the war who pro-
mises to make a little name for himself as a
writer and artist. Leonard Flemming (Madame
Fischer was the wife of Herbert Flemming, the
actor, now with the co. in S’Africa) had the
curious luck to come, with one other, unscathed
out of an action in which his detachment, num-
bering 30, had a fiery time. Of the 30, 28 were
either killed or wounded. Further news from
London says that Miss Grace Joel, a M.L. girl,
who studied in Melbourne Gallery and in Paris,
has set up a studio there. I remember a flower-
§iece of Miss Joel’s —a study of roses—in a local
ociety of Artists’ show some years ago. Those
roses had souls, and their mother was the sunset.
The M.L. tenor, Mr. Tom Jackson, who gave up
law for song, is shortly to be married to a Miss
Foxcroft, who is also of the professional musical
world, and is said to be young and pretty.
Miss Balgarnie, the visiting temperance lecturer,
made her final speech on a subject quite off the
reforming track. It had naught to do with evils,
wrought by the juice of the grape or distilled
grain, or with womanhood suffrage, but “Joan of
Are,” illustrated by limelight views of the maid in
armour, of her statues, of the streets and bridges-
over which she and her soldiers passed. There
was a good audience of men, women and children,
and all of the lecture would have been heard if
some he-oflicial hadn’t walked about the hall in
creaky boots from the time Jeanne came up from,
her sheep-keeping to the moment when
burning at the stake, she called for ‘ ‘ water, holy
water.” Miss Balgarnie, a stout, brown-haired
lady, much better-looking than her published por-
traits, recited her story in simple narrative
form like a schoolmistress addressing a junior
class. Whether her subject be beer, suffrage, or
martyred maids, there is never any attempt at
elocutionary effects. If it were not for the
“ Angels who came into the prison-cell to comfort
Jeanne” and the “Inspirations from on high,”
you might call it a round, unvarnished tale. But
these made the varnish. We all love angels and
inspirations, but when you get them mixed with
history (whilst the electric trams roar without, and'
the telephone bell rings within) don’t you find
your very toes curling with impatience ? Jeanne
was tied to her wooden bed ; she had no woman
near her ; she was watched day and night by coarse
soldiery, and given no change of garments ; yet an
angel visited the damsel to comfort her. If he
didn’t break the necks of the Tommies or
cut the maid’s bonds, he at least might
have loaned her his wings. Go to 1 Perhaps
the horrors as well as the varnish are false,
as history mostly is. Let us pray that Jeanne
eloped in the night with the captain of the guard,
and in a far, sweet spot lived a happy life of love
and vine-growing, and that that which was burnt
and which the Roman Pontiff is now about to
canonise, was only the sawdust and shavings of an
effigy.
The triumph of time: When the lecturess sat
down after her recital of a woman’s persecution
by men, a telegram was put into her hand. She
read it aloud. It was to Miss Scott, and set forth
that the Woman’s Suffrage Bill had passed the
House of Representatives. I should nave liked
to have questioned Miss Balgarnie about the
advisability of introducing a Bill for the propaga-
tion of phylloxera as an aid to the abolition of
drink; in (fact, I did venture something feeble
about what she would do with the vine-growing
industry, and she made reply about there being no
need to make wine of the grapes.
Janet Waldorf’s Juliet was a sweet thing in the
portrayal of the unsophisticated maid, the passion-
ate wife, and distraught widow. The American
Juliet had all the physical qualifications for the
part and her one fault was assumed—the “ old,”
deep voice which she affected for the purpose of
shading her lighter tones. The alteration was so
jarring—like a big bass drum breaking in upon the
strains of a flute. Mrs. Ada Currie ought to
make her pretty and gifted pupil say “boundless ”
instead of “boned-less,” and “wherefore” instead
of “whawfor.” Mrs. Brown-Potter had this
awful pronunciation wherever the ou occurred.
She also did the voice-out-of-the-barrel trick.
Miss Lane looked her Yeronese-picture part, and
played the unnatural mother naturally. Mr. H.
H. Vincent’s Mercutio was exactly what Mer-
cutio ought to have been, but in all probability
the real Mercutio was nothing so splendid.
Pale eau-de-nil and mauve orchid tints and
nasturtium shades are being used in millinery in a
way that is a trifle ahead of Nature herself. The
hats, dress-trails, and furs at Messrs. David Jones
and Co. are warranted to make you experience
des Emotions. One of the first has a long spray of
pale green skeleton leaves and a lot of chastened
Parma violets on a furry ground, the kind of gear
a fourteen-year-old Juliet might wear. Fifty
per cent, of imported hats carry birds, but most
Sydney women who buy these have the fowl
removed, and something that was always still-life
substituted. Loyalty to the wishes of Royalty, I
suppose! Dress trails of flowers, butterflies,
whisps of chiffon, and birds are waiting for some-
one, but the toniest are, I am told, of huge pearl
cherries, jessamine leaves, and some black Vir-
ginia creeper. Carracul and silvered fox are hot
favorites for cold days, and no one who calls her-
self a lady will be without a coat or muff plun-
dered from some pretty beastie.
Newbury-Spada 00. will begin a short season of
“Pops” at Centennial Hall next Saturday. As
Sydneysiders have proved themselves so tenor-
hungry during the last year they can now go
Newburying and take their fill of what Dani has
not got quite the monopoly.
On May 7, Miss Maud Fitz-Stubbs will begin a
series of musical afternoons, at which brilliant and
popular programmes will be presented.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Ever so many weddings on the same day
(April 23rd) as Miss Harper’s. At St. Colomb’s,
Hawthorn, the sister of big, red-haired, loose-
jointed Bishop Armstrong, was joined in the
bonds to Rev. Law, formerly curate of Hawthorn,
now incumbent of North Melbourne. The wed-
ding of the day was Miss May Harper’s. The
bride is the only surviving daughter of Robert
Harper, M.H.R. Her only sister died
at 15, of fever, at Rome, where she was
sojourning with her relatives in the course of
a Continental tour. Her bereaved parents
returned at once to Australia, with their child’s
coffin, and never again want to see foreign parts.
The former Miss Harper, now Mrs. Taylor, is a
dark, handsome, lively girl. Her mother, dark
and handsome too, is a daughter of the late Rev.
Dr. Cairns, and works off her superfluous energy
and love of rule (inherited from her father)
in endless church and charitable work,
but at the same time keeps herself well
to the fore in sassiety. ‘Dear Lady Loch,’
described Mrs. Harper as ‘the most lady-like
woman in Melbourne,’ and even the haughty
Countess remembered to send along a wedding
present to Mrs. Harper’s daughter. Dr. Cairns’
only son died young, and of his five daughters,
all well married, only Mrs. Harper and Mrs.
Suetonius Officer survive. Robert Harper’s new
son-in-law, Mr. Taylor, is a scion of the clan,
which owned, among other pastoral properties,
Euston Station, on the Murray.
“Mrs. Robert Harper was one of the few
selected to pump-handle Royalty in May last,
while at least 50 ‘ greater ’ somebodies—in the
Social sense—stood by and chewed the cud of
chagrin. Ever since then Mrs. H. has tried to
live up to that situation. The marriage of
daughter May with Mr. Alf. Taylor, on Wednes-
day last, was an occasion on which she (usually
thrifty) let herself go in the matter of dress and
decoration, and did her maternal best to eclipse
all the most swagger knot-tyings of this self-
advertising centre. The Presbyterian Church,
Toorak, right opposite ‘Myoora,’ the Harpers’
home, bristled with palms, white chrysanthemums,
roses, and dahlias. Hearts on wire rests (the
irony of it!) doves cradled on arches, and wedding
bells balancing on decorated masts, were features
of that floral scene. Up in the front pews sat
Better-Not-Wally with Mrs. Corbet. To the side
was the affable State Governor’s wife (in blue)
with her daughter and Aide Baby Browning.
Sassiety cream was concentrated within a pre-
scribed area marked ‘ Guests.’
“ Miss Harper, as a bride, proved pretty. She
never before dressed her social part so smartly as
this. Satin underskirts, iridescent with silver
sparklets, had a delicate veiling of real lace.
(Mrs. Harper is one of our keenest lace-working
promoters.) Four good-figured maids stood
to the bride —Miss Nora Dangar (Sydney),
Miss Raleigh, Miss Violet Bede, and Miss
Anderson. Their expensive frocks of French
grenadine over chiffon and glace were simply
made, and hats decorated in Limerick lace and
autumn leaves seemed scarcely large or pictur-
esque enough for such flowing frocks. Lady
Braddon, with her toque skewered straight this
time, had her patent smile on. The Tasmanienne
beams impartially, and not in the least particu-
larly, on the world. Mrs. George Chirnside
shimmered in cream and gold. Lady Madden,
pale from her recent resurrection from the Frank-
ston cloister, mothered her pet lamb —Ruby—and
rather chilled off the Naicest. The plump and
pink charmer in blue spoilt a pretty outfit by the
steeple of rigid, uncompromising aigrette in the
front of her toque. Lady Clarke drove away
after the ceremony, leaving her olive-branch to
walk up the gravelled path of Myoora, with the
cherub of an Aide. Wally walked, too, with the
debonnair Mrs. Corbet.
“Another smart bride, with a little London
experience, took herself and her maids to Mel-
bourne’s swell dress-builder, to be slipped into
their complete outfit in the much-mirrored rooms
on the eventful day. Half-a-dozen smart ‘ hands ’
tended to help, and succeeded in turning out the
most perfectly-gowned bridal party Melbourne
has seen since Mrs. Claude Macdonald produced
her stage bridal pictures at St. Paul’s Cathedral.
“ Quite a boom in Melbourne press marriages.
Since Easter no fewer than three have occurred,
leader-writer Hoare, of the Age, re-tying the
nuptial knot, and L. V. Biggs, municipal news-
getter of the same paper, and “Tom” Brennan,
of the Argus sub-editorial staff, also venturing.
The average pressman enters matrimony unosten-
tatiously, and casually mentions that he has taken
unto himself a ‘ missus ’ a few weeks or months or
years after the deed is done. All three of those
mentioned, however, went in for solemn church
ceremonials, widespread invitations, and wedding-
presents. The press is growing altogether too
stylish. Apropos , a famous Australian pressman
once told me: ‘When I was getting married I
said nothing about it to anyone except an old
public-house landlady who had somehow got an
inkling of what was in the wind, and put the
question to me straight. ‘ I hope you’ll be happy
my boy; here’s a wedding present for you’—
handing me the corkscrew with which she had
just opened a bottle for me. Alas ! It was the
most calamitous omen that ever came to a man in
the guise of a friendly offering.’
“At Metropolitan Liedertafel concert (Anri]
22) their minor Excellencies, the Clarkes, man-
aged to look pleasant all the evening. Trim, bald
button-nosed Pa turned on a duty smile, like a
good little man, whenever the good example of hia
better-half’s unflagging graciousness stimulated
him to fresh spurts of imitative affability. ‘ Her-
self,’ with waved silvery locks, brushed softly
back and twined in neat rear-coils, lent a touch of
height to her coiffure by aigrettes of mauve
chiffon mounted on satin rosettes of the same
color. Her neighbour at the concert was
spare, spectacled Dame Sargood, in low-
necked black gown and tiptop conversational
form. Not quite so many hoary members of the
‘ Metropolitan ’ choir as formerly ; the ‘ bearded
wheat ’ is thinning out, I suppose. There was a
time when the society scarcely got off a concert
without a dirge or elegy, in memory of some
departed warbler, promoted to celestial choirs.
“Melbourne Metrop. Liedertafel made an
attempt to place another Bendigo Castles before
the world last week in the person of Miss Beatrice
English, daughter of a member of the foorcein
the city of prodigies. Miss English has a fine
large voice, with a fair ring of quality, but it has
been subjected too long to the severe treatment
which Amy Castles had to endure ere making her
first Melb. bow and being ‘discovered’ by so
many people. She sings with the open chest
voice right up to the top, and it is painful to hear
her try for notes high up, and just get them by
the skin of her teeth. There is none of that soft
purring quality behind the “ ring ” which is Amy
Castles’ chief merit, but Miss English used to beat
Amy every time in A.N.A. and other competi-
tions, where it appears to be the duty of the
budding soprano to make as much noise as she can
if she wishes to scoop the prizes.
“The Liedertafel itself did its share of the
programme in good, solid fashion. The musicians
(mostly Teutonic) of the string quartette sounded
far more artistic than they looked. Tenor Gregor
Wood was regarded rather wistfully by spinster-
hearers. He’s soon to pass into what is, to a
tenor, nothingnes— i.e. , matrimony—in conjunc-
tion with Miss Allie Mattiason. Organist Fentum,
still detached, awakes a spark of maiden interest,
though even young, hopeful damsels couple hit
bachelorhood with that of violinist Curtis, as
almost too confirmed for cure.
“In William Snowball, M. 8., who died last
week, aged 47, Melbourne has lost the first and
most widely-known of its baby-doctors. Mr.
Snowball’s reputation as a specialist in the treat-
ment of infantile diseases was legitimately earned.
Other practitioners—men and women—could pre-
scribe exactly what he prescribed in nine cases
out of ten, but he was the oracle who knew what
to do and what not to do in the tenth case.
Nothing of the alarmist about him. Snowball
devoted much of his brains and experience to
discovering satisfactory reasons for ‘ oddness ’ in
children. He was the court of appeal for anxious
mothers whose babies had been, perhaps, roughly
misjudged by the family doc. They went to
Snowball with their hopes and fears, and came
away minus the fears. The death of Mr. Snow-
ball is a national loss.
“ A casual visit to the Federal House, the other
night, provided some thoughtful study. Alfred
Deakin, on the verge of being nominated as the
temporary substitute of the Prime Minister,
talked at lightning speed to three members. The
black-whiskered Minister, alert as a terrier, skipped
from one Senator to another, answering, criticis-
ing, and adding comment in lightning flashes. In
one of the corridors, Senator R. E. O’Connor
stalked up and down, up and down in tireless
fashion. Outside the refreshment lobby,. Boss
Barton sat—a worried, troubled figure, ink and
paper before him, a clerk at his side, indecision in
his attitude. In the refreshment rooms, John
Forrest entertained an hilarious coffee-party of his
wife’s friends. In the Chamber itself, the
Woman’s Franchise Bill engaged the consideration
of Minister Lyne, who vapidly, woodenly, maunders
on to his regardless brethren about the rights of
lovely woman. Erect and solitary on the Minis-
terial bench sits the long, lean Fysh ; he wears the
look of the righteous publican at a church service.
The broad-girthed Knox looks round at the visi-
tors’ gallery and smiles engagingly at the fair
franchise agitators. He passes the wink along,
till three or four other Federal loungers look and
laugh too. Miss Locke, in Vida Goldstein’s old
place, in the commanding corner of the gallery, is
the one absorbed listener. She is very grave,
visibly impressed with her leadership of the
franchisites. A few other casual girl visitors are
soon disgusted at the tame proceedings, and rustle
out. The Reps, at any time are not ladykillers.
“There was a big wedding-party at a Mel-
bourne theatre one recent evening. It turned up
in all its finery, bouquets, special dresses, and all
the pomp and circumstance of a glorious match.
The whole party left long before the conclusion of
the last act —and a rude gallery laughed up-
roariously.
“The venerable Duncan Gillies tip-toed his
way into a box at Her Majesty’s last Saturday
night with the Scotch balladist, Maggie M‘Cann,
and her married sister. Duncan grows more like
a dried Mildura apricot every day—the color of
that preserved dainty extending over the furrowed
face and round the hairless head. The venerable
legislator very often gives these dinners and.
theatre parties to friends.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

Related—was it of Jay Gould ? —that his
handwriting was so bad as to be illegible to the
old man himself. A letter of his replying to an
application for employment on one of his numer-
ous railways was, on the strength of the signature,
used by the recipient as a pass over the line for
many years. In later days the typewriter has
done much to prevent fraud and mistakes formerly
traceable to bad writing. Had J.G., like The
Bulletin, used a “New Century” machine his
reputation as a letter-writer would be better to-
day. New Century Typewriters, the latest from

A member of Parliament, called as a wit-
ness in a Sydney suburban court, one morning
lately, was asked by the presiding magistrate
what he lived upon. The member leaned con-
fidentially over the ledge of the box and gently
tapped his cheek. “Yes, yes,” said the magis-
trate, and business proceeded.
Premier Seddon explains that it was a
certain Maori chief, one Waipere, who made the
sanguinary remarks alleged to have been made by
him (Bun Tuck) at Papawai. The Bulletin is
prepared to believe that it was the Maori chief
Waipiro (which is another name for whisky) who
made the remarks in question.
Conroy, M.H.R., the toothache of the
Reid party, shows a lot of letters received by him
from Victorian protectionists. The writers are
alleged to say that they have been converted to
Freetrade by Conroy’s yelps and other arguments
during the tariff debate. Leg-pulling is still a
popular game, it would appear.
Ex-Premier Braddon, of Tasmania, though
73 years of age and nearly a quarter of a century
on Commonwealth territory, has never, as he said
in the House of Reps., seen an aboriginal gin.
Had he confined himself wholly to Tasmania the
statement would excite no surprise, but the ex-
Indian tiger-slayer has been in almost all of the
States at one time or another.
It is whispered that one of the Australian
Agents-General in London finds greater attraction
in liquids than in solids, particularly when the
first are represented by champagne and the latter
by routine office work. Folk in positions of that
kind should be cautious ; so many persons hanker
after the reversion of their billets.
If Sydney University Senate “ sacks ” Pro-
fessor Wood it will naturally force Oxford Univer-
sity to reluctantly sack Professor Bryce and
others, for Oxford couldn’t afford to be outdone in
loyalty by Sydney. And surely Dr. Maclaurin,
Dr. Renwiek and Co. will do nothing to disoblige
Oxford, where the lords come from.
What are public testimonials worth in face
of the fact that Dahomey Seddon is likely to get
several thousand sovereigns, while the Hori Grey
Memorial Fund, now closed, shows that the Cape
of Good Hope, Maoriland and Australia raised
just .£6ll 12s. lOd. (most of it contributed by the
respective State Treasurers) to commemorate the
name and fame of the great man who conferred
such inestimable benefits on the three countries
named.
Nearly 30 years ago Miss Stuart, a niece
of Alex. Stuart who became N.S.W. Premier
afterwards, delivered in London a speech on
women’s f anchise, which, on being sent by Mr.
Stuart to the late Henry Parkes, is said to have
converted him to the principle ; not indeed to the
principle of women’s franchise in itself, but to the
belief in its inevitableness. Parkes often ex-
pressed the hope that Miss Stuart would gain a
seat in the Commons. A badly disappointed
hope.
“ On the Spot ” to The Bulletin :
Re the sacrilegously-named S’African “ Patriotic
Scouts”—Vilonel, who took upon himself the formation
of this corps of Free Staters, lived in the Senekal district
and was a general law-agent, his partner being one
Kitchener. At the beginning of the war he was tried for
treason and locked up at Winburg. When this town was
entered by the British he was set free. Most of these
“ National ” or “ Patriotic ” scouts are of the same kidney
and in no way indicate the feeling of the mass of the
people in the two republics. Vilonel is simply inspired
by personal animosity.
“ A.P.” on the Rhodes bequests :
The late “ Colossus ” of S’Africa has bequeathed the
better part of 12 million pounds to further Imperial
schemes. Where is the £12,000,000 to come from ? The
bulk of the Rhodes estate consists of real property
in Rhodesia and Cape Colony. Well, his Rhodesian
lands (millions of acres) are now absolutely un-
saleable, and must continue to be unrealisable
(except at enormous loss) for years to come. As for
personalty, I am willing to bet that it will be found to
fall short of a million in cold cash. Did Rhodes make his
will before the war—when he actually was, on paper at
least, a multi-millionaire ; or did he—always an expert
self-advertiser—contrive the instrument on his death-
bed, in a spirit of grim jest ?
Anglican Canon Green, of Glenelg (S.A.),
has been sent ’Ome for a holiday, with a purse of
sovereigns from his congregation. Yet he told
them last Christmas that there were not six truly
religious women in that fashionable watering-
6 lace “in the ranks of what is called Society,”
ecause they did not consent to keep the Friday
of every week sacred, in memory of the Cruci-
fixion. It was the Canon who absolutely, by his
own exertions, ended a disastrous strike at Port
Adelaide a few years ago, and his eccentricities
are overlooked in favor of His real strength of
character.

Ex-Governor Beauchamp has not forgotten
N.S.Wales. He lately sent out to Mr. J. W.
Turner, head-master of Fort-street (Sydney) public
school, a substantial cheque wherewith to purchase
books for a lad of 17, a student at Fort-st., whose
considerable literary talent was brought under the
Earl’s notice a little while before he left Sydney.
Crick and Eden George were not the first
in N.S.W. to offer the Church a percentage of
their horse-racing wins. Governor Hercules
Robinson backed a double, and promised 5 per
cent, to St. Barnabas’ Church (C.E.), George-st.,
Sydney, if he won. The difference between the
transactions was that Crick and Eden George
lost, whereas his Ex. won, and paid over the pro-
mised amount.
“Exit May Yohe from the Peerage
Stakes,” writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent. “ She will hardly be remembered as a
swinging girl at the Victoria Hall (Melb.) in
Frank M. Clark’s famous or notorious Silk Stock-
ings Co. In ’93 she fairly took London by storm
in burlesque, and in ’94 married Lord Francis
Hope. She was then a lovely American brunette
(she was said to have Indian blood in her veins).
The co-respondent is a son of a N. Y. mayor who
is related to an Australian mayor of some pro-
minence—at least they both have been mayors.
Hope, who nearly blew his foot off while shooting,
was wheeled into court for his legal remedy. ”

Old David Mitchell, Melba’s father, makes
no secret nowadays about his admiration for
daughter Nellie. He was noticeable among those
who went to hear Amy Castles in Melbourne,
incited thereto by the comparisons made between
the famous soprano and the young girl who is just
bidding for elbow-room among the ilite. Asked
afterwards what he thought of Amy, old man
Mitchell could not conceal his pride in the dis-
obedient child who carved her way in spite of his
prohibition.
Another chapter in De Rougemont’s
strange career was entered upon at Parramatta
(N.S.W.) police court, the other day, when his
wife sued him for maintenance. It was a sorrow-
ful tale of misery—a poor, sick woman and hungry
children depending for their very thin bread and
butter on the few shillings a week occasionally
earned by their unhappy, careworn father at
life-assurance canvassing. The public might with
grace give a few pounds to this family: The
Bulletin will give a donation.
The South Australian Jenkins has done a
foolish thing. He did not exactly write a book
to gratify and satisfy a Scriptural enemy. He
did worse. He wrote an autobiographical letter
to one Lyon, of Guelph, Ontario. The de-
lighted Lyon, in the pride of .his heart, carried
it to the printer, and now Jenkins’ vainglorious
screed appears in cold type in the columns of an
Ontario daily. There is nothing reticent about
the letter. Such a document, if published just
before a general election, would make any Jenkins
sit up.
That clever and popular American artist,
Fred. B. Schell, who left Australia for New York
to take up the art-editorship of the Harper publi-
cations, and, after doing some years’ excellent
work, was compelled to retire on account of hope-
less illness, can now, many Australians will much
regret to hear, hardly manage to totter across his
room. His trouble is paralysis, from which his
nearest friend, Dr. Morrison, of “ Men of Mark ”
fame, died the other day. Miss Morrison, by the
way, is the wife of that well-beloved actor, Charles
Arnold.
A forgotten Premier of S.A. is Henry-
Bull Templer Strangways, who, being called to
the English Bar when 24 years old, went to Ade-
laide, and at 26 was elected to the local Parlia-
ment ; at 27 had the chance of forming a Govern-
ment, but declined, and at 28 became a subordinate
Minister. He resigned as head of a Cabinet in
1870, almost immediately afterwards returning
to England, where he has remained ever since.
He is only 70 years old, but if he is ever men-
tioned in S.A. it is as a man who flourished before
the Flood. He is chiefly held in esteem as the
author of the Act which created agricultural areas
in S.A., and permitted the sale of Crown lands
on credit.
Apropos the Vic. alleged reform move-
ment, Taverner, M.L.A., looking for the Premier
at the height of the fuss, asked Peacock’s police-
orderly whether “Mr. Goddard ” was in. “Who?”
interrogated the mystified protector of the Smiler.
“Mr. Goddard,” said Taverner. “I don’t know
any Mr. Goddard,” answered the minion ; “ he’s
not in this building.” “Perhaps I’m mistaken,”
remarked Taverner; “you see, I’ve been away
for the last week or two, and I understood there
had been a change of Govt. Who’s Premier now
—Peacock or Goddard?” “Well,” drawled the
orderly, “ I’m not exactly sure myself, but if it’s
any Treasury matter you’ve come about, I think
the best plan would be to see Mr. G. D. Meudell.”
The pen lies heavy to my hand,
My eyes are faint and dim—
There in the mirror as I stand,
How lustreless their rim 1
Is that my faoe ? The misty outline fades,
A phantom visage of the Land of Shades ;
And I had marked so many things to do,
So many things undone !
The priest declared my moments were too few ;
The doctor —I had none I
I dashed the mirror to the floor,
I spurned my best-lov&d book,
I opened wide God’s eastward door
To take my last long look !
And lo ! the mirror and the book were there,
And I betwixt them, kneeling as in prayer,
While all the many things I’d marked to do —
And all the things undone—
Seemed by God’s gracious mercy light and few,
And priest and doctor gone !
A New Sydney Daily Paper.
The prospectus of the co. which it is proposed to
form for the purpose of founding a new paper in
Sydney is now in preparation—for private circu-
lation only. Supposed sympathisers with the
project have, however, been canvassed for some
time past, so far (we learn on good authority) with
very discouraging results indeed. The prospectus
bears out that authority, for no provisional
directors are named, the only persons mentioned
being Malcolm M‘Eachakn, of Melbourne, and
Archibald Forsyth, of Sydney, who are
“ treasurers ” pledged to hold any money sub-
scribed in trust and to return it if the co. is not
successfully floated; and excellent treasurers they
are.
* * * *
There is not much in the project to commend it
to the investing public, or to those who would be
willing to help with a few pounds towards the
establishment of a national morning newspaper.
It is proposed to raise £125,000 in 125,000 shares,
payable ss. on application, ss. on allotment, and
the balance in calls not exceeding Is. per share per
month. So soon as 40,000 shares are applied for
the company will be formed. It is indicated
that the policy of the paper will be “to conserve
Australian industries,” and a new cable service
is promised.
* * • *
Considered politically, the proposed new paper
promises but very little change from existing
dailies. The names of Mr. M’Eacharn and Mr.
Forsyth suggest a consistent devotion to Jingo-
Tory principles. “A new cable service”
sounds attractive, but of that more anon. So far,
the people who have been approached for assist-
ance have been all of the one class—the class of
S’Malcum M'Eaoharn —and their response has
not been enthusiastic. There was talk of one
manufacturer who was going to “put down”
£lO,OOO, and of a big tanner who was good for the
same amount, and of many others who were eager
with thousands. Mr. David Syme was also re-
ported to be violently anxious to subscribe at
least £20,000. But all that hasn’t materialised.
There is no mention in the prospectus of acquiring
the Star evening newspaper, and whether that
part of the project has been dropped or not is a
subject on which there are many conflicting
accounts.
• • * »
Considered financially, the prospectus is almost
farcical. The full capital provided for is £125,000,
and a start is to be made when 40,000 shares are
applied for, which shares would provide just
£20,000 cash and £2OOO a month for ten months.
Considering the fact that the Herald property is
worth at capitalised value about £1,000,000, and
the Daily Telegraph at least £300,000, what
chance has an enterprise with, at the utmost,
£125,000 available, of competing wi th these concerns
on equal terms and of establishing an indepen-
dent cable service ? And the Australian Times
(that is the proposed name) promises to start when
it has £20,000 in hand and another £20,000 call-
able within 10 months ! Sheer midsummer mad-
ness ! A modern plant (two printing-machines,
12 linotypes, steam plant, etching plant, office-fit-
tings, &c.) would easily run away with the whole
£20,000, and the £2OOO a month coming in from
calls wouldn’t go anywhere near to paying rent,
wages, paper, ink, telegrams, &c., especially as
there is talk of an editor at £3OOO a year at least,
twice the salary of any present daily paper
editor. The proposed capital is, in short, ridi-
culously inadequate, and to start on the lines
proposed would mean inevitable failure.
* * * *
An independent cable service alone would
cost £BOOO a year at the least, and that sum
would have to be spent with discreet economy. Of
course this review of the chances of the proposed
new paper —a company-owned concern which
would have to win on its money if it won at all—
would not apply to the project if it were in the
hands of one or two knowledgeable newspaper
men with no political ties, with independent
minds, and with a resolution to give the public a
fair deal for its penny. They might start and
succeed >on £40,000. But the Australian Times,
if it comes to light, will have to fight with
its money-bags, and with them alone. It is
nonsense to suppose that its ideals will be any
loftier than those of the Herald or Telegraph,
or that it will be able to get better staff men,
except by paying better wages. And, in the con-
test of money, £20,000, or £125,000, has no chance
against £1,000,000.

The returned cohentingenter does not
promise to be an unmixed blessing to Maoriland
(writes a Bulletin correspondent). Some farmers
have been looking forward to the end of the war,
in the hope of getting cheaper labor when all the
patriots came home. But, unfortunately, the
man who comes back is altogether different from
the person who went away. The one was mostly a
farm hand, rather vain perhaps, and a trifle lazy,
but still useful, owing, in a measure, to his
ignorance of the outside world. The returned
hero is just as ignorant, a good deal lazier, and a
chronic wind-bag. His importance is immense,
and he nearly always expects a commission in the
next contingent. If the war is not over before
"next election, Seddon will require to despatch a
contingent consisting entirely of officers. From
their yarns (mostly gruesome), I learn that a
fatigue-party (Bushveldt Carbineers’), sent to
bury the dead, “chucks ’em all in, breathing or
not, damn them !” and that the greatest hardship
is the hunting for vermin on their clothing, which
is a daily duty, and begins soon after sailing.
What is Maoriland going to do with some thou-
sands of him later on ?
Sydney Morning Herald cordially assured
the public last week, in a leading article, that
there was no truth whatever in Professor Wood’s
statement that many volunteers went to South
Africa, not from love of the Empire, but to
escape the monotony of the bush. The wealthy
Herald, which dawdles all the year round com-
fortably adjacent to cool drinks and the pleasant
harbor and the opera and the refreshing Turkish
bath, can gather up the tail of its £6 10s. coat
under its arm, and take its half-crown
cigar out of its mouth, and say things
like that at its club quite easily. But Out-
back, where the grass and trees and saltbush
and sheep and cattle are dead, where the
crops are dead, where hope and credit are dead,
where the moving sand-drift blows where it lists
and banks up your house in a night, where whisky
is kerosene and old hatters are loony and squatters
are bankrupt, where almost the whole country
west of Dubbo is becoming depopulated, where
people wash about once a month because they
can’t get water to wash oftener, where 10s. a week
is wealth and 12s. 6d. is to be a Croesus, where
the selector cuts his own hair, where the perishing
framework which used to be a horse leans
up against the hut whose floor is dust
and its roof bark, where a half-naked race
of children is growing up in semi-savagery—well,
the well-fed Herald says that none of the volun-
teers who came from there were actuated by a
desire for change, and that they were all urged on
by the purest devotion to the Enipiah. And it
may be so. But this paper is willing to bet a
small sum that if the Herald proprietors changed
places with the people who struggle for a crust in
the back counties of Yanda and Yantara and
Yungnulgra they would, inside two years, be will-
ing to take a billet at shooting anybody for even
half-a-crown a week in order to get away.

“ Yeteran ” : Australia has made a mistake
in dressing its soldiers in kharki. The yellow-ochre
color was protective on the sandy plains of the
Punjab, where it was first adopted by the Sikhs
under old Ranjit Sing. So was the so-called
“rifle-green” in the pine forests of the Tyrol.
Kharki doesn’t seem to have been much of a suc-
cess as a protective color in S’Africa. Under
Australian conditions it is still less likely to
answer. Nature, which protectively adapts the
tints of beasts, birds, and insects to their back-
grounds, is remarkably sparing of yellows. Dark-
grey and various shades of brown are its favorites
for quadrupeds, and when you see a lot of kharki-
clad swaddies against a background of foliage you
know the reason. The kharki shows almost as
clearly as white from a distance, especially if the
foliage is distinctly Australian, whereas the dark-
green of the German jager or old-fashioned
British rifleman, though it shows up like black
against a background of bare sand-hill, is invisible
against a background of dark foliage. If an
approximately invisible dress is wanted for the
Commonwealth army it should be either dark-
grey or brown—say heather mixture. That would
match almost any background except the afore-
said sand-hill. Nothing would match that but
lemon-yellow.

A tip for the Sydney Health Court (5500
cases listed), now sitting. The drunks were so
numerous in Day Dawn (Murchison, W.A.) after
last big monthly pay, that the police had to go
out with a cart and gather them up, and “cord”
them alongside the chain-post. Then the local
“ foorce ” duly entered in the drunk-book : “ Two
and a-half cords of drunks.” Next morning the
local J.P. fined them 30s. a cord—a tarpaulin-
muster of a shilling apiece. It only wants to be
altered to £3OOO per cord, to fit the Sydney case.

Re goats in N.Q. At Charters Towers
they greatly abound, and in hard, dry times Nan
provides milk for hundreds of homes. In ’95 the
local council determined to enforce the licensing
clause, and the poundkeeper started to impound,
He started, but about 500 brawny Welsh, Irish,
and Cornish ladies made it so hot for him that
there was a sudden resignation, and Nan and
William continued to thrive unmolested on empty
tins and diorite.

“Yarrum” : Have often wondered why
poat, as a diet, is counted so objectionable. There
is no cleaner animal ; and who ever heard of a
goat having any disease ? A sick goat is as great
a rarity as a tortoiseshell tomcat. Knew a
Riverine squatter who would exchange a fat
wether for a good lump of a kid any time.

“ D.H.R.” : The “ native com-
panion ” has a peculiar method of feeding.
A flock of these birds will settle on a .'.well-'
grassed piece of land and diligently pick
it clean, leaving not a blade. Then they
shift, and repeat the process elsewhere.
“ Jaf ” protests :
Is there really a N.S.W. Forestry Department? If
so, what does it exist for ? Certainly, not to conserve
the valuable timbers of the State. I know of several
settlement-leases in Riverina where special conditions
were imposed on the lessees not to destroy useful box and
ironbark timber —of which there was none. But there
was much useful young stringy-bark which the lessees
are ruthlessly destroying, so that there may be a little
more grass for the rabbits and a few alleged sheep. No
action can be taken against these vandals, for the simple
reason that stringy-bark is not specified in the contract,
and anyway it’s too late now. What kind of a fool-dept.
is it that doesn’t take the trouble to find out anything,
but just trusts to luck and the grace of God in drawing
up its leases, in its eagerness to crowd land-hungry
settlers on to impossible areas? Will the Lands
Dept, paste in its hatPresident-of-the-Land-Appeal-Oourt
Oliver’s definition of a “home”: “The word home
denotes the maintenance, not of a bark-hut or shanty,
with sordid surroundings to match, but a reasonably com-
fortable place of residence, with the means and resources
derived from the holding on which it is situated, sufficient
to maintain a wife and bring up and educate the members
of an average family so as to take their places as respect-
able members of a community.” He might have added :
“ And not to create a race of farm-burners and looters ” !
» * * *
Good timber is getting scarce in N.S. W.
Timber-merchants complain of the second and
third-rate stuff sent to them, and attribute this to
the unfair amount of exporting by certain firms.
English builders specify Australian woods in their
contracts, and the result is that the first-class
material goes ’Ome while we have to put up with
inferior qualities.

Aboriginals at Hall’s Creek, Kimberley
(W.A.) make spear-heads of the white man’s
glass, chipping a piece to the shape of a gum-leaf,
roughly serrated. They use the sole of the foot as
a ’ "'sting-place for the glass while chipping it.
SI ueh a spear-head strike a bone it splinters
and i. a fearful wound, and its penetrative
power is fairly good—altogether a dangerous
weapon.

From Melb. Age :
The accommodation of the Great Western State school
is greatly overtaxed. Into a room 35ft. by 20ft. are
crammed over 70 children and three teachers, in addition
to II desks, teachers’ desk, chairs, press, blackboard, and
a large gallery. In consequence of the vitiated atmo-
sphere, children every day become ill.
And yet the Black Hole of Calcutta is one of the
awful examples of the State school books.
“ Alpha” : In a Murray township, well
over the snow-line, about a dozen miners meet
every winter Sunday evening in the bar-parlor of
the only pub, and pass the evening singing
hymns, reciting “ Banjo ” Paterson’s poems,
and drinking whisky. Everything is con-
ducted in the most decorous manner. The
publican calls out the hymn, leads off in
the singing, collects the money for the
whiskies, and looks after the dice. Each
member shakes for himself, and the winner
of the pool contributes a shilling to the
local hospital-box. The congregation repre-
sents several nationalities, but no religious
belief worth mentioning—although one man is
suspected of having been at church 10 or 15 years
ago !
• * * *
A Far-Western station-manager writes
The old shepherding-days have returned. I have
established a camp of six scrub-cutters where there is still
a good quantity of mulga, and here all the sheep I have
left alive are shepherded. Every two days they have to
be brought into my sole remaining tank for a drink.
This takes all day, a tedious job, and you can just imagine
what the cloud of dust is like, going constantly over the
same track. Total rainfall for the last five months, five
points. I always help to drive the sheep on m.v bike as
the.few horses (or rather horse frame-works) I have left
couldn’t carry a saddle. The scrub-cutting must cost
about £lO a week, but men don’t care about coming out
here now no matter what wage. The prospect of death
from thirst on the track isn’t alluring, and even when
they do reach a scrub-cutter’s camp, water is carted to
them in grudging quantities, out of a tank which has to
keep alive men, sheep, horses, bullocks, diseased rabbits,
emaciated kangaroos and emus (their bodies covered with
poverty-lice), some of which creatures get stuck in the
side-glue and die there, whilst in the water round them
float the drowned blow-flies which have been too eager to
get at them. Great stretches of timber have given up the
ghost—yarran, pine and belar. Carriers will hardly come
here even at the highest rates, and it always ends in your
having to send your own bullocks along to help, so you
have the satisfaction of paying and doing the work your-
self. The Bogan was dry long ago, Bourke and Wilcannia
are nearly deserted, and the Darling is a mere chain of
rapidly diminishing water-holes. Nothing but tales of
woe ail round.
• * * *
“ Insignis ” : lie paying patients in coun-
try hospitals, “ Scotty W.” hits the nail on the
head, but doesn’t drive it home. It is common
for medicoes in a country town to treat a casual
patient until all his cash is expended, and then
send him to the hospital to be boarded, nursed
and doctored at public expense. The average
doctor is generally pretty keen on the bawbees,
and cares not who sinks so long as he comes on top.
I have been a hospital committee-man for years,
and am well up to the miserable tricks of the saw-
bones trade.

“ Walga ” : One aboriginal method of
duck-catching. They spread a net (made from
grass and bird-sinews) across a lagoon, and one
blackfellow remains on guard, provided with a
piece of bark, roughly shaped to resemble a flying
hawk. Others then go up creek and frighten the
ducks. As the birds pass the man on watch he
suddenly rises, and, throwing the piece of bark
into the air, utters a shrill, hawk-like scream.
The ducks imagine that their enemy is after them;
and, swooping down in affright, get their legs
entangled in the net.
THE BUSHVELDT SEDDONEERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thrice a thousand cannibals
Silent men and stark,
We can smell our quarry well,
And track him in the dark—
We, the lion’s next of kin,
We, the tiger’s peers,
Point the foe, and let us go,
The Bushveldt Seddoneers 1
We need no commissariat, -
We make no useless fuss ;
Blood—hot, fresh— and human flesh—
Meat and drink to us !
What are ruth and pity,
What are children’s tears,
Age, or sex, to Richard Rex
And his Seddoneers ?
Club and spear and tomahawk,
These shall teach you plain
How you did with heart of kid
Wage a war in vain.
Plague on silken courtesies 1
Lo, in after years,
Bones and hair shall tell men where
Passed the Seddoneers 1

SCENE FROM "THE RUNAWAY CTRL”
An ex-employee of a Victorian meat-
freezing factory tells this :
It was just on 12 o’clock one Saturday night, and all
but two or three of us had knocked off. The chief
engineer sent me to attend to some little matter in the
freezing-chamber. I went in and pulled the door behind
me. When I went back to the door to get out I found
somebody had screwed it up fast from the outside. I
called out, but was only answered by the echo of my own
voice. I got a hammer that had been left in the chamber
and banged at the door with it. Just then the electric
light was turned off, and I knew that everybody
but the boiler attendant had gone home—he was in
the engine-room, 150 ft. awav, and not at all likely to
visit the chamber. I could not get to him. I realised
that I was a prisoner for an indefinite period in a place
which would be kept at 15 degrees below zero. The
darkness was intense—l could feel it. I had but a few
feet square of clear space near my doorway in which
to move about. I dared not go among the hanging
sheep for fear of losing my way—and my door. I tried
hammering again. No answer. I slapped my arms
round mv shoulders to keep the blood in circulation. I
stamped mv feet; I shouted. I calculated the chances
of reaching a skylight in the roof. No, I couldn’t. Even
if I could, my frame might not go through the opening,
I felt that ‘ I was growing weaker' and knew that
my efforts to attract attention were growing more
hopeless everv moment. After about an hour in that
awful prison I began to calculate how long it would be
before I lost sensibility, and how long after that I should
live. Not more than an hour or two I guessed, and there
was no reasonable hope of any rescue within that time,
I picked up my hammer again and made a few feeble
strokes upon the door. Suddenly it opened. “ Good
God ! What the blazes are you doing here ? ” were the

first words—welcome, if rough—l heard from the boiler-
attendant. I found afterwards that the engineer, in his
haste to get off duty, had closed the door, forgetting all
about having sent me in. Yes, that’s how I came to
leave the job. I wasn’t having any more freezing-chamber,
so had to go. The risks of labor? Yes; but lam going
to take my risks in the open with an axe, or a pick and
shovel, in future.
* * * *
“Piccola” on Australian encouragement
to Indian hawkers :
What’s the use of clamouring for a white Australia
while the piebald variety is encouraged by so many of our
farmers? I’ve stayed at scores of farmhouses in the
course of my wanderings, and have noted the different
treatment meted out to the white hawker and his Indian
rival. The former is invariably looked on as a “ take-
down,” and practically chased off the premises, whereas
the Indian is positively welcomed. He always shakes
hands “ Inglishee ” fashion with the missus, and, after
selling her some tinsel rubbish at fabulous prices, mono-
polises the kitchen-fire and cooking utensils till he has
cooked his meal. I have eaten at a table while a filthy,
foul-smelling Hindu, with a face of villainous cunning,
squatted on his haunches within a yard of me, cooking
his meat and potatoes ; said potatoes, by the way, together
with sugar, milk, and salt, being supplied gratis by the
farmer’s wife. I believe he’d have had a seat at the table
if he would have graciously consented to pollute himself
by eating with the degraded whites.
Another Australian certificate of good
character (given this time to the bushrangers
Kenniff) from a squatter:—
If they borrowed a £lO-note and agreed to pay it back
within a specified time, the pledge would be redeemed
even if they were obliged to go into their creditor’s pad-
dock and steal enough stock to raise the coin.
* * • •
“A.C.” gives an interesting bit of vital
history:—
There is at present living on his farm at Oathcart (Vic.)
a patriarch who has four generations of descendants
living. His eldest daughter went to N. Queensland 27
years ago with a numerous half-grown family. Her
second child (a daughter also) married in Ravenswood
(Q.) and is now at Charters Towers with six sons and four
daughters. The eldest of these sons married last year in
Charters Towers, and has a son, the fifth in direct
descent, counting the old veteran in Victoria, who is still
on the farm which he took up 50 years ago. His children,
grand-children, great-grand-children and g. g. grand-
child run into three figures. This family provides strong
refutation of Dr. Ahearn’s sugar-planter theory that the
white race cannot live and thrive in the tropics, as here
are practically three generations ; and Mrs. Pollard, the
grand-daughter of the patriarch, a Victorian native, was
aged II or 12 years on her arrival in the tropics. Her
son, a tropical native, has married another tropical
native, and the families in this clan will bear comparison
in number with similar families in any temperate climate.
They will also hold their own, both physically and
mentally, despite all the sugar-dividend literature that
may be written with the blood and sweat of unfortunate
nigger slaves.
“BobR.”: Don’t quite
go with “Wallaroo’s”
sweeping condemnation of
the improvidence of the
farmer. Nearly every
N.S.W. farmer is the slave
of a bank or a storekeeper.
The latter mostly has the
crop, and takes good care
that every bag of wheat is
brought to him. Then,
when prices advance, he
can sell the farmer his own
wheat again at a profit—
which is all very well for
the storekeeper, but keeps
the slave’s nose close to the
grindstone. As for “keep-
ing a few sheep,” many
farmers make a bit by buy-
ing store stock off shears,
putting them on stubbles,
and selling them fat on the
approach of winter and
ploughing-time.
•* * *
The proposal to intro-
duce the African eland to
N.S.W. backblocks does
not commend itself to the
sheep-man of the West.
This largest of the antelopes
is bigger than an average
bullock, and has a stomach-
capacity equal to a moder-
ate silo. Now, what would
be the use of such a beast in
the hard and dry West,
except for walking slap
through wire fences. A
15cwt. beast would snap No. 6 wire like cotton,
and his usefulness as a meat-producer would thus
be badly discounted.
* * * *
At Swan Hill (Vic.) once a man rushed
into one of the pubs, yelling—“ whisky—whisky
—quick ! —been —an —-accident to a —young —-
woman!” The whisky was clamped down double-
quick ; whereupon the man seized it, gulped it
off, and exclaimed, with a sigh of relief : “Ah,
that’s better ! I never could bear to see a woman
suffer ! ”

“Bob R.” to The Bulletin :
Tour criticisms of “ my opinion ” and Union matters
are hardly fair : I merely instanced two cases on which the
majority of the men are at variance with the A.W.U.',
and these two also constitute the divergent point between
the new and old unions—viz., raising the price
of shearing and shed work, and increasing amount of
membership fee. On neither of these questions have
the men had a chance of expressing an opinion, so that
the kicking-out of their officers goes for nought; more-
over, it being customary on the part of the A.W.U. to
take a vote amongst the men regarding any proposed
innovation in its rules and constitution, why was it
omitted in the two cases I instance ? Summed up, the
new rules simply represent the opinions of a few
delegates, not those of the men, and they will prove one of
the greatest blunders yet made by the A.W.U.
In South Melbourne court, the other day,
a miserable squabble took place between two
brothers over the coffin of a third. Francis Hall,
who described himself as “ a respectable citizen,”
sued his brother Joseph for £lO 135., the cost of
the third brother’s funeral. In cross-examination
plaintiff told defendant’s counsel that he was no
man to appear for him, and that if he did so in
some countries he would be dragged to pieces.
Then he lapsed into big tears. Later on plaintiff
said his idea was that as defendant gained under
their father’s will he ought to bury the brother.
“If I had had any money left to me,” he re-
marked savagely, “ I would have given my
brother an honorable funeral. Human nature,
sir! Common nature, sir ? ” Then he admitted
that his brother had left him some clothes, and
that he was going to keep them in a box, and let
them lie—“for 20,000 years, if you like,” he
cried. He added that defendant had got some of
the clothes, however, and that he had gone to
Geelong in them. Joseph Hall denied that he
was legally responsible for the debt, but said,
“From a moral point I am. If plaintiff will
handover deceased’s belongings,” he continued,
“ wearing apparel to the value of about £3 to a
poorer member of the family and pay the cost of
this action, I will pay the funeral expenses.”
Plaintiff promptly asked what the costs were, and
on learning that they amounted to £3 3s. he de-
clined his brother’s offer. The case was there-
upon dismissed, and plaintiff left the court in a
rage and saying bitter things about still another
brother.
Dear Bulletin. —ln reply to “Bob R.”
(5/4/’O2) and “Old Stoney” (12/4/’O2), let me say
that their ignorance of everything connected with
the A.W.U.’s policy and internal management is,
to me, sufficient proof that they are not, and pro-
bably never were, of its membership. For
instance, the memtership-fee has not been raised
to £1 per year, but from 12s. Gd. for shearers to
155., and from 7s. 6d. for shed-hands to 10s. The
salaries of branch secretaries have not been raised
by £25 per ahnum. Each branch votes its own
salaries. What the recent conference did was to
fix a maximum. lam certain that neither of
these gentlemen, who so enthusiastically hail the
bogus union, and so gushingly laud the lordly
squatter, ever read the Worker, or ever were in
sympathy with unionism. Their ignorance is too
dense for the one ; their spleen too evident for the
other. Let me say, also, that this alleged new
Shearers’ Union could not have been formed as a
protest against what was done by the late A.W.U.
conference, for the very good reason that its rules
were in type while that body was discussing the
policy for the coming season, —G.B.
Terrified by Apparitions.
A peculiar story concerning fancied apparitions
has been told to the local press by a Miss Viola
Dawson, residing at 156 Alfred-street, North
Sydney. “Four years ago (she relates) I had to
leave school because I imagined that I could see
black faces looking at me wherever I went. My
nerves were so unstrung that St. Vitus’ dance was
induced, and until recently, when Dr. Williams’
pink pills for pale people cured me, I suffered
severely. I was so nervous that I would start at
the slightest sound, my hands and face twitched
involuntarily, I lost my appetite and fell away so
much that I was almost too weak to walk. The
tonics I first tried did not relieve me, but after
taking one boxful of Dr. Williams’ pink pills I
felt greatly improved, and by continuing to take
them I progressed nicely. Three boxfuls made
me a strong, healthy girl, free from all weakness
and nervousness, and I now enjoy the very best
of health.” Indeed, Miss Dawson’s attractive
appearance and fine complexion are worthy
of remark as showing the great value of Dr.
Williams’ pink pills in inducing perfect health.
Being a nerve tonic and a blood builder they act
directly on the blood and nerves—enriching the
blood and freeing it from all impurities, and
making the nerves taut. They cure hysteria,
heart palpitation, dyspepsia, biliousness, anaemia
and debility, rheumatism, neuralgia, skin diseases,
ailments of children, etc. From chemists and
storekeepers and the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co.,
Queen’s Place, Sydney, two and nine per box,
six boxes fifteen and threepence, post free. A
boxful or two of Dr. Williams’ pink pills will, by
strengthening the constitution, free one from all
ordinary risks of plague.
