POLITICAL
POINTS
The colossus of Rhodes (and Bridges)—-
O’Sullivan “Facing the Dawn.”
Mr. W. Martin, president of the Austra-
lian Natives’ Imperial Association, “is understood
to be a dead-bird for a seat in N.S.W. Leg.
Council.” Humph !
Mr. Barton at the Seddon banquet (where
every thought was turned to gore and one could
almost see the joints Weeding afresh):
We mean to stick to the Empire, and the blood that
made it is the blood that will preserve it.
One member of the Reps., during the
tariff discussion, worked very hard for a duty on
Oregon and M.L. pine, with the object of pro-
tecting the Q. timber industry. Then he turned
round and wanted axe-handles on the free list!
The Bowen railway (just about to be
closed down) lost £2577 last year. Cooktown line
(just reprieved) lost £8621 for the same period.
The Mackay line was £3963 short for the same
time, but Mackay sends in two faithful Govern-
ment pillars, and is, consequently, quite safe from
injury.
It seems to be accepted in the press
gallery that the Govt, will have a majority of
two in any crucial division on the tariff in the
Senate. The Labor party bosses the situation,
and, in return for the legislation on black labor, is
pledged to give the Govt, support on the tariff
question.
The Kyabram (Vic.) reformers are eager
for the abolition of the Speakership, Speaker’s
duties to be carried out by the Chairman of Com-
mittees. There is sense in this. The chair is,
after all, only a support for an aspirant to reach
for a K.C.M.G., and Victoria can’t afford £lOOO a
year for that.
South Australia, in 1901, had 5204 less
children under five years than in 1891, and 5548
more ancients over 60 years. N.S.W. is in the
same situation, and so, probably, are some of the
other States whose figures are not available yet.
This swapping of babies for patriarchs is a bad
sign—also a bad bargain.
DE LA RETS REWARD.
(As the Chamberlain Government seems unable to
make a choice of a reward for De la Rey for his
generous treatment of the captured General Metheun,
THE BULLETIN offers a hint.)
KETOHENER : “De la Rey, ice are deeply touched
by your noble humanity, and I am requested
to offer you this small token of Imperial
appreciation—it is the rope with which we
hanged several of your countrymen .”
It is a sinister fact that whenever retrench-
ment is tried in Q. the Education Dept, gets the
first blow —and the last. In ’93 the unfortunate
teachers, north, west, and Central, had lumps
deducted from their miserable pittances, and only
last year got back to normal. Now the Depart-
ment is again being assailed.
Groom, of Queensland, and Hartnoll, of
Tasmania, cost the Commonwealth £1650 for their
election to the House of Reps. ; and Hartnoll,
“the candidate for Adams,” stood the country
£llOO of the sum. That was a lot to pay for
Hartnoll, and should his election be upset, as
threatens, it will be lost after all.
The way of the “ temporary ” civil servant
is going to be hard up North. There is a Publio
Service Act, but members with a “pull” could
always find impecunious friends a job by getting
them “ temporarily” into the various easy-working
departments at 10s. a day. The Black Wednesday
of the “ temporaries” is coming at last.
Premier Seddon claims to be the author
of the Imperial trade-preference scheme which he
trumpets so blatantly. As a matter of fact, he
prigged it from B. R. Wise. It is a characteristi-
cally Wise kind of measure, subtly designed to
evade the spirit, while keeping strictly to the
letter, of England’s commercial treaties with
foreign rivals.
Henry Copeland gets an assistant named
White at £5OO a year to take secretary Yardley’s
place in N.S.W. Agency-General. On Mr.
Yardley’s death, Premier See stated distinctly
that the appointment of a substitute was not
contemplated, and it was said also that the office
would be reorganised, and Australians appointed
to future posts.
As to the chances of peace in S’Africa, it
is an unhappy omen that the proposals should be
made in March. It was in March, 1900, after the
battle of Paardeberg, that the first peace negotia-
tions were started. In March, 1901, Ketchener
met Botha in fruitless conference. In March,
1902, the third negotiations opened. Is there
any special reason for discussing peace in March ?
The High Commissionership is not to be
filled until Mr. Barton returns from London.
Bigjohn Forrest is clamorously eager for the
billet (and if refused he might try and be nasty
when another member of the Cabinet wants to be
“ translated ”), but Mr. Barton thinks that, if he
takes Mr. Forrest to London, he’ll be able to
frighten him off asking for position by pointing
out its difficulties. Vain hope. Bigjohn is not a
modest man.
Some members of Vic. legislature seriously
contemplate the introduction of a Bill as the
corollary of the reduction-of-screws movement,
to reduce the expense of being M.P. This
measure would make it bribery and corruption
for any member to donate money to a sports club,
or any other institution except the local hospitals
or benevolent asylums during his official term.
It would also strictly limit election expenditure.
If these two reforms are effected, most members
will be quite willing to have their Parliamentary
dole cut down.
The N.S.W. Government programme for
the new session is officially announced as consist-
ing of an Audit Bill and “amend-
ments” of the liquor law, old-age-
pension law, and land and income
tax. This is so vague that it pro-
bably means nothing more than a
colorless and hazy tinkering with
small details. If amending the liquor
law means abolishing tied houses or
establishing a State Drink monopoly,
or if the patching of the land-tax law
means so graduating it that it will
fall with special weight on owners
of large estates, absentees, and
especially on the growing squire
class which doesn’t cultivate its own
land, but lets it out to rack-rented
tenants, it will be real progress. If
it means an extra-careful dotting of
the “i” that was overlooked in the
original Act it means nothing in par-
ticular. There is not a word about
reforming or abolishing the Upper
House or reducing the Assembly.
As regards the annexation of the
rents of the Sydney resumed area as
revenue, and paying the correspond-
ing interest out of loans, there is the
deadest of dead silences. But it is
vaguely explained that the amend-
ment of the Old Age Pension system
is in the direction of rigid cutting-
down of expense—while there are old
Boers to be killed the keeping of old
Australians alive has to be done as
cheaply as possible.
English Roman Catholics, it is
cabled, are petitioning the English
Government to take the control of
church property in England out of
the hands of the Papacy, and to
claim a veto on the appointment of
Irish R.C. bishops (presumably
with a view to discouraging any
tendencies to Fenianism in the
episcopacy). But these petitioners are regarded
by the Irish R.C.’s not as Catholics but as Car-
tholics—English Chrtholics; and the withering
contempt of that Car is something that cold
print can’t express.
Sydney D.T. growls viciouslv at any pro-
posal to increase the allowances of Federal Parlia-
mentarians. That syndicate organ would like to
see payment of members abolished together.
Premier Peacock answers the reform agi-
tation in Victoria with a sullen declaration that
it would be money in his pocket if he were out of
politics. Possibly, but does the vain bird mean to
say that he does not owe much of his success in busi-
ness to his luck in politics ? Peacock the Minister
and Premier has been a big advt. for Peacock the
legal manager.
“AND SO SAT ALL OF US”
JOHNNY: “ What's a capital offence, gran.?”
GRAN. (Mayor of Croabungalong): “ Why, if
they don’t fix the site of the Federal City at
Croabungalong that’ll be a capital offence, and
don’t you forget it, my boy.”
Works-Minister O’Sullivan has decided
that in N.S.W.
All communications addressed to the Department of
Works are to be replied to through the members con-
cerned, whether presented by them or not.
What hope is there of Local Government when
the roads-and-bridges influence is thus deliberately
fostered ?
In his alleged history of the Boer war,
Conan Doyle says:
A brave race can forget the victims of the field of battle,
but never those of the scaffold. The making of political
martyrs is the last insanity of statesmanship.
True, and it applies with as much force to
Scheepers, Lotter, and other victims of military
courts as to the victims of Slachter’s Nek. But it
is a lot easier to acknowledge our grandfathers’
wrong-doings than our own.
It is wonderful how little makes an
“outrage” in Ireland as compared with other
countries. Last Monday it was cabled all the
way out here that the tails of ten cattle had been
cutoff in Galway. That was called “agrarian
outrage ! ” Think of sending such poor trash to a
country where certain cattle-stealers have just
burned a squatter and a policeman and hung the
bags containing their bones on a horse, and turned
the walking sepulchre loose !
“ Aurora ” to The Bulletin :
What is Lyne’s game ? Speaking on the Supply Bill, in
the Heps., he said he refused to be bound to take junior
officers from State departments exclusively, and justified
his appointment of a junior clerk by saying that the clerk
had been temporarily employed for some months in the
Dept, of Home Affairs. S’William forgot to mention
another junior he has appointed, and who has not been
temporarily employed in the Dept. Perhaps he thought
latter not worth mentioning. Said junior has only left
school a few months, and had been running messages for
a local bank in S’William’s electorate. Now he is a junior
at £lOO to start! Father is a “ big ” man and supports
S’William. How many has he really appointed ?
S.A. Opposition-leader John Darling con-
demns the Govt, for offering the Northern Terri-
tory to the Federation for the amount of its public
debt (£2,500,000). “Why,” he declares, “even
with the handicap of the Alien Immigration Act”
(that’s the sort of man he is), “ I would be willing
to form one of a syndicate to offer £3,500,000 for
the Territory.” Also, he would do all in his power
to engineer a land-grant railway across the con-
tinent. He considers that at least 10 millions
should have been asked from the Federation, as
that would have left a margin for beating down.
When the deputationists of the “ Reform ”
Conference waited on Premier Peacock with their
resolutions about reducing the cost of governing
Victoria, that thin and futile man, instead of
telling them certain healthy and essential truths
about themselves, begged them not to cast
aspersions on the management of the country lest
it should have a bad effect on future loans.
Australia is so pathetic a slave to the foreign
Cohen nowadays that it can’t even talk of its own
affairs above a whisper less Moses or Levi should
have his ear at the keyhole.
N.S.W. Premier See garrulously reveals
that he always consults the members of his
Cabinet and does not believe in despotically con-
trolling affairs. Probably a very prudent, business-
like habit in Mr. See’s circumstances. When
S. H. Parker was Minister in the Forrest (W.A.)
Government he thus, in a bold, free hand, con-
veyed his sentiments to his chief :
You appear determined to treat your fellow-ministers,
not as colleagues but as subordinates, a position which I
certainly refuse to occupy.
How a Premier and a Cabinet may work together
depends wholly on the kind of man the Premier
is—and the kind of men his Ministers are.
“The Country Party,” or Cocky Party,
which has been formed in N.S.W. to counteract
the influence of the Labor Party, hasn’t got the
courage of its Toryism. It represents itself as
being mildly in favor of everything the L.P.
Strongly favors : for instance, it believes in day-
labor—a cocky day, presumably, beginning at 3
a.m. and ending at 11.45 p.m. The Country
Party doesn’t declare against a mad borrowing
policy, only suggests that the madness shouldn’t
rage beyond £2,000,000 a year. It is altogether a
weak and spineless affair and fitted only to be a
municipal council somewhere.
A series of indignation meetings is being
held in Victoria to demand reform of the
fcitate Parliament—that reform on which
Peacock and Co. backed down a year or
so ago. When the present Vic. Assembly
was elected it was divided into two parties.
Both agreed that the legislative machine
was now absurdly cumbrous, but one section
advocated a popularly-elected convention
to draft a new State constitution—the only
idea likely to meet the case—while the
other wanted Parliament to reform itself.
When Parliament met, the Convention
party, which included the Peacock Minis-
try, dropped its scheme; and the party
that wanted Parliament to reform itself,
which included the whole Upper House,
was never heard of. In view of next year’s
election the country begins to inquire after
these lost parties. When both parties in &
Legislature get lost it is time to inquire.
The Philp (Q.) Ministry is really pitiful
in its paltriness. Labor-member Bowman was
ousted by Leahy, brother of the Minister for
Railways, from the Warrego seat. Acting under
a misapprehension as to the currency of his
Parliamentary pass, Bowman scrupulously and
honestly paid his fare back to Brisbane, and then
learnt that he was entitled to use the free pass
back to the capital. So he applied for a refund,
and this was refused by the Minuter on the
ground that—
He would be extremely sorry to take *way from the
ex-Labor member the privilege to pose as a hero who had
gained so much kudos and applause for his self-sacrifice
on the altar of principle.
The lone remnant of*"' the Victorian
Freetraders got together in Melbourne the other
day, and not being able to find a local shoddyist
of any standing to talk to them they were
harangued by Symon, the S.A. lawyer, and
M'Millan, the Sydney agent for an English
exporting firm. The latter told them that
“ Freetraders had only to organise themselves to
win all over Victoria,” and the audience tried to
look as if it believed this. But the Freetraders
have been for years about the best organised party
in Victoria, and have never won anything. Even
the most careful organising of two foreign im-
porters and a dog won’t make them count for
more at the polls than 50,000 disorganised
Protectionists.
Wellington Post, under the heading of
“ An Amazing Falsehood,” deals with Seddon’a
denial of his “ Dahomey ” speech :
If this bloodthirsty speech was never uttered, how
came it that it was published in the colony without a
word of question or contradiction ? For the simple
reason that it was true. Its author dared not resort to a
denial. He knew that it would only cover him with
shame and confusion. When he arrived in Sydney, how-
ever, he doubtless became unpleasantly aware that his
appeal to the savage instincts of the native race had been
there made the subject of strong and adverse comment.
He had captured the Maori vote for next general election,
but he had forgotten that the shameful means employed
might become known to the outside world. The dis-
credit of it must be removed. What more certain and
easy than an absolute denial ? But the denial was as
base as it was mendacious.
The true “strength” of what is locally
known as the ‘ ‘ Kyabram ” movement for Vic.
Parliamentary “reform” may be gauged by the
force behind it. Both Argus and Age barrack
for all they are worth ; neither will insert any
letters adversely critical; in fact, all opposition is
stifled. Reduction is wanted, but to reduce the
Assembly from 95 to 46 and the Council in pro-
portion means more than doubling the present
electorates. This would give Fatman an even
bigger advantage than at present—especially if
salaries be reduced to £2OO a-year—and destroy
almost all hope of ever reforming that costly
obstruction, the House of Landlords. A single-
House of 60 members would cost less, do more-
work, and would be nearer to government by the
people, not by David Syme.
The general elections throughout S.A., for
both Council and Assembly, take place this Satur-
day, when all 18 of the members of the Upper
House will be returned by four districts, and 42
representatives will be chosen for the other
Chamber by 13 constituencies. Unfortunately no
pretence is being made by the- Government
towards any scheme of reform, either consti-
tutional, social, industrial or financial, and so the
splendid opportunity of the double dissolution
(which has coma merely as an incident of the
reduction in the strength of the Legislature) will
be lost. Kingston wrecked his long-lived Minis-
try in an attempt to force the Council to the
people, and, now the event for which he strove so
hard has happened, there is absolutely no leader
of Liberal thought in the State far-sighted enough
or sufficiently bold to take advantage of it. And
the press is as flaccid as the politicians. Where-
fore a Conservative reaction is threatening.
From Adelaide :
Perhaps never before in Australian political history
was there quite such a subordination of principles to-
expediency as is now apparent in the contest for Torrens
(Adelaide suburban) in S.A. Assembly. Premier Jenkins
was once a colleague of Kingston and professes to be a
Liberal. Opposition leader Darling is a hard-crusted Tory
of the most rigid commercial type and glories in his
antagonism to everything that savors of healthy socialism.
He is endeavoring to carry in with him a weak reflection
of his views in architect Soward. Jenkins, however, is so
anxious to secure place and pay again that he has con-
cluded an alliance with Darling’s committee by which he
promises them the votes of his supporters in return
for their help. There are five seats for Torrens, and
the other candidates include Tom Price, leader of Labor
party, Coneybeer and Dankel, the Labor nominees,
Maekie (Mayor of Unley and sec. of the Commercial
Travellers’ Association) who is a good Liberal, and Craw-
ford Vaughan, a Single-Taxer. None of these, however, is-
receiving the slightest help from Jenkins, who has thrown
himself wholly into the arms of the enemy, and who yet
wonders why the Labor party is so bitter against him,
while the Liberals gaze mournfully at his desertion of
their ranks. Some vigorous personality like that of
Kingston is urgently needed in S.A. just now to
strengthen the weak hands and confirm the feeble knees
of his olden party, which now wanders as a flock without
a shepherd, a prey to ravening wolves in sheep’s clothing
of the Jenkins type, who care nothing for conscience or
consistency as compared with the fleshpots on the
Treasury benches. Unfortunately the Labor party is in
very incompetent hands.

THE PIANOLA
You have
heard the
Name , but
I
Have you
heard the
Instrument?
The PIANOLA
is a piano-player,
or rather it does
that part of the
playing which the
fingers do in or-
dinary hand-play-
ing—it strikes the
The Pianola in use with Upright Piano.
When you desire
to play a selec-
tion outside of
your repertory,
you roll it into
position again, so
that its felt-cov-
ered fingers rest
notes, while the musical taste of
the player (in either case) is re-
sponsible for the expression. Both
“touch” and “tempo” are regu-
lated by him, thereby portraying
his musical personality in the
result.
It is this human effect which
has astounded the musical world,
and which is not fully understood,
except by those that have heard it.
As shown in the block, the
Pianola looks like a small cabinet.
When you wish to play the piano
by hand, the Pianola may be
rolled to another part of the room.
upon the keys of the piano and
insert the roll of music to be
played. By the use of the ex-
pression-levers you render the
selection according to your own
interpretation of the composer’s
meaning, or to suit your particular
mood.
The operation of the Pianola is
simple, requires no musical know-
ledge on the part of the player,
and yet there are no limitations to
the artistic effects obtainable.
Can be purchased on the hire
system if desired.
The iEolian Co. of New York.

SYDNEY.
“Jimmy” Thomson, erstwhile of Melb.
Evening Standard, announces the issue of a new
daily evening paper—the Fremantle (W.A.)
Courier—for Ist May. He does not intend to
launch out on such a scale as when he promoted
the Standard, but he reckons on building up a
good property, as there is no other paper in
Fremantle—now credited with a population of
nearly 30,000. The new concern is a limited co.,
and has capital behind it. Jimmy had a lot of
bad luck on the Murchison goldfields, which were
oo poor to keep a goat alive, and is now due for a
hange of fortune.
, r . . , , „ n •
feature of last Eight Hours Day m
Melbourne was the number of lost infants lodged
at the watch-house. One burly Bourke-st. police-
man came to the place wrestling desperately with
a three-year-old kiddy. The Lavv had the waif in
its arms, and the waif was howling like a small
fiend. One fist was full of the whiskers of the
Law, and a soddened ginger-bread horse in the
other furiously battered the face of the Force. A
watching crowd was convulsed with rapture, and
sent up a yell: “ Assault an’ battery, an’ obsthruct-
in’ the police in the ixicution av their juty ! ” and
it was a most distracted officer who finally reached
shelter.
Clerical clown Geil (appropriately pro-
nounced Guile) grand boss of the Melbourne
Simultaneous Mission, told a Town Hall audience,
t’other day, that a leading American infidel, also
a military man, had disgraced himself during the
Civil war by bolting the first time he was under
fire. Geil ingeniously added that he would not
defile his mouth by mentioning the man’s name—
but left the impression in everybody’s mouth that
the poltroon was Ingersoll. Now, no such inci-
dent was ever related about Ingersoll before,
though the orthodox platform arid press all over
the world strove hard during his whole life to dis-
credit him. Moreover, such an action is wholly at
variance with every other detail of Ingersoll’s
fearless and self-sacrificing career; while, finally,
it is extremely unlikely that a lieutenant who had
exhibited gross cowardice on the battle-field would
ever have attained the rank of colonel. Of course,
had anybody arisen and contradicted Mr. Geil, it
would have been easy to say that the reference
was not intended for Ingersoll but for another
man whose name could not possibly be revealed.
Anyhow, the incident shows the tactics that the
legions of piety are forced to adopt. And after all,
what does it show if an infidel did run away?
Frederick the Great did the same thing in his
first engagement.
How to File up the Centuries.
This is a feat best known to cricketers, and of
all cricketers probably those of Australia most
frequently perform the trick. But the best cen-
turies are those which represent years of life. For
a man or woman to become a centenarian is in
itself Nature’s own certificate of a well-spent life
no less than of a sound constitution. Of this, the
better kind of century, Australia has also had a
few notable examples, and we will hazard the
prophecy that she will have yet others. Mrs.
Elizabeth Fuller, of No. 44, Ryder-street, Surry
Hills, Sydney, N.S.W., now 83 years of age, is
still hale and hearty, and, we believe, good for a
further indefinite term of life.
Anybody who lives to the age of 71, as Mrs.
Fuller did, without knowing what it was to be
seriously ill, and then to beoome ill only as the
result of accident, has something substantial to
boast of. “ I was 71,” says Mrs. Fuller, “ when I
lost my footing while ascending the stairs, sus-
taining a fractured arm and broken collar bone.
I became an inmate of the Sydney Hospital for
two months and was discharged as cured. My
worst trouble, however, was now about to begin.
In my fall I must have injured the kidneys, for a
dull continuous pain set in in that region which
gave me no rest by day or night. Obstinate
constipation and urinary difficulties were soon
added to my troubles, arising, I believe, from the
same cause. I visited the leading hospitals as an
out-patient, and was also under treatment at my
own home by a lady doctor; but none of the
plasters, liniments, lotions, or other medicines
they gave me, proved of the least use. As time
passed the pain in my back increased so much
that the agony was intolerable, and I felt as if I
could have welcomed death. Such was my con-
dition for two years, when my daughter, Mrs. W.
Johnson, of Arden-street, Waverley, gave me
three of Mother Seigel’s Soothing Plasters, a box
of Seigel’s Operative Pills, and a bottle of Seigel’s
Curative Syrup. The plasters, used in conjunction
with the Syrup and Pills, at once reduced the
backache, and the bowels and other internal
organs, were soon brought into proper working
order. I could eat, sleep, and get about again,
and, in three months, was as well as a woman of
my years could hope to be.”
For many years physicians were of opinion that,
when the human body had reached the age of
three score and ten, it was no longer responsive to
even the most appropriate remedies. That opinion
is now no longer held, and the case of Mrs. Fuller
again disproves it.

HAIL TO MEN!
y
A
Even unto old age you may feel the
vigor of youth, with its light heart,
elastic step, courage and
tireless energy. You may be
free from pains and aches,
and defy your years.
There is a fountain of perpetual yonth, and you have
only to reach out your hand and take it. You can drink of
it until your heart shouts with gladness, and with all your
might you will proclaim, as other men have—
“ I AM A MAN 1”
Like the giants of old, you can be in your prime at 60
—strong, vigorous, and full of youthful enthusiasm.
Congested Kidneys, Sciatica.
Mountinghouse Steed, Martinville, N.S.W.,
March 28th, 1902.
the dr. McLaughlin company.
Dear Sirs, —It gives me great pleasure to state, in answer to your letter, that my health is
very much improved since I have been wearing the Electric Belt I got from you. The very
second time I put it on I could feel the benefits immediately, and I have been only wearing it
six weeks to-night. I feel very much better all over my body, and whereas it was a misery before
to sit down or walk before I got the Belt, now it is just a pleasure. The day I left my home to
see you I had no life in ine, and felt as though life was a burden to me, and, as it was, I was
simply a “ walking misery.” But I thank God I came to you for the Belt, and I cannot tell you
how glad I am, for I feel so much better, especially .with the pains in my legs ; in fact, I am
better all over, and it is just wonderful. You can do what you like with this letter, and if it will
be of any benefit to let my fellow-sufferers know, I will be only too pleased for them to write to me,
when I will be able to tell them how bad I really was before I got the Belt. lam telling my own
personal friends all about the wonderful cure, and I do not want to make any secret of it at all.
I am, yours very gratefully,
(Signed) WILLIAM CLOUTEN.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Smallest field (7) of the day lined up for Warwick
Handicap, Saturday, though 2 to 1 more money induce-
ment was offered than that in connection with any other
of the six events. Dottswood was favorite at “ two’s,”
but winner turned up in the syr.-old Abercorn-Ruskinite
horse Ruskin, who easily won right through the 10fur.,
and gave most turf-followers a big knock. When ridden
to the front in other races he generally fell back before
the journey’s close. Several hundreds to 10 and 12 were
taken by those posted, but Mo. garnered a heap of bets
through Dottswood, Clangour, Nahpro and Spring Rose,
all of whom had troops of supporters at shorter prices
than the ultimate winner.
* * • *
Plain Tom 11. and Alannah were served up
uncommonly warm for Warwick Farm Flying Stakes,
though, through known connection between their respec-
tive owners, cutest punters wouldn’t swallow that each
was a legitimate trier. Loudly-voiced good thing, Rose
Petal, of recent A. J.O. and City Tatt’s meetings, was most
in request, and she won nicely from Alannah and
Plain Tom as named. Rose Petal is by Grand Flaneur
from Rosecleer, by Trenton. S.T.O. Nursery Handicap
fell to her, but since then she finished nowhere in first
and second A.J.O. Nurseries, and though weightily
backed, failed to fill a place in City Tatt’s Youthful
Stakes. This filly is unquestionably speedy, but un-
reliable, and plays up too much at the post to encourage
investments.
* * * •
Grafnell, the promising winner of Warwick
Farm Pace Welter, will always be worth an investment
whenever the right money comes. Though he failed to
show among the first seven in Novice Handicap at City
Tatt.’s Randwick meet a fortnight before, he was, last
Saturday, backed down to “evens” in a field of 13, and
won as if out for an exercise spin. He is a great raking
three-year-old gelding by Grafton from Little Nell.
Trainer “ Billy ” Frost has only just come back to the
game after 12 months’ suspension. The luck of it.
• • • *
Marvellous what a difference two or three days
make in the form of Sydney nags. Saturday The Persian
and Burraneer were hopelessly beaten at Canterbury,
and yet, four days later, former gathered in Welter Han-
dicap and latter the principal race of the programme at
Newcastle. The Persian went out at evens, and Burra-
neer 5 to 1 against.
* * * '■ *
S. Mo. reports best biz. for years over Good-
wood Handicap; Cup. Handicapper Hughes’ well-
judged weighting has brought much backing for many
nags. About a dozen in the Goodwood have been coupled
with everything having a chance for the Cup. Looks as
if Trochen or Kingston should get away with the first,
though there are others who’ll surely make the finish
interesting. Field for the Cup doesn’t read as strong as
that for the shorter race. Flagship stands out despite
his recent failure at Moonee Valley, which is explained by
the fact that he suffered the heavy handicap of running
on the outside all the way round a cramped course—a big
disadvantage. Another thing in his favor as against then
is that he will hump 7.12 instead of 9.6. Idler and Sport
Royal are other candidates with good prospects.
» * * •
Winner (Glenloth, by Glenmarkie—The Gem) of
Melbourne Cup in ’92 dropped dead at Tarpeena (Vic.)
last week through a ruptured blood-vessel in the head.
Glenloth had been at the stud for years, and was being
prepared to run at Oasterton meeting. His appropriation
of Melb. Cup will not be soon forgotten ; he won literally
floundering in mud, and did the ring such a huge turn
that the smile of satisfaction at the result never left Mo’s
classic mug for months. Latest owner (Mr. H. M. Egan)
is said to have refused £l5O for him only just before.
• * ♦ *
Oudeis mare Ross, winner of Wagga Cup last
week, has paid for her oats for many a day ahead. Last
season she ran 30 times at N.S.W. country meetings and
annexed Murrumbidgee Riverina Plate (8.6), Wagga
Boxing Day Handicap (9.12), Adelong Farewell Handicap
(9.5), Tumberumba Handicap (9.5) and Forced Handicap
(9.5), Gundagai R.C. Handicap (8.9) and Town Plate (9.6),
Tumut Town Plate (9.7), Junee Clarendon Handicap (9.5),
Murrumbidgee Town Plate (9.6), Lockhart Handicap (9.8),
and Albury Citizens’ Handicap (9.7). Among other wins
since, Junee Farewell Handicap (8.8), Wodonga Handicap
(9.2) and Flying Handicap (9.3), and Wagga Publicans’
Purse (9.5) have been added to the list. Wagga Cup was,
years ago, worth £lOOO to the winner.
* * • *
A woman lately applied to drive her own neddy
in a bracelet event at a Christchurch (M.L.) trotting
meet, and the committee-men were wondering what to do
about it, when someone unearthed a regulation that
drivers had to wear white pants and top-boots. Then
they declined the application.
* * * *
Bataillous —Miss Newbold Byr. old nag Boscobel
gathered in the big event (April Hep., 11m.) at Brisbane
latt.’s meet. Despite that at latter end of season before
last there was talk of withdrawing him from racing,
Boscobel has, during past eight months, figured in 30
races and won 10.
• • * •
M.L. nag Menschikoff abandons racing for
stud duty at Wellington Park. In 18 starts he got home
14 times —10 as a 2yr. old, and at 3yrs. the money fell to
him every time he started.
* • • *
T. (after a well-known Sydney solicitor and
M.L.0.) apparently carries something of the curse nature,
as, when there’s little or no demand, he runs well enough,
then, next time, punters tumble over each other to get
money at any price, and he finishes second or third—
second mostly. Saturday was no exception. On what
they saw at Canterbury, a week previously, guns, touts,
and backers generally went bald-headed for T.M.S. in
Warwick Farm Stewards’ Mile, sending him out hot with
“ two’s,” the best available, but he could get no nearer
than a not-by-any-means meritorious second to the well-
bred Light Artillery-Lady Lovelace 3-yr.-old filly Fanfare,
who surprised most people.
• * * •
A generous M.L. person, who drew a big prize
in “ Tatt’s.” a while back, was asked by a priest for a
donation towards building an inconsequential country
church, and he wrote a cheque for the whole job. The
remainder of his cash he boozed away and went to utter
ruin. The priest got him three billets, but he lost them
all and, in despair, went to the sorrowful father and
implored one more chance. The cleric gave him a severe
lecture, a draft for £l5, got him a billet on board a liner,
and shipped him off to the Coronation.
The dual-dividend system is proving a facer for
the crowd of bookies who compete with the machine in
M. L. They have lost grasp of a stack of coin since its
initiation, and as some hot, straight-out coups have been
landed since Xmas., the bigger men have a bad pain
under their weskit. Some of the badly stricken gentry
lowered the “ limit,” and decided not to lay over a certain
amount each fancied neddy. But far-off, big punters,
whose names are as good as gold, have been wickedly
flattening out this little scheme by delaying sending their
wires until it was too late for poor bookie to reply that
he couldn’t do it. It’s time the public had a look in,
anyway, and it has a better show of handling Ikey’s money
under the new system than previously.
» * •
In re M.L. turfite G. C. Stead. His £1825
haul at recent Canterbury J.O. Autumn meet brings his
spoil in stake-money for this season alone to £11,795 (a
record for the colony), and judging by prospects there’s a
chance of further loot. He bought Menschikoff as a
yearling for 210g5., garnered £6BOO in stakes with him,
and then sold the gold-mine to the nag’s breeder for
600g8. Canterbury (M.L.) J.O. Champagne Stakes have
fallen to Mr. Stead every year for the past 10.
* * * »
Threat of Federal P.M.G. to “catalogue and
gazette” names of advertising Mo’s as persons to whom
letters may not be delivered has materially affected the
income of the sporting press, as, for fear of being
specially named, Mo has ceased advertising. Meanwhile
his business prospers as merrily as ever, and even
“Tatt.’s” sweeps show little falling off, for the latest
closed with 25,000 subscribers—particularly good for a
minor event.
* * * »
While the Federal Govt, was suppressing
“ Tattersall,” Gov.-General Hopetoun’s patronage to a
huge lottery drawn on the 28th was openly advertised.
• * * *
Australian all-round swimmer Dick Cavill
goes London wards by the German boat Weimar this
Saturday to take on the best of the ’Ome product.
Cavill carries much higher credentials than Read, who
preceded him by a month or so, and whom he (Cavill)
easily accounted for whenever they met during the con-
tinent’s Championship Carnival at Wanganui (M.L.).
Unfortunately, through Read’s friends having canvassed
exhaustively before, the Cavill expenses fund won’t
exceed £7O, which is much short of what is necessary,
but “Dick” insists upon going, though friends
foretell he’ll fall below doing himself justice—
deficient in funds, without friends, and handicapped by
his possibly unchecked boyish tendency to treat things
anything but seriously and get outside all sweets, ginger-
beer and such like, in sight. With a shrewd, well-
balanced, well-fixed (financially) man behind him Cavill
would have big possibilities ahead, but as things are the
outlook isn’t too assuring, though it might result dif-
ferently.
* * • •
Hoped now that N.S.W. crew for inter-State
8-oar race in Adelaide Saturday next week will show
better than any of years past. They’ve come on a great
deal lately, and dip and lift in unison, catch crisply, and
work shoulders and legs very satisfactorily. Unfortuna-
tely coach Brotherton, who has brought his charges up to
their present pitch of excellence, couldn’t leave with them
Tuesday and will not be over for the race, which means a
big loss, as had he been able to stay to the end further
improvement was certain. Victorians will take a lot
of beating. Their catch is even and leg-work near
perfection though ’tis complained that bow side
is weak compared with stroke. Melbourne row-
ing men generally are very confident. Bananalanders
are still, at time of writing, as originally chosen, men
who dropped out having come back again, rendering the
combination the best possible. Their stroke is short and
hard with a strong kick in the leg work—much more
American than English. This manner of doing the thing
isn’t the least bit favored among Southern State
rowers, though Northerners swear by its efficacy. Wes-
tralians have chopped and changed so much that few of
the first eight selected will row. South Australians are
locally considered a capital team likely to put up a great
race. Interesting that Vic., Q., and W. A. teams will all be
boated by the Melbourne firm of builders, Messrs.
Edwards and Sons.
» * * »
“Brumby”: A well-known N.S.W. boat pro-
prietor, once a prominent oarsman, on one occasion
caused a deluge of blasphemy amongst the cunning
crowd who called him “ friend.” In a match “ arranged”
between him and a lesser light he was, as usual, made a
hot favorite by the public, while the cognoscenti went
around with a vacant expression, taking the odds. The
“ finish ” led past a concourse of the Uppah Ten who had
all plunged more or less on the favorite ; and, as the
latter came opposite them, hanging on to his opponent,
but seemingly out of it and falling behind, there came a
roar from the naicest barrackers : “ Bravo, Mr. Blank ” ;
“You’ve got him”; “Go on, Neddy.” The wise ones
sniggered, but suddenly, in the midst of the clamour,
Neddy’s head went down and in a magnificent spurt he
led past the post by a couple of lengths. Those yells had
roused Neddy's sporting-blood; and, as he afterwards
explained to his abusive clique, “ he ’ad to do it or bust.”
What a ray of sunshine for the poor punter if something
of this kind were to occasionally occur at Sydney pony-
meetings 1
• * * •
The Continent’s top rung amateur sculler J. J.
Daley is in good fettle for the Commonwealth champion
sculls at Adelaide, Saturday next, when he’ll probably
meet Queenslander Slack and Tasmanian Bourke who
gave him a close call in Sydney a month or two since.
* * * *
A peculiar happening at Sydney Cricket
Ground t’other day: Sydney first and Sydney second
(batting on different wickets) notched 100 at exactly the
same time, and were together at 152 and 183.
» * • *
The present Australian XI. is nicely balanced
in the matter of age. In 1899, all the 14 were 30 or there-
abouts, except Worrall (who was more), and Noble, Hill,
and Trumper. This time, there are six old stagers, 32
this year, or over, and five 25 or under, with only Noble,
Hopkins and Saunders to bridge the gap. The two
last-named have had their chance comparatively
late—at 27. Armstrong is the junior of the team, but he
is not so young as Hill and Trumper were last time (to
say nothing of what Hill was the time before). Trumble
and Kelly are easily the seniors, as both will be 35 their
first month in England ; but there is nobody as old as
Worrall was in 1899, or Giffen in 1896. In fact, 12 years
covers the lot.
* * • •
Bananaland abo. bowler Henry compares un-
favorably in appearance with Marsh, being the ordinary
lean, lank, and unbeautiful native. Marsh, nattily put
together, johnny-like-attired, uncommonly intelligent, is
rather good-looking. For half-dozen or so overs Henry
drives the leather faster than Jones ever did, and pro-
mises much with proper guiding. In the field he works
capitally, but pumps himself out early through wanting
to do the work of the other nine.
* * » •
Writes “ Scrip ” : The scheme projected a while
back to send a M.L. Rugby team to England can’t be
carried out, as the conditions under which they’d play
would make them pros. Rugby is a strictly amateur
game in M.L., and men would have to be paid for the
trip. Well, I can’t see why M L. Rugby Union should be
so very conservative in the matter ; an exception ought
to be made in such cases on the broad ground that it’s in
the interests of the game. ’Ome-Australasian matches
should be encouraged just as is the great cricket curse.
Englishers could be induced to repay visits. We see far
too little English Rugby in Australasia. To my mind,
we’d be all the better for more of it. So far as amateurism
is concerned—well, I’ve long ago given up consideration
of the subject. The line of demarcation seems to be about
100yds. broad.
* * • *
Concluding tussle of Sydney bowling Pennant
matches on Ohatswood green this Saturday should result
interestingly and be as exciting as bowls can be. Teams left
in (City and Petersham) vanquished all others without
going under once. Conditions called for two defeats
before any club dropped out, hence City and Petersham
had to meet twice, or thrice if necessary. Three weeks
ago City fairly romped home and were so certain of
victory again last Saturday that they had the photogra-
pher of a Sydney weekly on the green “ taking ” them as
the absolute winners. They delayed so much in posing that
t’other side was on the point of claiming the match
through the opposition not being up to time. City were
very much second from the start and went under by 81,
losing in two rinks and tieing in a third. Sydney bowling
hasn’t for long known such a set back, and fact of losers
being so aggressively cock sure of success rendered their
downfall very palatable to bowlerdom generally.
* * • *
Sydney lightweights are apparently suffering a
veritable M’Gowan scare. Though they’ll go into a ring,
ostensibly to fight, only the ropes prevent them getting
out into the street when Mac. lays his ears back and
rushes. Deep-chested, powerfully shouldered, sturdily
underpinned, and with the biceps and forearms of a 6ft.
weight-lifter, M'Gowan’s appearance is enough to win
fights. His latest victim, ex-Broken Hill miner Mat
Mitchie, stripped, trained to the hour, and looked every
way the man for the job, but, though a rough, forceful
fighter, and apparently bubbling over with grit ordinarily,
he got the shakes in front of M'Gowan, and laid himself
out to keep as far off the chances of harm as possible.
Mitchie fighting on that occasion and Mitchie fighting
with Irishman Denny Maloney, recently, were widely
different people. Difficult to say who of Australia’s 10
stoners will stand up with M'Gowan, unless Bob Turner,
and, though he thrives on punishment, it’s odds on him
going under inside, perhaps, a dozen rounds. M'Gowan
has been fighting as often as opportunity allowed for 13
years, is a twice-married man, and the father of two
families, and yet looks no more than a boy now.
* * * *
Westralian Dunn and Sydney pug “Bull”
Nicholls struggled through a bitter bout at Sydney Golden
Gate A. 0., Saturday night. Dunn showed more cleverly than
Nicholls, but latter seemed ironclad, for Dunn’s punches
only made him snort and come in head-down for more,
till an opening showed, when Dunn winced from some
shattering thumps about the ribs. Frequently
Nicholls wobbled and staggered, and the Westralian
would operate to finish, but Newtown’s champion kept
going. Spite of furious onslaughts and damaging two-
arm work he stayed, and a full house howled
delightedly. In the last few rounds Nicholls petered out
some, but Dunn kept scoring tellingly, though he couldn’t
find a weak spot for a knock-out punch. The opening of
the ninth 3min., found Nicholls in dire trouble, his
“corner” elevated the “wipe,” and the crowd roared
applause for a fight that was a fight, and not a boxing
match. Nicholls, especially, did the rough-and-tumble
act all the time, hence, probably, “ Bull.”
• • * *
Marvellous how erstwhile clever Australian
middleweight Jim Hall dodges death. Years ago he was
stabbed by a sailor during a “rough-up” in a Woolloo-
mooloo pub, twice he was “ knifed ”in America—once by
his manager ; then we heard that he was a hopeless case
of consumption and he wouldn’t live a month ; next came
the news that Hall had picked up wonderfully and was
getting right again. Three months ago American news
told that the same man fell in the street the victim of
severe internal hemorrhage, and it would be a miracle if
he survived a week. Now ’tis told a recovery, good
enough to warrant the opinion that he will with care last
years longer, has taken place. Jim’s a veritable human
cat, apparently.
* • * •
Sydney mannikin pug “ Tibby ” Watson (7.10),
after a satisfactory trial at the swell National Sporting
Club, London, was matched with admitted good ’un
Fitzpatrick, who is taller, much heavier, and in
prime trim. Watson got in some forceful work on
the body, and spite of the re-opening of an old cut
over the right eye, got the money at end of sixth round.
London Sportsman cracks him up considerably and fairly,
but as “Tibby” proved no wonder here, the material
“ down under” is apparently very decadent.
* » * •
Chances of Armidale (N.S.W.) bruiser M‘Coll
fighting ’Murkan Scanlan are improving. Mac wants
at least a £5O side bet and the venue at Newcastle, and,
as Scanlan agrees, matters may be clinched any time.
Mick Dunn, his coach, reports Scanlan improved out
of knowledge, but, all the same, M’Coll is sure to
start first choice, as New England will stump out to a
man on his chance, Winner of this may meet Australian
premier heavy pug, Bill Doherty, or claim the belt if he
won’t come.
» * * •
Yic. pro. peds. kicked against the management
of Melbourne Eight-hour Sports enforcing betting sup-
pression measures, t’other day, and struck in a body,
spite of sec. de Gruehy’s pleadings. Some spoke plainly,
and said, as prize-money wouldn’t go round in paying
proportions, Mo.’s presence was necessary to prevent loss
to them. One entrant coolly stated that he had no hope
of winning a race, but the day’s running was always as
good as a tenner to him. Strike ended second day by the
committee refusing to budge and the starters starting
and betting on the quiet. This action is a severe
facer for professional running, which is much more
vigorous in Victoria than in any other State. There’s a
pedestrian council there and greater and more frequent
opportunities for raking in coin at the game are provided.
The cinder path man will probably move to O’Sullivan’s
State now and join the unemployed, as his occupation is
almost completely gone ; there’s no encouragement at all
for him in any other section of the Commonwealth, thanks
to his dark ways and mean tricks.

The parts of the cycle most needing proper atten-
tion are the tyres, upon which nearly all of the driving
strain falls. Cyclists can learn everything re cycle tyres
from the Dunlop company’s fine booklet, “ All About
Dunlop Tyres,” which they offer to post gratis to any
cyclist who applies for a copy.*
* » • *
Riding tyres in a slack condition is very con-
ducive to sideslip. A tyre that can be blown “ board-
hard ” without any risk, and still be resilient, is the Dunlop.
Now that the wet season will soon be with us, cyclists
should note that sideslipping on wet and greasy roads is
more likely to happen when the tyres are ridden soft.”
The glorification by B. R. Wise at a
Sydney banquet of blood-drunk Seddon, because
he is blood-drunk, shows a lamentable falling-off
from the Wise who, a decade ago, referred to the
Soudan contingent as a “lamentable eccentricity”
and on the subject of Imperial Federation wrote:
Union with England we gladly believe will come ; but
it must be the union of an equal with an equal, and not
the union of a superior with a subordinate : so that it is
no paradox to say that the first step towards union must
be Separation.
S.M, Herald, April 25, ventured, in a
leader, on a philosophical disquisiton as to the
“Increasing tendency on the part of members (of
Parliament) to sink their individual responsibility,” and
the “ decay of that governing faculty or instinct which it
has taken years of free institutions to cultivate.”
Thus it delivered itself, bound hand and foot,
into the hands of W. A. Holman, M.P., who,
wielding a trenchant pen, pointed out to the
Herald how he refused to “sink his individual
responsibility,” and didn’t allow his “governing
faculty or instinct, cultivated by free institutions,”
to decay, but on the question of the S’African
war ventured to differ from Mr. Chamberlain and
agree with Mr. Gladstone, John Ruskin, Herbert
Spencer, and other Englishmen of that stamp;
and the sort of treatment he got from the Jingo
daily press in Australia in consequence. Mar-
vellous to relate, the Herald published the
letter.
Melbourne Age, in its leading article of
9th April, booms up the Tory legend that
Australia is a country that is governed on a
hideously expensive plan (i-e., that the local Fat-
man is oppressed with heavy taxation which
should be cut down) after this fashion:
In a similar manner the extraordinary rate of public
expenditure in Australia is often attempted to be
explained away, and to some extent improperly In the
following figures railway expenditure has been eliminated
so as to make the comparisons fair:— ’
Expenditure
Per Capita.
United Kingdom .. _ .. £2 19 i
United States '. 1 16 4
Chief Continental Countries .. .. 2 3 1
Australia . 6 6 7
There is a pretence of fairness about this which is
calculated to impose on the multitude, but it is
really a shameful fake in the interests of the
growing Tory conspiracy, and some features of
the fake are these:
(1) Only half the Australian railway expenditure has
been eliminated. The expense of running the railways is
certainly deducted, but the interest on their cost isn’t.
That knocks about £1 6s. a year off the Australian figure
and reduces it to £5.
(2) No mention is made of the fact that the Australian
expenditure covers the whole cost of police, charities
and education, which are not included in the British
figures, these items being mostly charged to local rates
in the United Kingdom.
(3) No mention is made of the fact that the Australian
figures cover old age pensions in two States (there are no
old age pensions in the U.K.), and much road and bridge
expenditure (paid by local taxation in the U.K.)
(4) The United States figures are faked by taking only
the Federal expenditure, and leaving out the almost
equally large State expenditure. Also no mention is
made of the fact that there is no interest on railway debt,
and no expenditure on telegraphs in the U.S. budget!
railways and telegraphs being private monopolies.
(5) In the figures for Continental Europe the writer
apparently takes the German Imperial expenditure only,
and leaves out the expenditure of the States. In like
manner if the Australian Federal expenditure only were
taken Australia would appear as almost the most cheaply-
governed country in the world.
In short, the whole affair is a conspiracy on the
part of a very lightly-taxed propertied class to
make out a case for a further reduction of its
burdens. And its ways of making out a case are
remarkable, to say the least.
Premier Barton has suspended the opera-
tion of the law against the importation of nigger
labor for three months so far as the pearl-shelling
industry is concerned, in order that he may think
over the matter. Will Mr. Barton explain
exactly what thinking over it requires ? It is
known to every person of reasonable intelligence
that the pearl-shelling industry was worked by
white men till about a dozen years ago. There-
fore it can be worked by white men. The fact is
as well-known now as it will be three months
hence. The cheap-nigger bosses will be no more
willing to give up their black labor three months
hence than they are now. A White Australia
will be as essential three months hence as it is
now. The only thing gained is that a weak, futile,
half-hearted Premier gets three months rest from
trying to make up his mind.

Hospitals and Doctors Fail—Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills Permanently Cure. His
Kidney Aches, Gravel and Stone.

1901. The W. H. Comstock Co., Ltd., 58 Pitt-
street, Sydney. Dear Sirs. For nearly twenty
years I have been a sufferer from gravel and
stone, and was compelled to give up my position
in Sydney. I took a position as boundary rider
on the ‘ Clifton Grove ’ Station, in Orange, hoping
to receive some benefit, but later was compelled
to give that up also. I have been in several
hospitals in Sydney and elsewhere, without
getting any relief. No one knows the intense
agony I have suffered, and I was becoming
despondent after being treated by doctor after
doctor without getting any benefit. Seeing an

increasing in flesh daily. I cannot speak too

as I have done to give them a trial. I should
be pleased to answer any who care to com-
municate with me regarding my case. You are
at liberty to make use of this as you think fit.—
Yours very truly, J. W. Richards.” Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills are a perfect blood purifier, and
a positive cure for biliousness, indigestion, con-
stipation, headaches, sallow complexion, liver
and kidney troubles, piles, pimples, boils and
blotches, and for female ailments. Sold by

Hannan’s Oroya, still feebly fumbling for the
rich Iron Duke chute, must hit upon it some day.
Proper course would have been to have followed it from
the Iron Duke, but probably too great risk of gold-
robbery. Hannan’s Star mill been running on Oroya
stone for past three weeks, but no clean up to take place
till end of April. Plant treats 1800 tons monthly, so
should do well on for 3000 tons by then. Treatment-cost
said to be 465. a ton —quite a god-send for Star but the
devil for Oroya. Algernon Moreing, in advocating
amalgamation of three shows, stated that Brown Hill had
both oxidised and sulphide plants, and Oroya had not,
forgetting that Oroya has three Huntingdon mills in
about as dilapidated a condition as the Brown Hill coffee-
mills. In these Oapt. Parkes treated over 50,000 tons of
oxidised ore for an Bdwt. average at a gross cost per ton,
including mining and everything, of 16s. 8d. —a feat
Algernon is incapable of performing for various reasons.
• • * *
Latest published assays from Sons of Gwalia
(W.A.) give No. 6 level lode as 7ft., worth 26dwt. No. 7
level, sft., worth 24dwt. No. 8 level, lode 9ft., worth
24dwt. This kind of stuff has been published all along,
but the battery talks, and takes all its time to get 12dwt.
No shareholder ever seems to ask the directors what
becomes of the presumably lost balance of gold.
* * * *
March clean-up for Floater, Phillips River
(W.A.) announced as 300 oz. from 200 tons. Alleged that
concentrates, tailings and slimes contain by assay another
loz. 15dwt. per ton treated. If true, treatment must be
extremely poor, or ore most refractory. This field is
practically a new district added to the gold-producers of
W.A.
• * * *
Peak Hill Goldfields (W.A.), about which pro-
bably more lies have been circulated than about any other
show on earth, lately issued 11,000 reserves shares at £2
apiece, bringing issued capital up to £280,000 in £1 shares.
Ex-Gov. Jerry Smith is now hobnobbing on the board
with Darlington Simpson, who oiled everybody, from
the festive policeman to the highest Gov. officials, to get
the flotation through. 00. owns a once very rich but now
practically worked-out alluvial patch, and will one day
disappear like the Golden Cement, another meteoric
feline.
• • • *
First clean-up of Kanowna Champion Lode
proved the dismal failure here predicted, and all sorts of
excuses are being bandied about as to cause. Keal reason
not far to seek.
* * * *
Tarcoola is shaping well for permanency, which
will be a novelty for S.A. —all previously-worked gold
shows there having been mere surface fiz-gigs.
« • » •
“A Loser”: The shares in the S.A. copper
mine named are not worth holding. The Bendigo mine
has a good record. It pays dividends occasionally. Bulk
of the shares held by Fortunatus Lansell and Dr. Atkinson,
who know a good thing and how to stick to it.
* * * •
Said to be upwards of 80 silver sections around
Zeehan (Tas.) held idle by reason of the vicious Flyspeck
mining regulations. Tributors would jump at the
chance of working many of these shows, but there seems
little desire on the part of leaseholders to have any work
done “just at present.” Miners bitterly declare that it
is useless putting in applications for forfeiture, as the
regulations and an easy-going Minister of Mines block
them every time. If forfeiture is applied for on the ground
of non-compliance with labor conditions, it appears that
the lessee is given three months to mend his ways. If he
fails to comply he may be fined £5. For a second offence
he gets another three months’ notice, and may be fined
£lO. Six months’ time is thus gained at a maximum cost
of £l5. On a third application for forfeiture being made
a further term of three months is given the leaseholder
to comply with the labor conditions 1 If he still fails, the
Minister may recommend forfeiture, or may impose a fine
of £25, or may suspend the labor conditions for any
term he thinks proper. Further, he may declare any
application for forfeiture frivolous, and impound the
applicant’s fee ; and if the average Tas. Minister doesn’t,
in addition, have the applicant prosecuted for disturbing
the blessed vested interests, it is only because he
doesn’tdesiretodrawtoo much attention to the workingof
his dreadful department and the rottenness of the law
that allegedly controls its proceedings. For enabling big
syndicates to starve working miners into accepting
almost any terms, Tas. mining laws and their adminis-
tration are evidently the best ever devised.
• * *
A seeming root of Mt. Bischoff (Tas.) has been
discovered through the apathy of a cow, belonging to a
farmer named Mayne, whose selection lies in the vicinity
of the boss tin mine. Mrs. Mayne picked up a stone to
throw at the dreamy milker, and the missile was so heavy
that she took it to her husband. He, finding it to be
pure tin oxide, started digging, and came to a 10ft. face
of t ; n ore s am pi es taken from the face yielded results
which give farmer Mayne the appearance of a mine-
owner. The luck of it ! Only a furlong away from the
spot where the stone was picked up the Orient co. of yore
was ted £30,000 in searching for black tin oxide, whilst
the grey oxide, such as Mrs. Mayne chucked at the cow,
was thought to be worthless stuff.
* * * *
A correspondent:—“Mount Garnet railway
f rom Lappa Lappa (Chillagoe railway) will be open for
traffic in a day or two. The Garnet ore is a very basic
sulphide needing much highly silicious flux. This being
the case, general-manager Ashcroft has formulated a
tariff of returning charges, which is fairly acceptable to
the directors of Tartana, Ltd. Mt. Garne't can take 400
tons of silicious ore per week, and the Tartana people are
arranging to convey that quantity from Tartana Hill to
Mungana railway station (Chillagoe terminus)—ls to 16
miles road-carriage. Thence transport is easy : Rail 47
miles by Chillagoe railway to Lappa Lappa junction and
34 miles by Mt. Garnet railway. Probably the Bourke
Carrying Co., Abdul Wade, and the camels will convey 200
tons per week, and the local teamsters the balance, over the
road-portion of the journey. The consignments of ore will
be graded to average 15 per cent, copper. Estimated that
an average ofl 60 cubic ft. broken from the quarry face
(50ft. in height) will yield one ton of the necessary-grade
ore. The balance of the ore will be kept for leaching,
Ragging-up ore to such a high grade is not to be the
permanent policy-only until the leaching plant is ready,
In Brisbane the fact has been established with a working
model plant that 28 per cent, carbonates of copper (15 per
cent, metal) can be leached successfully successful!j
because the extraction equals 96 to 97 per cent. The
whole proposition is thus reduced to the problem of cost
of sulphuric acid at Tartana Hill. Mt. Morgan produces
chamber acid at 30s. per ton, but the road (15 miles)
and rail (156 miles) carriage of sulphur from Cairns to the
mine will bring the cost up to £3. Of course the manu-
facture of sulphuric acid (four tons from one ton of
gulphur) is pal | and parcel o v f a leach i„g plant. At the
outset sulphur will be imported, but directly the sulphide
zone of the mine is opened, the sulphide ore will be roasted
f or production of acid as at the Arizona and other
American mines. Working on the general average value of
Tartana Hill ore—7 to 8 per cent, copper, 66 per cent, silica,
4 per cent, iron—ls tons will produce one ton of metallic
copper. This will need one ton of sulphuric acid to
convert the carbonates into sulphates, which are soluble,
and can be washed out, and less than one ton of pig
j ron (£5) f or the precipitation vats. Allowing for all
sorts of contingencies, £1 should be ample to treat each
ton of crude ore. Then come the realization charges,
and the less said about those the better. Anyhow,
it seems that nothing can stop the Tartana (North
Chillagoe) group from being vigorously worked at last,
One of Tartana directors goes forthwith to America for
trained men in hydro-metallurgy of copper.”

Obit. : Joseph G. White, of Warrnambool

George Meudell, the Melbourne broker
who is whooping up on his own account The
Bulletin’s financial gospel, wrote a cheeky letter
to Premier Jenkins, of S.A., the other day,
telling him: “Directly you announce your new
S.A. loan in London I warn you I shall stop it.”
In answer, Jenkins tells the story of the Cana-
dian-Indian who set out to stop an express
train, and lassoed it, whereby it came to pass that
a small piece of brown meat was afterwards
noticed trailing behind the engine. Very soon,
though, S.A. may have to sit down seriously and
decide whether its loan is the locomotive or the
Indian. _ Jenkins Iscariot takes it for granted that
his loan is the locomotive, but mistakes often arise
in such matters.
Premier Seddon’s ruffianly speech (as
reported) advocating that 5000 Maoris be sent
to S’Africa with full permission to conduct
their share of the war on principles of pure
barbarism. and with no regard whatever to
civilised ideas, and the greedy cadging of his
creatures for subscriptions to the rich man’s
cash. testimonial, have made many of his old
admirers feel very sick. The remorseless docking
of Is. each from a multitude of old age pensioners
towards Seddon’s purse of sovereigns is about the
lowest-down proceeding Maoriland remembers.
The testimonial is booming right enough, for the
old age pensioners’ subscriptions and the contri-
butions of public servants (who are afraid lest they
get the sack if they don’t subscribe) will save it
from failure if nothing else does, but a lot of public
contempt will go with the money. And if the
German papers take to publishing Seddon’s
alleged speeches as a fair sample of British
humanity it will probably get in the way of Dick’s
dukedom.

Or its tones can be modulated to the soft, sweet tones of the German Zither.
In addition to its wonderful tone quality, the Harp-Zither has a great many
advantages over all other Zithers. It is the only Zither which may be played
while holding vertically like the Harp, or it may be laid upon a table, as is
necessary with the ordinary Zither.
Observe the diagonally crossed strings, almost the same as in a piano, the
melody strings passing over the chord strings. By means of this improvement
in construction the similarity in tone and volume of the piano is produced.
Beautiful in Design, Grand, Resonant Tone, Perfection in Every Point,
and it is the easiest to learn to play of any musical instrument in existence.
A child can play it almost at sight. The reason anyone can play this instru-
ment on fi -st trial, even though the person may know absolutely nothing
about music, or may not have an ear for music, is this : Each string is num-
bered, as is each note in the music, so all one has to do to render the most
difficult selections is to strike the strings as indicated by the numbers; hence
we guarantee that anyone able to read figures can learn to play.
The Harp-Zither is built on the lines of the large harp which sells at
£2O and upwards, and to the astonishment of all the Harp-Zither has the
tone of the two ; in fact, its tone is similar to that of a piano.

A case somewhat akin to that) of the two
Australian officers recently executed by order of
Kitchener, occurred at the siege of Delhi in the
Indian Mutiny. A captain took two princes
prisoners, and had marched them some distance,
intending to hand them over to the authorities,
when he grew tired and shot them both dead in
cold blood. Almost all the prisoners were
similarly disposed of, but owing to the rank of
these two the commander spoke rather crossly to
the officious captain.
Gov.-Gen. Hopetoun’s recent laudation of
the Cairns winter climate is the biggest puff that
district has had. In the course of time, Southerners
will probably realise that the greater part of North
Queensland, in place of being a bed of fever and
malaria, is really one of the healthiest countries on
earth. Very hot, it is true, in summer, and sultry
on the coast, but eminently salubrious, all the
same.

_ The eminent Hygienic Scientist, Dr. GOODFELLOW, Ph.D., F.R.M.S., in a recent lecture to Hygiene students,
said: “We have seen from previous work that many hair lotions contain lead and other injurious constituents, but
that ‘ Koko,’ the type we have taken as the subject of our examination to-night, is not only a valuable tonic dressing
for the Hair, but is perfectly free from, all deleterious constituents.
“A perfect hair-dressing should fulfil, among others, the following essential conditions (1) It should be free
from all poisonous and deleterious substances. (2) Pleasant in odor or odorless. (3) It should not affect the natural
color of the hair. (4) It should give tone to the scalp and hair, assisting to restore the hair follicles to their normal
conditions, and promoting the natural nourishment of the hair roots. I can give no better example than * Koko.'
which w© have proved to fulfil these essential conditions.”

THE EIGHTEENTH REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS OF THE COLONIAL BANK OF AUSTRALASIA LIMITED.
To be presented to the Shareholders at the EIGHTEENTH ORDINARY GENERAL MEETING, to be held at
the Bank, 126 Elizabeth-street, at NOON on TUESDAY, 29th APRIL, 1902.
REJPORT.
THE DIRECTORS beg to submit to the Shareholders their Eighteenth Report, with a Balance-sheet and State-
ment of Profit and Loss, for the half-year ended 31st March, 1902, duly audited.
After providing for expenses of Management, Interest accrued on Deposits, Rebate on Bills
Current, Tax on Note Circulation, and making provision for Bad and Doubtful Debts, the net
profits amount to £17,172 13 2
Brought forward from 30th September, 1901.. .. .. .. .. .. ~ .. .. 1,531 210
£18,703 16 0
Which the Directors propose to apportion as follows, viz.
Dividend at the rate of 5 per cent, per annum on preference shares £7,601 2 0
Dividend at the rate of 5 per cent, per annum on the ordinary shares 3,341 1 1
To reserve fund .. .. .. .. .. .. .. .. .. 5,000 0 0
Balance carried forward .. M «. «. .. 2,761 12 11
£18,703 16 0
The last two instalments of the Deferred Deposit Receipts, due in 1905-6, were paid on 30th December last, and
the bank has now paid all the liabilities of the old bank, which removes the restriction to the payment of dividends
on the ordinary shares.
The Directors, in view of the increase in the bank’s profits, feel justified in recommending that in addition to the
dividend on the Preference Shares, a dividend be paid on the Ordinary Shares at the rate of 5 per cent, per annum.
In co lsequence of the payment of the balance of the Deferred Deposit Receipts, the seats of Sir Rupert T. H.
Clarke, Baronet, Mr. Thomas Russell, and Mr. James Moloney, who represented the Deferred Deposit Receipt
holders on the directorate, became vacant; and the board, under the powers conferred upon it by the Articles of
Association, filled up the vacancies by the appointment of Messrs. Thomas Russell, James Moloney, and John Todd ;
who, with the other Directors, now represent the Shareholders.
The Dividend will be payable at the Head Office on and after the 30th inst., and at the branches on receipt of advice.
The Eighteenth Ordinary General Meeting of Shareholders will be held at the Head Office of the company, 126
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, on Tuesday, the 29th day of April, 1902, at noon.
By order of the Board.
Melbourne, 17th April, 1902. SELBY PAXTON, General Manager.

The little Isle of Man, half-way between
England and Ireland, has just shown its inde-
pendent frame of mind. It has its own Parlia-
ment, and is only loosely kept in hand by an
English Governor (fancy sending out a Governor
to a place right in the middle of the. U.K. !)
Lately its legislators resolved to come into line
with the British law of married women’s property,
as passed 20 years ago ; but they also absolutely
declined to do away with the old-fashioned
widow’s right of dower. After which, to show
that they were not really behind the times, they
penalised the selling of cigarettes to boys, and at
a stroke abolished the crying evil of brewers’ tied
houses, which other legislatures have not yet had
the courage to tackle.
An English lady has a letter from Barnum
Powell “ apologising for writing to her in pencil
and with his left hand, on the ground that his
Tight hand and the pen and ink were engaged in
writing dispatches ! ” Can’t Harry Rickards or
Fitzgeralds’ circus get this gentlemen for an Aus-
tralian season ?
Inspector Cameron, who has become
Superintendent of Police, has received more testi-
monials in the way of watches, chains and lockets
than, perhaps, any other member of the N.S.W.
Force. He holds a unique gift from the
pastoralists of Queensland and N.S.W. combined
for his border work at Moree. He chased the
Breelong blacks, cornered Glasson, and pursued
sheep-stealers uncountable.

The Wrong Mr. Wright, if he does nothing
else, gives a very effective reply to Juliet’s naive
inquiry, “ What’s in a name ? ” In the right
name or the right brand there is everything. The
right brand of Whisky is D.C.L. every time.
D.C.L. is the delectable fluid which assists the
business man to profitable cash conclusions, the
author, actor, orator and artist to brilliant in-
spiration, the philanthropist in good doing ; adds
to everyone’s happiness and is everywhere known
as The Whisky of the Wise.*
New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency Co.,
Ltd. Wheat report: Though the market has lost its
excited state lately referred to, values continue on the
firm basis then noted, sales have been limited locally in
consequence of the small quantity offering-, but in the
country districts considerable clearances have been made
in many instances for the purpose of feeding starving
stock on a basis equal to 4s. 3d. to 4s. 4d. per bushel at
the seaboard.*
In these swift-moving days nothing goes ahead
like goaheadedness, and when you see a business man
adopting enterprising and novel methods for attracting
oublic attention, it is safe to predict a success for him.
Mr. Meyers, of the London Tailoring 00., Bourke-st.,
Melbourne, who has elbowed his business right into the
front rank and a little ahead of it, is now about to break
out in a new place, viz., the premises at the junction of
Bourke and Elizabeth-sts., known to everyone who knows
Melbourne as Che “Bee-Hive Corner.” And when Mr.
M. has converted the said premises into a palatial store,
Melbourne will have such an artistic display of artistically
made raiment as the city never saw before.*

There is absolutely no question about the curative
ability of this combined and perfected method. It
has stood the test of time; it has been through the fire
of critical cases; it has come out victorious in every
curable instance. It has saved the child to a life of
independence; it has given the youth his natural
endowment of health and strength to fight the battle
of life successfully; it has conquered the blight upon
old age and turned years of suffering into ending
days of joy and giadness. Whoever is Ruptured, or
knows a person suffering with Rupture, should keep
in mind the fact that in eighty per cent, of all cases
there is a daily liability that before night they may
say good-bye to earthly things. Does not this fact
influence one to pause and consider whether they
are using their best efforts in the way a wise Creator
intended they should? And if the forethought, the
research, and the painstaking effort of,one who
invents and studies for the relief of others is placed
in our hands, is it not our duty to read and listen
patiently,that we may take advantage of that which
contributes to our earthly benefit?

The “Allenburys” Foods provide nourishment suited to the growing digestive powers
of the Infant, and constitute an altogether distinct advance on the mischievous expedient of trying
to make the child’s stomach accomodate Itself to a food essentially unsuited to its digestive powers.
Farinaceous foods of all kinds are totally unfit for infants under five or six months of age, such foods
being a potent cause of digestive troubles, rickets, and many disorders arising from mal-nutrition.
The “ Allenburys” Foods are especially valuable in hot
climates. They are in the form of powder and keep perfectly.
