At Poverty Point.
Melba, it is said, has ceased to interest herself in Regina
Nagel, and the “ career ” of that disappointing contralto
hangs fire. Miss Nagel must be nearly into her “thirties”
by this time. Her voice is one of the best without a
doubt, and well-trained, but something keeps her back.
Seemingly the “ Ben Bolt ” songstress lacks patience as
well as confidence.
When Wilson Barrett arrived in Melbourne last year he
got a mayoral reception at the Town Hall. When he
departed, from Melbourne for S’Africa last week he got
only a brief paragraph in the local press mentioning that
his Australian experiences have “inspired him to con-
struct” a novel and a dramatisation thereof, entitled
“ The Never-Never Land.” Barrett is a mechanist with
a good memory for points. The term “Never-Never
Land ” cropped up frequently in Haddon Chambers’s first
play—“ Captain Swift ’’—and it will serve as a reminiscent
advt., so to speak, for W.B.’s dual “construction.” He
threatens to produce the drama in Capetown.
“ S. Snell ” : If Geil, who is the star of the new U.S.
evangelical variety company, has not served his time as a
low comedian in the New York halls, he was born with a
decided list to the theatres, and drifted into a church by
mistake. He fetches the crowd for holiness and the plate
exactly as the Musical Burglar fetches ’em for fleshly
pleasure and the pay-box, and is the better comedian of
the two. His face-play is studied and elaborate, his
trickery of voice, action and expression absolutely
artificial. There is even a suspicion of make-up. Geil’s
face is the comedian’s unmistakable chiv, and he has the
comedian’s woeful lack of creative power as a humorist.
His stories are hash-hashed, old and stale, owing every-
thing to the telling. These Yank devil-chasers are having
a bigger success than the rival ’Murkan show, “The ,
Wrong Mr. Wright." If the Musical Burglar told ti.<- j
story of Jonah as comedian Geil does, he would be hiss. d
off as a blasphemer; but it is impossible to be irreverent
in a pulpit.
Amy Castles is fast degenerating in Australia into a
sectarian question, and the wearing of her portrait
threatens to become tantamount to singing “The
Wearin’ of the Green” on 12th July. The day after one
big concert in Melbourne, a girl “ of the wrong color”
strolled into the fashionable drapery establishment where
she is employed, and threw down the gage by referring to
“ last night’s failure.” Instantly half-a-dozen dark-eyed
colleens came from their respective counters to demand
an explanation. Then a few yellow supporters rallied,
and winged words flew around ; finally, one debater
clinched an argument by dragging down her opponent’s
hair. Then there was a wild whirl of skirts and stockings
as a dozen excited damsels clawed and banged one
another’s pretty faces in support of their respective
faiths, while sleek shopwalkers tried to tear them apart.
The riot was finally subdued, and the combatants distri-
buted to different parts of the shop, where they continued
to enrage the cat opposite by unrolling orange or green
fabrics on the counters upon the slightest provocation.
Melbourne Johnnies who remembered the lovely legs of
Palotta in the first “Gaiety Girl” production seven
years back, crowded to see the fair Austrian’s symmetrical
supports in the last act of “ Dorothy,” where the two
girls come out to the duel disguised as men. But dis-
appointment met them. The Palotta of seven years back
was not the one who presides over sassiety functions to-
day and is photo’d with the Naicest in the weekly crawl-
papers. While shapely Florence Young wore her long
coat quite open, Palotta’s was carefully buttoned over ;
her long-looked-for legs remaining hidden throughout
the act. Sassiety could not be expected to endure the
sight of the legs of one of its pets in anything less than
Rosalind, though some years back the smart daughter of
a Melb. University Prof, came out boldly to charm her
male acquaintances as principal boy of an amateur bur-
lesque show.
“F.R.”: Sydney Palace’s “Merry World” gives the
theatre-goer a good opportunity to contrast American
and English soubrette methods. Rose Hamilton is a
fairly representative sample of a successful English
comic-opera singer. She has a generous figure, is a fair
dancer, good singer—with a very clever method of putting
“ expression ” into her songs—and a not bad actress when
she does act, but that is not all the time she’s on the
stage. Rose Sutherland represents American methods :
small piquant figure; splendid dancer, especially in
eccentric lines; a “funny” singer, though her
voice has been rasped terribly by shouting coon
songs and Bowery sketches ; a most vivacious
actress who plays up to the audience every second
of the time —smiling, grimacing, posturing, laugh-
ing—and gives herself never a moment’s rest. The
American system of amusement runs to painstaking
detail. Every little incident is worked out and' every
second on the stage utilised for something. The British
system is one of haughty indifference to details. The
artiste does what she is billed to do, looks well, sings
well, supplies the number of smiles provided for in the
contract, and seems not to worry in the least about
extracting all the gold from a part. But it is no good
cyaniding the tailings after an American has been at
work.
The Christian Scientists seem to have captured a real
prize in Barthold, the German tenor, who appeared with
Musgrove’s co., last year. Barthold, handsome, grey and
spiritual-looking, should be a fine advt. for any craze he
may adopt. He is probably the brainiest student on the
operatic stage, for, although his Christian Science
enthusiasm suggests that he is a Wagnerian mystic, Bar-
thold has the reputation of being able to sing—on
demand—the tenor music of about 90 operas other than
the tumultuous works of the great Richard.
“ Dorothy,” at Williamson’s Melb. theatre, came like
mellow wine to the public, who had lately been imbibing
musical comedy soda-water after their doses of Italian
opera brandy. A delightfully tuneful thing, with enough
of Lauri Lurcher and massive Priscilla in it to please the
Philistines. Yet not at all a captivating show. The two
cousins—Dorothy and Lydia—couldn’t persuade anyone
that they were a pair of saucy young spinsters from
Chanticleer Hall masquerading as the daughters of a
rural pub.-keeper. Florence Young is too large and hard
for the part of dainty Dorothy. Grace Palotta’s coquet-
tish manner is town-bred and well-matured. But both
actresses appeared to advantage in their “ boy ” dis-
guises.
The Cornstalk law made a vigorous attempt
last week at Grafton to hang a man for the alleged
murder of a person whose alleged dead body can’t
be found, but failed to secure a conviction. _ A
Chinaman had gone away for a day or two leaving
his mate in charge of the house and vegetable
garden, and their hired Cingalese working cheer-
fully under his direction. He came back to find
no mate, no Cingalese, and no money, but sundry
bloodstains. The Cingalese was arrested with
money, believed to be that of the allegedly-dead
Chow, upon him, while either flying from justice
or else travelling like any other ordinary inhabi-
tant. The most energetic search failed to reveal
a scrap or vestige of the vanished Chow. The
Crown held that if a man could escape punishment
simply because he took reasonable pains to conceal
the body no one was safe. The defence held that
if, because a Chinaman repented of his sins after a
severe attaok of bleeding at the. nose, tried to
make reparation for his past avarice by giving a
few pounds to an honest black toiler, and then
withdrew himself from society to commune
with Joss, that black man could be arrested
while on his way home to his own
country and suspended by a rope, then
liberty had perished out of the land. The absence
of the body was an insuperable obstacle, and the
jury returned a verdict of not guilty of murder.
But the Cingalese man gets seven years for steal-
ing the Chinaman’s money, and as the law can no
more prove theft without the evidence of the live
Chinaman than it can prove murder without the
evidence of the dead one, it is a foolish and
tyrannous way to strike an average.

There have of late been very bad re-
ports as to the health of Queen Wifhelmina
of Holland, and if she dies the great but
old and worn-out family of Orange becomes
extinct. Then William of Germany will probably
reach out for Holland and its big colonial empire,
on the ground that it is an old-time province of
the German empire and lapses back to the empire
for want of direct heirs. And Britain, which has
taken quite a lot of Dutch territory, will be
shocked, and France will want Belgium (which
was once French territory) as compensation, and
Austria will remark that it ought to have Servia
or Macedonia to make things square, and Russia
will ask for a bit of China, and Italy will fix its
eye on Tripoli, and the fat will be in the fire all
round. And then, as likely as not, nothing will
happen.

At Birmingham (England) 40,000 signa-
tures to a petition in favor of increased Customs
taxation were recently obtained in a week. In
the United Kingdom over £80,000,000 a year is
now raised by direct taxation, local and national,
in order to avoid taxing the goods which the
American sends into the country to undermine
local industries, and at last England is growing
tired.
Of the making of books there is no end, and the
same might be said of the information .contained in
“ Pugh’s* Queensland Almanac and Directory for 1902.”
The almanac is a red-covered volume of 992 pp., and
there is a legend that once a man read it right
through. Having done so, he bailed up three
Queenslanders and talked over Queensland affairs,'
statistics, the high water at the principal ports, reviewed
the past year, made sympathetic remarks on the careers
of the mighty dead who had snuffed out since 1900, told
about the general elections of 1896 and 1899, and analysed
the results thereof, and reeled off “ Queensland Men of
the Time,” with full particulars of everybody in Banana-
land who has any particulars about him at all ; also
alluded casually to postal arrangements, Masonic lodges,
banking, stamp duties, the distances of the country,
particulars of the tradesmen at the smaller towns, and
all about the shire councils and divisional boards. Two
of the Bananalanders were insensible by this time, but
the oldest one asked feebly—“ Say, boss; when did yer
travel those parts ? ” “ Never, thank Heaven 1 ” the man
replied, “ but I’ve just finished reading Pugh’s almanac.”

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Te Hoheha Mata” writes: “Here is some-
thing approaching to absolute financial genius. In a town
down this way (South Queensland) a resident recently
decided to raffle bis old horse in a ridiculous number of
tickets at a respectable amount each. At first fixed date
the raffle was postponed owing to inadequate patronage.
Second attempt the same thing happened and the event
was again postponed, though under violent protest.
About this time the old horse died, broken up with dis-
appointment at not being raffled, and the proprietor
hired a passing ox-persuader to make the necessary
remarks.- Just here, however, a wise and up-to-date
friend intervened. The money wasn’t returned, but the
friend wrote to every member of the raffle stating that
the drawing had taken place under his supervision, and
that the person written to was the winner. By next post
he wrote to them all again, stating that ‘ your horse ’ had
unfortunately fallen down dead, and requesting ss.
for burial expenses. And if it hadn’t been that 82 ticket-
holders compared notes in the matter and came
round to make inquiries, the scheme might have been a
very brilliant success.”
* • • •
A bank clerk in a Queensland mining town
made good perks out of a 141 b. brass weight and a file.
When Saltbush Bill brought along his gold to the bank,
out of the little lot the clerk annexed, say, 3dwt., and to
make up weight threw in brass filings. When he had
got a few ounces together he would make an entry as
having bought so many ounces from some imaginary
person and pocket the cash. When a big parcel from
that town reached the mint the brass filings were thrown
out. The blame at first went to the Chinamen from the
Nor’-West until close investigation revealed the trick.
» * • *
Litigation was never at so low an ebb in Mel-
bourne as at present. The litigious spirit seems to have
forsakeri the people, and the injured man prefers turning
his other cheek. In a recent week the law courts were
almost utterly deserted—civil, criminal, insolvency,
divorce, banco, county, and practice. The excessive
costliness of legal process has, of late years, convinced the
average man that it is better to bear the ills he has than
to fly to the lawyers. The lawyers have not quite killed
the goose that laid the golden eggs, but they have at
least taught it not to be such a confounded goose. And
the trend of things in Sydney is similar. When a big
firm like (say) Dalgety’s has a dispute with (say) the
Colonial Sugar Co. they don’t go to law—they simply
appoint a third party to adjust the trouble amicably.
« * « *
The “Chancery estates” game is played
assiduously in Australia, and the bait of “ count-
less millions” lying idle dangled before the foolish
for the sake of fees. A recent British Treasury return
shows that there is just £1,050,000 in Chancery, in 3200
-separate accounts, of which 19-20ths are under £lOOO in
value. Proof of claim, the Court of Chancery explains,
would in many cases probably more than absorb the
amount available. A knowledge of these facts should
save many people from wasting money foolishly.
# * * *
“E.C.” —Have just annoyed an alleged “Re-
coverer of Unclaimed Moneys, Missing Friends, etc.”
The gentleman collects and remits prizes in Tatt’s sweeps
aiid as I lately had the luck to strike a £2 cash prize I
consulted him. As said sweep only half filled and as
Tatt. takes a tenth it followed that I had but 18s. to
receive. I cautiously enquired his charge, and, on being
told the amount due, he replied “Well, I’ll only charge
you 7s. 6d.” That was all 1 I flatter myself I surprised
that man.
• * * *
A result of Victorian Parliament’s abolition of
the Coupon system is a theatre-ticket scheme. Several
Melbourne suburban shops now give a voucher with
each 6d. purchase, 500 of which entitle holder to
two dress circle or eight gallery seats in the theatre, or
a trip down the Bay. The new Act is said to be not
comprehensive enough to catch these fish. But, as there
is no middleman living on the game this time, it doesn’t
matter much.

Brother of S. African warrior: “’Ow’s Bill?
Oh, Bill’s orlright. Just got a letter from ’im; says
yestidy was pay-day. He got £l2O that night, and he
made a nice bit going over in the boat. Er course, I
knowed Bill’d be orlright on the boat, becos he ’ad three
packs of his own.”
• * * *
Copy of a prospector’s report received by mem-
bers of his syndicate at Matlock (Victoria) :—“ Revisited
Clear Creek and more convinced than ever of its being a
duffer. Resolved to try Snob’s Creek ; three days think-
ing' of it, one day inspecting, two days deciding, two
days shifting, two days in camp, one day prospecting
large dyke outcrop, good prospects at times, six days on
duffer, no more good prospects—think of shifting again ;
two days writing letters re back pay, one day resolving
to shift to Bear’s Creek. Hear of reef buried there 20
years back showing gold in stone. Two days shifting
camp (would have been longer had funds come to hand),
two days inspecting Bear’s Creek, six days looking for
buried reef. No success ; think should shift camp again,
thinking of shifting towards Noo Gee. Will put in week
till funds arrive.”

Life Insurance Independent (Chicago, U.S.)
on the cheap assessment form of life assurance—a delu-
sion with which Australia is now making acquaintance :
You choose an assessment company :
Because it is cheap,
And “ just as good as any while it lasts.”
It saves you 15dols. yearly,
Which is 300dols. in 20 years.
After awhile,
When you are old,
And poor,
And uninsurable,
And have paid assessments for 20 years,
The old thing busts.
How much have you saved,
And where is it ?
• • • •
There was a sustained howl in Melbourne from
the shop-keepers over Solicitor-General Wynne’s action in
remitting fines imposed upon street fruit-hawkers in local
courts. Now the police decline to take action, and the
Dagoes have pretty well their own way. The fact is most
Melbourne people depend upon these hawkers for their
fruit-supply, and the abolition of the barrow-man would
enormously reduce the present consumption of apples,
peaches, pears and grapes, and give the grower another
hard knock. Good fruit that is 4d. or 6d. per lb. in the
shops is ljd. or 2d. on the barrows. The difference means
rent mainly. The barrow-man is a nuisance in one way,
but he is extremely useful, and as the landlord is a more
intolerable nuisance, public sympathy is all with the
hawker. Hundreds of tons of good fruit are wasted
every season in Victorian orchards owing to the extor-
tions of middlemen and landlords. A relative of the
writer’s sent several cases of apples to Melb. la3t season,
and received in return two 2d. stamps. He sent the
stamps back to the writer, who bought with them lib. of
the same apples from the retailer (apples in Melbourne
are sold by the pound instead of by the dozen as in
Sydney).
J J * * * *
“E.W.A.” gives some of his experiences with
an inventor, and wonders if he was the “Byronite”
gentleman who proposed to make a tremendous explosive
out of beetroot“ At first he had a flying-machine, pro-
bably the same that he offered to Gen. French for
£10,000,000. His idea was delightfully simple, and cheap
at the price. The power of the earth to attract bodies to
itself ceased at a distance of 45 miles from its surface;
therefore, you had only to rise into space for that distance,
wait for the earth to turn round, and then descend
by gravitation. He had got it all worked out, and merely
wanted a motor designed that would carry him up the
required distance. (In engineering, you know, the master-
mind leaves the working-out of details to others.) He
had gone into calculations, and reckoned that he could
deliver the mails to England in 37 hours, and had got a
contract signed by Dan O’Connor (then P.M.G.), by which
he was to receive some enormous subsidy when he accom-
plished this. I asked how he intended to drop
down by gravitation if he got beyond its scope.
He soon fixed up that little difficulty. All you had to do
was to take a strong magnet covered with a brass sheath,
and when you wanted to descend take the sheath off,
when the iron in the earth would attract the magnet and
incidentally the air-ship, to itself. Yet another invention
was an automobile to travel a mile and a quarter and 44
yards in a minute. He was nothing if not precise in his
calculations. He had a workshop rigged up and spent
months making the engine. The idea was simply the old
one of tremendously high gearing, a rock upon which
many an invention has split, and of course was a dead
failure. I remember the trial well, and the look on the
face of the man who financed it, when the machine
refused to budge. Another invention was a ship to
travel 70 miles an hour and only draw two inches of water,
and a perpetual-motion engine to be worked by water
from a tank above, which water the engine pumped back.”

According to an English paper (only it doesn’t
put it that way), London has made such extraordinary
progress under Freetrade, that it now does much
fine laundry work for Holland, France and Belgium, while
Germany shows an increasing tendency to have its shirt
washed in London. Britannia may in future be repre-
sented as an old lady at the washtub, and the country
which used to advance cash to almost every other place
on earth, depend for existence on taking in international
washing.

Notice in the window of a land-agent in an up-
country M.L. town :—“For sale, house and section for
£240. House insured for £250.” Pleasant outlook for the
insurance co. Yet there are plenty of such cases.
• • * *
The agent curse ! A friend of writer’s casually
mentioned that he was about to purchase a steam engine.
Since then he has not rested. Agents, with the “ best
make of engine on earth,” swarmed like bees into his
private residence at all hours, and hummed like the same
insect. He was waylaid in the streets by seedy-looking
persons, who wanted a word for a moment about engines.
Went to the country, and all varieties of “ direct importers
of steam engines” hunted him around in all manner of
vehicles. He is now on a trip to the Islands, to escape
the persecution. Does all Australia live on the commis-
sion racket ?
* * « *
“Pukaki”: The Maoriland dairying industry
is one of the brightest examples of the benepts of co-
operation. When the factory system was inaugurated 10
years back, all the factories were proprietary concerns,
and the farmers didn’t get a big price for their milk.
But factory after factory was bought out by the milk-
suppliers, and run on co-operative lines, with the best
possible results for the farmers. They now get well-paid
for their milk; and, pigheaded and cantankerous as
cockatoos generally are, they seem to be able to run a
factory in comparative harmony.

rers. A business man (in a large way chemically),
estimates that more than half the smaller try will “ fiz
out altogether, and says his own firm is looking forward
to a record bad-debt year,

The struggle for a crust! A variant of an old
dodge was exposed, in Melb. City Court, lately. A young
man went round selling “ Honolulu Lily ” seeds, guaran-
teed to sprout in a glass of water, with a handful of salt
therein, and to exhale a delicious odor, also to keep a
house free from flies. The seeds were those of common
buckwheat.

Re civil servants and money-lending : The worst
shark money-lenders are Govt, servants, who generally
lend money to under-men on terms that might bring a
blush of shame to the face of a Rand syndicate.. Suppos-
ing the lender lets out 10s., he gets an 100 from his
victim for £1 due at the Ist of the month, but if the bor-
rower fails to pay at the time stated, he has to pay 30s.
the following month. One genius who lends small sums
to very junior clerks is reputed to make the borrower
always forge his father’s name to the I O U. He is always ,
paid.

A new Australian industry is the export of
kosher meat for the Jewish population in S’Africa.
. * *
A thriving little local industry was started in a
Q. town the other day without any tariff stimulus. The
citizens were energetically canvassed on behalf of a new
patent American varnish, which was the best thing in the '
world for buggies, bicycles, oil-paintings, grates, and :
many other things, and they bought freely at a good
price. It was found subsequently that the tins were
made locally and filled with a mixture of turps, oils, and ,
vinegar ; but by that time the industry had moved on to '
another town.
• * » *
The latest M.L. scheme is an endowment fire-
policy. Under this, a man who had kept up his fire
insurances for 20 years would receive enough to enable
him to rebuild or renew the insured premises. Claimed
that this would greatly check arson.
• * * ♦
“H.P.”: Finding that my grocer was still
charging the old N.S.W. duty-paid rates on tea, I asked
him had he not made a mistake. “Oh ! no,” he replied,
“ the duty did not go on." He thought I meant that
he was charging too little ! Evidently, had the Federal
Govt, passed the old Vic. State tea-duty (3d. a lb.) he
would have raised his tea accordingly. Disgusted with
such dodgery, I interviewed another grocer. He, too,
quoted the old prices, but when I pointed out that the
duty was now removed, he admitted that he kept his
price tip “unless the customer spoke about the abolition
of the duty.” Then he reduced it. Moral: read the
newspapers, advertisements included.
Dear Bulletin. —The reference to “ the
reunion of the Ballarat and Bendigo printers with
their metropolitan conferes ” in Bulletin of the
19th April was somewhat “ off its feet ” as regards
the facts. The “exes” were not, as unkindly
alleged, “ provided by means of a circular inviting
subscriptions,” but by the entertaining comps,
themselves, each of whom planked down at least
“ half-a-sov.,” like a “toff.” Truly, some of the
prizes for races were gifts, but in this respect even
the “bakers, butchers, and grocers” enjoy a
“shout.” Now, who’s the angry Scotchman? —
J. W. Tucker, Carlton, Melb., 21/4/’O2.
More evidence from an Auckland (M.L.)
paper of the ignorance of the Boers, who, it is
suggested, should be given no quarter, and
butchered like wild beasts by savages :
Trooper Alfred B. Morton, of the Sixth Contingent,
writes to his parents :—“Rolfontein, February 9, 1902.
We are still here, near Spitz Kop, awaiting to attack
Botha, who is a few miles distant, so we hear. This
morning I called at a farm-house and saw the ruins of a
splendid observatory, the telescope of which must have
cost nearly £lOOO. It was mounted on a huge iron
pedestal 13ft. high, and telescope barrel was lift, long,
and 9in. diameter— i.e., one length of it. The house had
a circular revolving roof. Everything was smashed as
much as possible. I could not find the lens.”
The Lower House of Par ia ient in Den-
mark has one representative to every 16,000
people, in Norway one to every 19,500, Switzer-
land one to 20,000, Sweden one to 22,000,
Canada one to 22,688, Hungary one to 38,000,
Belgium one to 42,000, Spain and Austria one to
42,000, Australia one to 50,000, Britain one to
57,000. As regards their State Parliaments, even
N.S.W., with 125 M’s.L.A. (one to every 11,000
people), hasn’t such a pressure as Prince Edward
Island (one to 4000), or New Brunswick (one to
8000). Certainly some of the Australian State
Assemblies could bear cutting down, but it is
far more important to get rid of the foolish
and useless Leg. Councils where the dead men lie
—and how some of them do lie /
The latest American horror from the
Philippines is to the effect that Uncle Sam’s
soldiers have dropped into the habit of torturing
the natives by pumping water slowly on their
heads —a process which even the Inquisition
never improved on much—to extract confessions.
Doubtless they obtained a lot of confessions —999
people out of 1000 would tell everything they
knew and anything they could invent as well
before they had been pumped on many minutes.
It would be interesting to know if the Southern
States practice of burning colored men alive with
kerosene has also been carried to the islands. By
the way, the name of that pleasing person,
General Jacob Smith, turns up in connection with
this latest atrocity also. Jacob appears to be an
interesting remanet from the dark ages.

M. J. RYAN, J.P.,

The Jingo Press in Prayer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ War is the game of the newspapers.”
—’Murkan aphorism.
“The Public, Lord, are weary now of scandal
and divorce ;
They tire of retail murder, and of swindles on the
Bourse ;
So now, we pray Thee, hearken how they clamor
wild for Gore,
And vouchsafe Thy faithful Pressmen just a
passing breeze of war.
“ Our circulation’s falling—yea, Thou knowest it
is so ;
The ads. are waning daily, and our profits dwindle
low ;
But the public purse will open, and the public
purse will pour
In a rain of endless coppers if the papers give it
gore !
“The scent of battle-powder—O, the People love
it well;
They love to read of slaughter and the shriek of
shot and shell;
The Brute is strong within them, and the Brute
will always pay
If it sniff the bloody banquet where the wolves of
battle prey.
“So send the sound of bugles and the peal of
battle-drum,
Let thousand clarions clamour and let Mercy’s
voice be dumb;
What matters woman’s weeping when her home-
less children die,
So Thy loving Journals flourish and Thy loving
Journals lie.
“Thou knowest, Lord and Master, in Thy realm
of Land and Sea,
What a lever for Thy potency Thy loving Pressmen
be ;
Thou knowest, Lord, to prosper, we must go the
ways of Guile—
For the breath within our nostrils is the lucre of
the Vile;
“And the Yile are always with us—and they
curse the Good and Pure,
And Thy Press must curse in chorus if Thy Press
would fain endure,
And the Vile assault us daily with a lustful lion’s
roar,
‘Give us blood to stay our craving—Give us
Rapine—Give us War ! ’
“ The Days of Peace fatigue us with their endless
tales of Lust,
Their Greed that rules the Market Place and
grinds the Poor to Dust;
These fatted Peaceful Robbers —we are weary of
them too,
For a change give Open Rapine with her thrice-
accursed crew.”
So they prayed a rain of blesssing, and the rain it
came in flood —
For the gentle Journals’ healing came a pouring
rain of blood,
And the Lucre that was coveted —it came without
surcease,
And on weary Press and Pressmen fell the dews
' of Fatted Peace.
PHILLANDER FLAM.
Our New Selection.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SADDLETOP was going ahead. A new
church and an old public-house went up—
the public-house that used to be at the Gap.
A camp of men came along with a tent and some
tools, and dug a Government dam; some more
cleared the lanes of timber and trees—felled them
and chopped them up and left them stacked in
heaps that blocked the road and frightened horses
and made them bolt.
A lot of new selectors came and brought large
families with them, and murmured like the
Israelites because the school was six miles from
them.
Dad became their Moses. He couldn’t see that
they had anything to grumble about, but Dad
always listened to people who had a grievance.
He went to work and agitated earnestly for anew
school at our end of the district. Dad worked
night and day to get them that school, and when
at last it was granted and the building went up,
they murmured more because it was erected
within a few feet of Dad’s land.
* * * *
One day a young man, overwhelmed with a
collar—a lean stripling of a man, with no more
hip than a goanna, a clean face, a “ haw ” in his
voice, a cane in his hand, and a gorgeous band on
his straw hat—mounted the verandah and an-
nounced himself to Dad as the teacher to the new
school.
Dad scarcely heard him. He was confused.
He stared and couldn’t think of anything to say.
Had the angel Gabriel, or the hangman,
suddenly appeared before him, Dad’s equa-
nimity couldn’t have been more disturbed.
Dad was never himself in the presence of leading
people; and the prig-pedagogue and the sage
were one and the same to him.
The teacher bowed and said he believed Dad
was Mr. Rudd. His own name was Wood-Smith
—Mr. Philip Wood-Smith—and he handed Dad a
card, and, sitting in an easy-ehair, began to talk
of schools and curriculum in an earnest and
learned manner. He believed in teaching a boy
mathematics and mentioned Napoleon Bonaparte
and others whom Dad hadn’t heard of; but con-
demned classics and the dead languages.
“What is the use of them,” he said; “what
earthly use is Greek to you now on this farm, Mr.
Rudd ? ” (Dad looked along the verandah-boards).
“Can you say you have ever found your Latin or
your ethical problems in Shakspeare of any use to
you since you left school?”
Dad, in tones of uneasiness, said he hadn’t.
“ And yet ■”
Mother found her way to the verandah, and
Dad told the teacher she was Mrs. Rudd. They
shook hands, and when Mr. Wood-Smith looked
round to address Dad again he was gone.
The new teacher was a polite man, and enjoyed
society. Never before was there anyone like him
at Saddletop. Whenever he met Miss Wilkins or
Grey’s daughters or Sarah he would smile and
take off his hat and strike his knees with it. And
it didn’t matter how far off they were—whether
on a verandah a mile away or on horseback or
carrying in sticks—he smiled and took his hat off
just the same.
• * • *
Dave regarded Mr. Wood-Smith with disfavor.
The polish of him and his attention to girls
annoyed Dave.
“ He’s a goat! no matter how much he knows,”
Dave said in the kitchen one day.
Sarah stood up for the ways of the pedagogue.
She thought it proper such respect should be
shown her sex.
“Then you’re mighty fond o’ bein noticed,”
Dave answered, cynically.
Young Tom was sitting at the table, having
late dinner, alone. He joined in.
“ Not when they’re m-milkiri, Dave, an’ haven’t
their stockin’s on ” (Dave looked at him and
grinned), “or’na tub o’ water.”
Tom struggled on the floor from a poke mother
gave him with the tea-pot.”
“But it wouldn’t hurt you,” Sarah went on,
quietly, “ to lift your hat.”
“No, it wouldn’t ,” Dave snarled; “an’ it
wouldn’t hurt anyone if I didn’t, an’ who wants
t’ wear a hat out swingin’ it about as if lie wanted
t’block a cow?” Arid Dave chuckled trium-
phantly, and went out.
Mr. Wood-Smith was a frequent visitor at our
place, and if he chanced to remain for a meal any-
time, Dad would become agitated. He would lose
his head, and at the table make all kinds of
mistakes. When he didn’t pass meat to someone
who didn’t want any, he dropped a plate and spilt
gravy about or mutilated his fingers with the
carver.
But Dad usually contrived to avoid Wood-
Smith’s society. Dad had never received a great
education himself, and the presence of so much
learning annoyed him. But always when the
teacher had left Dad talked favorably of him.
Once mother asked how much salary he thought
Mr. Wood-Smith received, and Dad reckoned he
would get at least a thousand.
* * • *
Politeness was the broad plank in Wood-Smith’s
curriculum, and he hammered it hard into his
pupils.
One day Dad was riding on the road and met
the scholars returning home. Several raised their
hats to him. Dad stared and went on. Some
more hats. Dad scowled. Then Tom limped
along, swinging a lizard by the tail.
“ Hello, Dad”—and up went his hat.
Dad turned the mare sharply and went after
him.
“Yer young devil!” —he shouted, striking at
Tom round the base of a tree with a riding-switch
—“ would y’ make sport o’ me, too ?”
“ ’E tol’ us t’ —” Tom whined.
“’Oo?”
“S-S -Smith:’
“ T’ mek fun o’ me ? ”
“ No—t’ ”
Dad attempted to dismount and Tom dropped
the lizard and escaped.
* • * •
Boxing Night. A party at our place—Sarah’s
party. Such a gathering 1 Every soul on Saddle-
top must have been present —everyone except the
Careys. And the display of lights and lanterns
would blind you. The verandahs right round
were hung thick with them. Two accordeon-
players and a violinist were in attendance, and to
hear the music they made when you reached the
gate would make your heart jump.
Sarah flew about everywhere; met her female
friends at the steps and hugged them, and escorted
them in and took their hats and things and found
sleeping-places for the babies.
Joe looked after the men for her—warned them
of the dog and the barb-wire; showed them where
to put their horses, and conducted them to the
ballroom and introduced them to any young ladies
they didn’t know.
Dancing about to commence :
Mr. Wood-Smith arrived. He came late, and
McGregor, a very old mate of Dad's, strolled in
about the same time. Dad hurried forward and
seized McGregor by the hand and welcomed him
boisterously. Dad hadn’t seen McGregor for a
number of years and the pair sat together at a
table and talked of old times.
They talked for hours.
Intermission. The room containing Dad and
McGregor became crowded, and cake and coffee
were being handed round.
A lull in the clatter of tongues. McGregor
turned to Mr. Wood-Smith, who sat near him
sipping coffee, and in a loud sonorous tone said :
“’N’ hoo’s th’ auld mon—quite weel, Phil?”
Dad stared and nudged McGregor. He thought
his old mate was making a mistake.
But Wood-Smith understood. He blushed and
fidgetted, then forced a smile and answered:
“ He’s ah—pretty well, indeed.”
“ An’ auld Mick ?”
Mr. Wood-Smith fidgetted more. He wished
someone would come to the door and call him.
The company appeared interested.
“Pretty well, I think,” he said, eyeing the
door.
McGregor turned to Dad.
“Y’ken,” McGregor said, “auld Micky, wvn
cleanit the dustboxes i’ Dreeyton—uncle to tlm
mon ? ” And he pointed his thumb at the teacher.
Dad drew himself up like a cockatoo, roused.
“ Not his uncle ! ” he explained, his eyes opening
like a door.
“Yes, mon”—and McGregor laughed at Dad’s
astonished look—“ Yes, Phil’s a son o’ auld Jem
Smith, o’ Quartpot, is he nae ? An’ wus nae auld
Micky a brither o’ Jem’s ? ”
The discovery was too much for Dad. He stood
up and stared at Wood-Smith.
“ Dammit! ” he said, “ I know y’.”
“Y’ couldna f’get auld Jemmy?” McGregor
said.
“Remember him well,” Dad answered, his eyes
shining with enthusiasm. And turning to the
company, who, to the discomfort of the teacher,
were all grinning, said, with a ring of pride in
his voice: “ His father an’me knew one another
thirty years ago.”
‘ ‘ Eh! an’ th’ auld woman tae —, ” said McGregor.
“ An’ his mother ,” Dad added.
“An’ y’ min’ th’ bunnicks she used tae male
us a’ ? ”
Dad burst into a loud laugh at mention of the
“bunnicks.” But Wood-Smith didn’t. He only
smiled, but his face was very red.
“An’min’, too, th’auld black hen she used to
lia’ settin’ wi’ eggs in th’ bed ? ”
Dad held his sides, and the tears ran down
his furrowed cheeks.
“ An’ th’ p-p-p-” (McGregor couldn’t speak for
laughing) “th’ pig i’ th’ hoose wi’ ” (choked again)
“ wi’ rihbon tied tae it.”
Dad gave a tremendous roar ; the whole house
exploded, and it was minutes before Joe’s voice
could be heard yelling—
“S’lect your partners for a waltz.”
